Some more specific thanks are also due here: first to the Arts and Humanities Research Board (now Council), whose grant enabled both the payment of Will Broadhead, Federico Santangelo, Margaret Watmough, and Gabriel Bodard and the visits to museums that underly the Corpus; to University College London, which I am proud to have served for the second half of my career, which allowed me to hold the grant at the Institute of Classical Studies, with immediate access to the library without which the work would not have been possible; to the Institute, to its Directors, to Richard Simpson, who has overseen the publication, and above all to its librarians, Colin Annis and his colleagues. We here publish, not pictures of all the inscriptions in the Corpus, but of at any rate a very large selection; and we hope to place a larger set of pictures on the web. Some of the inscriptions are pigs to photograph, and partly for this reason, partly because we have not in the end managed to see all the texts, we have sometimes scanned a published photograph, of which details will be found in the Bibliography for the entry. We are aware that not all the photographs are of the quality or size we would have wished; we hope that they are all serviceable. And although we are aware that drawings contain a larger element of interpretation than photographs, photographs of squeezes or squeezes, we have sometimes used them, either because of the unphotographability of the text or because a drawing is the only visual record that survives. In the cases of Falerio 1, Buxentum 3, Blanda 1, Laos 3, Laos 4, we have adapted existing drawings to reflect our readings of the text: where texts survive we have of course checked drawings against them. We draw attention to the fact that we have used the convention 000 bis in two different ways: in the case of Pompei 108 bis and 126 bis, to list two texts as perhaps mis-read examples of Pompei 108 and 126 respectively; in the case of Cumae 4 bis and Pompei 96 bis to insert a new text at the last moment. One of the losses of the last century that should not be forgotten is the disappearance, despite our enquiries, of the squeezes that were certainly owned by von Planta, Conway, Buck, Ribezzo, Vetter, and Bottiglioni; in the case of Bücheler, nothing survives in Bonn, but since squeezes of Mommsen, which went via Henzen to Bücheler (Bullettino dell' Instituto 1876, p. 207), and were lent to Zvetaieff, in the end returned to Berlin, it may be that other squeezes of Bücheler also went there. And what has become of the four pieces owned by Domenico Venuti: 'Quattro tazze ordinarie (from Saticula and Telesia). Tutte con iscrizioni Etnische o antico italico'?1 It is appropriate in any case to salute the CIL archive in Berlin, which has kept its squeezes of Osean material safe through two world wars, and to thank Dr Manfred Schmidt, who enormously facilitated our work by lending them to us. We also thank the Ecole des Hautes Etudes, IVe Section, and Professor Laurent Dubois, for lending us the photographs collected by the late Michel Lejeune. Since we do publish a very large number of pictures, and devote much attention to letter forms in the Epigraphical Account for each entry, we deliberately do not publish Tables of Alphabets, which would inevitably be a pale simplification of the material in the pictures. When we began the project, we thought to provide a section of maps; but the publication of The Barrington Atlas has transformed the cartography of the ancient world, and we think users of these volumes will be much better served by having that work at their sides, along, e.g., with the road maps of Italy of the Touring Club Italiano.2 We have, on the other hand, provided concordances that include inscriptions figuring in earlier corpora, but excluded by us, along with a brief account of the reasons for their exclusion; having done this, we understand why no- one else has tried: we have aimed at, but certainly in this context failed to achieve consistency. 1 G. Marchini, Antiquari e collezioni archeologiche dell'Ottocento veronese (Verona 1972) pp. 242-67, for the inventories of his collection = Cortona, MS 551, at p. 265. 2 We have seen, while going to press, A. La Regina, in L. Franchi dell'Orto (ed.), Pinna Vestinorum e il popolo dei Vestini (Rome 2011) pp. 230-73, 'Il Guerriero di Capestrano e le iscrizioni paleosabelliche', which transcribes the 'South Picene' letters &lt; inconsistently and □, we think, impossibly: we stand, without exception, by our texts and (very much less adventurous) translations. xi</page><page sequence="9">xii IMAGINES ITALICHE Much of the material in the Introduction has been repeatedly discussed between members of the team, but also with colleagues in all our institutions. It has been particularly helpful that I have been able to present parts of it in the course of teaching at the Università di Trento, under the auspices of Professor Elvira Migliano, in the autumn of 2007, and at the Università Federico II, Naples, under the auspices of Professor Dina Storchi, in the spring of 2009. The whole of the Introduction has been read by Peter Wiseman, whose unrestrained criticism, testimony to a friendship of nearly half-a-century, has improved it out of all recognition. We emphasise at the outset that, for each text, it is essential to read the epigraphical account, text, and apparatus criticus together, as well as the relevant part of the Introduction; diplomatic transcripts cannot be given for obvious reasons of space, and it is our epigraphic conventions and apparatus criticus that explain the relationship of our text to the stone, bronze, etc. (Some material of general import is also to be found in the Introductions to each of the four Indexes.) At the same time, the material we present is of enormous richness, and we hope that this book will help to put it back into the mainstream of historical work on ancient Italy: we feel no. less privileged by the contact this material has given us with its peoples than we do by the warmth with which our present-day Italian colleagues have helped us. Nearly finally, there is one battle that I have lost, perhaps unsurprisingly, namely with the integrity of Ulrike Roth over her appearance on the title-page; but her unerring capacity for spotting an Osean inscription below layers of dust, the sharpness of her eye in reading worn or damaged letters, her knack for finding at the very last moment a few more inscriptions tucked away under a shelf, her insistence on taking the very best possible photograph - we all hate to think of the number of delayed or missed lunches - mean that there is almost no page of this book that is not in her debt. It has been a long journey from the gleam in the eye that represented the beginning of the project to now, and we could never even have hoped to complete it without the openness and generosity of some friends and colleagues in particular, who have accompanied us for all or much of that journey: Nino Ampolo and Cecilia Parra, Rosalba Antonini, Gianfranco De Benedittis, Marco Buonocore, Lavinia De Rosa, Carlo Franciosi, Giorgia Gargano, Maurizio Gualtieri and Helena Fracchia, Ezio Mattiocco, John North, Paolo Poccetti, Fabrice Poli, Giovanna Rocca, Alessandra Villone. We have exchanged information and ideas, and met in a strange variety of places, from seaside resorts out of season to the cafeteria at Roma Termini: we hope we have served the Republic of Letters well. Michael Crawford</page><page sequence="10">ACKNOWLEDGMENTS Note that the place to which individuals are assigned is that where help was given and not necessarily that where they now work. Cavedoni (Leipzig 1850) The text of the Descriptio was printed in 1812, but not published; Carelli' s collection was available to F. M. Avellino, who on its basis in 1814 composed a Supplementum to his own Italiae veteris numismata I (Naples 1808), II (Naples 181 1); the Descriptio was in general circulation by 1833, whence the Adnotationes of Avellino (1834, preface in fact 1835); plates + text were published in 1850, but were available to G. Fiorelli before 1845; the volume incorporates the 1812 text and Avellino, Adnotationes. Zvetaieff, Sylloge inscriptionum Oscarum ad archetyporum et librorum fidem: pars prior, textum interpretationem glossarium continens, pars posterior, tabulas continens (St. Petersburg 1878, reprinted Hildesheim 1971)</page><page sequence="18">INTRODUCTION The inscribed texts of the peoples of peninsular Italy conquered by the Romans provide almost the totality of our knowledge of what they had to say about themselves. It is a field that has been transformed out of all recognition by excavation and exploration since the early nineteenth century; and it remains one in which new discoveries abound. When Lepsius wrote in 1841, he included nothing from Capua; when Mommsen published Oskische Studien in 1845, he knew of no Osean inscriptions in the Greek alphabet;1 and when we began this project, we thought we would be dealing with some 800 entries, whereas in fact we here present about 1000, some multiple, including the coinage of the Social War. We of course expect new discoveries to change the picture presented here: there is an obvious cor- relation between archaeological excavation, for instance at Capua, Pompei, Campochiaro, Pietrabbondante, or Rossano di Vaglio, and the volume and variety of material now available; and the results of modern excavations at Campochiaro, Pietrabbondante and Rossano di Vaglio, all sanctuaries, are particularly striking. But, despite the local particularities revealed by the material, to some of which we draw attention below, we are on the whole struck by the broadly similar patterns of language use, including graffiti, over the whole area with which we are concerned, despite the disparity between the volume of material from Pompei at one extreme and sites such as Tuder or Caulonia at the other. In particular, we think that the literary culture attested as existing among the Paeligni, both by three long inscriptions (Corfinium 6, 11, Sulmo 13) and by the use of Greek cognomina (Corfinium 14, 19), existed also among the neighbouring Marrucini (Teate Marrucinorum 6, clearly despite its lacunose state a literary composition) and among Oscan-speakers: we infer this from the existence of a number of inscriptions of some complexity (Teanum Sidicinum 17, Teruentum 8-10, Aufldena 1 and 3, Anxia 1). The Iguvine Tables (see below) are not imaginable without the background of a literary culture; we draw attention also (apart from Bantia 1) to half-a dozen certain or probable legislative texts (Tadinum 1, Incerulae 3, Teate Marrucinorum 2, Velitrae 1, Abella 1, Histonium 2, Buxentum 1); and we signal the recent appearance of the future imperative in a legislative text of the archaic period (see below, p. 13). The evidence from both Capua and Cumae, as well as from Laos, shows curse tablets as coming from what were clearly elite tombs. They too attest to a literary culture; and 4 out of 14 relate to legal procedure. Of the peoples of ancient Italy, in addition to the Latins and the Greeks, we exclude the Etruscans,2 whose language is not Indo-European, the Messapians, whose language may be akin to Illyrian,3 and the Faliscans, whose language is close to Latin.4 We include the small number of inscriptions of the Oscan- speakers who established themselves at Messana in the early third century BC, while excluding the other languages of Sicily that have been taken with varying degrees of plausibility to be akin to those of peninsular Italy.5 We exclude the Iguvine Tables, whose text is not in doubt, where there is no 1 See M. H. Crawford, in H.-M. von Kaenel et al. (edd.), Geldgeschichte vs. Numismatik (Berlin 2004) pp. 131-39, 'From Lanzi to Mommsen: the coinages of the peoples of Italy'; Mommsen himself reviewed his work in Bullettino dell 'Instituto 1846, pp. 45-48. 2 See H. Rix, Etruskische Texte I-II (Tübingen 1991). 3 See C. de Simone &amp; S. Marchesini, Monumenta linguae Messapicae I-II (Wiesbaden 2002). 4 See G. C. L. M. Bakkum, The Latin Dialect of the Ager Faliscus (Amsterdam 2009). 5 We understand that the second of L. Agostiniani's two volumes is imminent. 1</page><page sequence="19">2 IMAGINES ITALIŒ archaeological context, and where Vol. I of a new edition by A. L. Prosdocimi appeared in 1984: Vols. II-III have still not appeared.6 The languages which we include form a clear family, along with Latin and Faliscan, even though the relationships between them are not without problems.7 Briefly, we are dealing first with three northern languages attested already in the archaic period: Umbrian, that continues in use down to the Social War, Sabine, for which there are no attestations later than the archaic period, and the archaic language written in the 'South Picene' alphabet (for details of this and other terms of art, and for problems of dating, see below, p. 8). On one view, the last two are the same language. A relatively straightforward language is then that used by the peoples of Campania, and by the Samnites and Frentani, and by the Lucani, Brettii and Mamertini, known collectively to modern scholars as Osean, and remarkably homogeneous, even if written in two different alphabets (see pp. 16-17, and the Introduction to the Index of Words); traces of related languages are to be found in Campania and Lucania in the archaic period, the languages again continue in use down to, perhaps after, the Social War. Finally, in between these two groups lay the so-called central Italian languages, of the Vestini, Marrucini, Paeligni, Marsi, Hernici. The evidence now suggests that the Volsci did not use a language similar to these, but both the Osean language and the Osean alphabet. It is not clear to whom the long text hitherto attributed to the Volsci should be assigned (for details see below, p. 16). One archaic inscription from territory controlled by the Volsci at the time raises more problems than it solves (Satricum 1: see below, p. 10). The different alphabets used are: for a group of early inscriptions, mostly from Sabine or nearby territory, an alphabet that may for convenience be called Sabine; for a second group of early inscriptions, from territory that was later occupied by the Piceni, the Praetuttii, the Vestini, the Marrucini, the Paeligni, and the Frentani, an alphabet now usually called 'South Picene'; for the inscriptions of Umbria, an Umbrian alphabet, and then the Latin alphabet; for the central Italian peoples in the late Classical and Hellenistic periods, the Latin alphabet (see on Incerulae 1); for Campania, the so-called 'alphabet of Nuceria', the Etruscan, Greek, and Osean alphabets;8 for Samnium, including the Frentani, the Osean alphabet, and then in a handful of cases the Latin alphabet; for Lucania, Bruttium, and Messana, the Greek alphabet (see below, p. 17). At the end of the story, the languages of Italy were by the last quarter of the second century BC a threatened minority; and even those that flourished were in our view becoming dependent on Latin models: we find it impossible to read either the treaty between Abella and Nola (Abella 1) or the inscription from the so-called Samnite palaestra at Pompei (Pompei 24), without seeing in their style the influence of Latin legislative style - 'in that land, which land . . ., those men, all of which men . . . ' This style seems to have been invented by Gaius Gracchus; and its borrowing in Osean texts of the second century BC means that the Osean law from Bantia (Bantia 1), translated almost word-for-word from a mostly much earlier Latin legislative text, has at least partial precedents. Much of the story must be of day-to-day contacts between speakers of different languages. When Plator moved to Teanum Sidicinum (Teanum Sidicinum 25; see below, p. 4), his homeland still had another generation of independence before it, until the Roman conquest of the Sallentini and Messapi in 267 and 266 BC. But the Romans had already founded colonies of Latin status at Cales and Suessa, in 334 BC and 313 BC respectively, neither of them more than a few miles from Teanum; and it is quite hard to suppose that Plator managed to avoid contact with Latin-speakers. The next two centuries saw not only the creation of the Roman empire in Italy, but the associated emergence of Latin as the dominant language of the peninsula, something that is inevitably central to the story that our texts have to tell. It is currently fashionable in the linguistic history of the modern world to 6 For the early history of the study of the Tables, see M. H. Crawford, 'The study of inscriptions and the practice of religion: Jean Matal and the Iguvine Tables' (forthcoming). 7 The attempt by A. L. Prosdocimi, in Studi sull'Italia dei Sanniti (Rome 2000) pp. 208-13, 'Il sannita', to write the cultural history of the speakers of the various languages, is in our view very substantially premature. 8 Campania is too restrictively defined by L. del Tutto, Eutopia 4, 1 (1995) pp. 151-87, 'Forme della romanizzazione nell'epigrafia lucana', at p. 160, also dating the Tabula Bantina to the 'tardo I sec. a.c.' (!).</page><page sequence="20">INTRODUCTION 3 emphasise not the suppression of languages, but the freely made choice to change language for economic reasons;9 such a view downplays the fact that speakers of dominant languages never have to make that choice at all. One wonders what the people of Aquinum or Caiatia or Teanum thought when they decided to use Latin for their coin-legends (see below, p. 6). Teanum at least remained of allied status and largely Oscan-speaking down to the outbreak of the Italian war against Rome in 91 BC. The story of the inter-relationship of empire and language is neither simple nor linear,10 and we shall return to the case of Teanum later (see below, p. 30). We should also remember that the Roman was not the only empire in early Italy. The Etruscans spread their language to the Po valley in one direction and Campania in the other, in the context of what later sources saw as a kind of empire; and it may be that the inscriptions will reveal to us another empire in Italy of the Republican period that was not Roman (see below, p. 12). * * * Our aim is to offer both the primary evidence for the texts of the inscriptions, whether a manuscript drawing of an inscription that has disappeared or a photograph of one that still exists, together with an account of the archaeological context and of the nature of the monument or object on which the text is inscribed. We have been guided throughout by our belief, confirmed by our experience, that autopsy almost invariably makes a substantial difference. Even with a text as well-known and long-studied as the Agnone tablet, discoveries remain to be made (Teruentum 34). If we have more to say about evidence than about sociolinguistics, that is because the alleged insights of the latter, for instance, that different social groups use different varieties of language and that people regularly employ different registers of language in different situations, seem to us pretty banal." There are also reasons for being cautious about models generated by contemporary linguistic research: much of the linguistic observation that lies behind such models has been undertaken by people who take the normality of the nation state for granted; and much of the historical depth built into European models inevitably comes from a world in which a church with a claim to universality set much of the agenda. We are comforted in our empiricism by the decision of the author of the most important recent book on the subject, J. N. Adams, to define bilingualism simply as the active use of two languages. 12 We have always gone back to the original find-reports, and frequently discovered a quite terrifying loss of knowledge over the years, knowledge that is in fact essential to the understanding of the texts. For instance, in an otherwise ground-breaking article on the urban topography of Oscan-speaking Pompei, almost everything that Paolo Poccetti says about the inscriptions from the Porta di Nola and the so-called Samnite palaestra, and on the eituns inscriptions, is demonstrably wrong.13 Daniela Cammarota has recently published the archival evidence relating to the discovery of what are described as two Osean curse tablets in a tomb at Capua, the modern S. Maria Capua Vetere; but the drawings that accompany the published account in Ruggiero's Scavi di antichità of 1888 make it clear that the curse tablets are in Greek, not Osean; and 9 R. A. Houston, European Review 11,3 (July 2003) pp. 299-324, 'Lesser-used languages in historic Europe: models of change from the sixteenth to the nineteenth centuries'. 10 C. Letta, in H. Solin (ed.), Le epigrafi della valle di Cornino (Associazione Genesi 2005) pp. 47-62, 'I Marsi dal III sec. a.C. all'Alto Impero nella Collezione Graziani di Alvito (Fr)', assumes a single linear linguistic progression within a particular area. " E. Horodowich, Past and Present 181 (November 2003) pp. 3-33, 'Civic identity and the control of blasphemy in sixteenth-century Venice'; see the evidence-based account of D. Langslow, in J. N. And a brief notice in Fasti Archeologici 28-29 was picked up in the Rivista di Epigrafìa Italica for 1981, and taken as recording a funerary graffito from Iberia in the Latin alphabet and the Osean language relating to someone 200 months old; but even the photograph shows that what we have is simply the name ccrraAoc in the Greek alphabet (see Concordance II). We attach particular importance to indicating cases where the object + inscription may have migrated from elsewhere to its find-spot. 14 Italy in the period with which we are concerned was marked by a high degree of geographical mobility. In about 300 BC, a man called Plator, mentioned above, whose name identifies him as Messapian, moved to Teanum Sidicinum, where he continued to ply the trade he had learnt at home, that of a potter. At Teanum, he introduced the type of pottery characteristic of his homeland, with painted decoration on a black slip background, a fashion that was picked up at Teanum by another workshop, that of the Berii, whose products were widely diffused in Campania. Plator had learnt Greek, thus acquiring the second of the three hearts claimed a hundred years later by Ennius, who also came from Messapia (Gellius XVII, 17, 1). It may indeed be that knowledge of Greek was widespread in the world from which they came: numerous cities in Apulia, whether in Messapia in the south, Peucetia in the centre, or Daunia in the north, were in the same period producing coinage with legends in Greek, though it may be that the Greekness of the institution of coinage brought with it the Greekness of the language used to identify it (see below, p. 6). But at least one other Messapian potter seems to have learnt Greek: we have his funerary inscription in a stamp on a tile of 350-300 BC, perhaps made in his own workshop, SaÇiiaoc Kep / aiaeuc xaiPE&gt; fr°m Rione Molinello, south of Ginosa and south-east of Montescaglioso, between Metapontion and Taras.15 And the helmet of a Dasimos, whose father bore the Greek name Pyrrhos, was probably buried with him at Anzi, ancient Anxia {IG XIV, 655; see also LGPN, s.nn.). In Teanum, Plator signed one piece that he made in Greek, irXaTcop ETTOīrļce; but he also learnt at least some of the language we call Osean, the language of Teanum, and signed one piece in Osean, irXaTcop omye, using the standard Osean word for 'made', but using the Greek alphabet, not the Osean alphabet, and keeping the Greek termination -e, rather than the -ed or -et normal, though not universal, in Osean (Teanum Sidicinum 25). At the other end of our period, the convulsions of the Social War offer an explanation for a Venetic inscription from Colle Manicola, near L'Aquila, on a lead weight in the shape of a bell, perhaps to be attached to a cord in order to trip a horse and rider.16 We have on the other hand been very selective in the provision of bibliography, almost without exception excluding items that merely repeat earlier ones. The publication in 2000 of Untermann's Wörterbuch, in particular, has placed our understanding of our texts on an entirely new footing, and relegated much past speculation to oblivion. One of our interests is the geographical distribution of the scripts, the languages, and the onomastic and other systems of the peoples with whom we are concerned. We have therefore included texts which consist of only a single letter, provided it is identifiable as belonging to the script in question; such an inclusive approach has enabled us to provide at least some context for the otherwise small corpus of communities such as Aufidena. We have in any case always tried to see the inscriptions that we include in 14 The problem of 'oggetti itineranti' is more complex than appears from G. We of course include any texts where the language is in doubt and may belong to one of those that we do include; and we include inscriptions for which the text has never been known in modern times, which provides us, for instance, with good evidence for the boundary between Bouianum and Aesernia (Bouianum 117). But where so little remains that one cannot even be certain of the alphabet, or where a graffito is a cross, the inscription is excluded. We have also sometimes included texts that strictly speaking do not belong in this volume: to us it makes sense to present together all the material from a single votive deposit at Anagnia, or to include the single inscription each from Capua or Rossano di Vaglio with an Osean name in the Greek language. We also do not think that one can understand the linguistic usage of, for instance, Roccagloriosa, without being aware that, while a curse-tablet and a statute are in Osean, the kerykeion of the community is apparently labelled in Greek (Buxentum 2); and the presentation of coin legends in Osean, but not those of the same community in Greek or Latin, would make no sense at all. * * * One phenomenon that particularly invites attention is that of non-Latin inscriptions from colonies of Latin status; and one sometimes discovers either that they are not non-Latin or that they are not from the colony. An example of the latter phenomenon is furnished by the colony of Aesernia, founded in 263 BC, to which all the handbooks attribute a gold ring with an Osean inscription, of the third century BC (see Bouianum or Saepinum 1). This is again a case of a loss of vital knowledge after the first publication, an article by Heinrich Dressel in the Deutsche Literaturzeitung of 1 882, where one discovers that the ring did not come from Isernia, but was merely sold in Rome in that year by a peasant from the neighbourhood of Isernia; and that it was found with an assemblage of Greek silver coins. The inference is then obvious, that the assemblage is part of a coin hoard closing around 260-250 BC, which is conventionally called the Benevento hoard, but was in fact merely seen in Naples in 1884 and described as having come from somewhere west of Benevento. Both hoard and ring presumably come from Saepinum or Bouianum, and tell us quite a lot both about the elite of the Pentri in the immediate aftermath of the Samnite wars and about the military context of the penetration of silver coinage into Appennine Italy, in the course of the First and Second Punic Wars.17 As for the former phenomenon, Giovanna Rocca lists two possible Umbrian inscriptions from the Latin colony of Spoletium, founded in 241 BC, both originally published by Giuseppe Sordini.18 Of the two, one was already read as Latin by Bormann, in full knowledge of Sordini's publication, and Sordini's squeeze, unearthed by Federico Santangelo in the Archivio di Stato, shows that Bormann was right. The other has disappeared, and no squeeze survives; but Sordini's drawing does, together with a description that makes it clear that the text comprised one surviving line, running along the length of a small column, with tops and/or bottoms of letters lost when part of a block was cut and rounded to make it into the column. The text was, we think, in Latin. Similarly, Poccetti 136, from Cales, is clearly in Latin: Mn. S(?).19 17 See Crawford, CMRR (1985) pp. 36-8; F. Catalli, in G. de Benedittis (ed.), Sulle colonie fondate durante la seconda guerra sannitica (Campobasso 2004) pp. 21-42, 'L'economia del Sannio attraverso le monete', at pp. 33-34, fails to distinguish an argument over the dating of Roman coinage from an argument over the dating of the circulation of a coinage. 18 See G. On the other hand, Greek inscriptions that contain Osean names are for the most part of the same period as Osean inscriptions, and we include them in an Appendix; and we include in the corpus itself the tiny number of inscriptions in Latin where the filiation consists simply of the praenomen of the father, without flilius), the Osean usage. Of possible candidates for inclusion in this category, we do not include: ILLRP 286 (Supinum), where flilius) is in fact present; 545 (Ariminum), where flilius) may have been on a neighbouring block; 806 (Perusia), where 5 may stand for sigillator, rather than being a praenomen; 947 (= CIL I (1863) 1553, b, Ariminum), where flilius) is in fact present; 799 (= CIL I (1863) 1256, Volcei), a Greek; CIL I (1863) 1559 = I2 (1917) 2398 (from Spain); CIL I2 (1986) 3103, 3443, a ( fljlius ) omitted in error on both); 3365 (an Etruscan); or R. Cordella &amp; N. Criniti, in Cultus splendore. Studi in onore di Giovanna Sotgiu (Senorbì 2003) pp. 319-45 'Parergon valnerinese', at pp. 324-6, known only from a drawing, where fljlius) may have been on the side of the stele. We emphasise two important methodological points about the coinages of the communities with which we are concerned: first, since coinage is not only a Greek institution in origin, but in many cases one borrowed directly from a Greek city, it tends to carry with it a legend not simply in the Greek alphabet, but also in the Greek language, and we list these legends along with Osean legends, since they are clearly one of the ways in which our communities referred to themselves; secondly, since the space available on a coin may be small, the legends are often abbreviated in ways that may seem erratic and arbitrary. As far as the first point is concerned, the Latin coinage of Caiatia is not in itself sufficient to identify Caiatia as Latin-speaking. There is a prolific coinage, in the middle of the third century BC, of the Latin colonies of Cales and Suessa Aurunca, of the allied communities of Aquinum and Teanum, and of Caiatia. The coins are identical in types, weight and metal, and were clearly interchangeable between the different communities. Teanum had produced silver and bronze coinage with Osean legends, Aquinum was, to judge from the inscriptions, Oscan-using; but the legends on the group of coins in question are without exception Latin, despite the fact that the denominational structure of the coinage is Greek, not Roman.22 It is hard to avoid the conclusion that the Latin of Cales and Suessa Aurunca was seen as more prestigious in an economic context that involved all five communities. The second point also requires development. In general, in considering the coinages of the ancient world, it is important to be aware of certain essential features of their production: not only was the space available for the die-cutter to create the legend often very small, but the dies also tended to deteriorate in the course of use. The legends that scholars can study therefore often display mere errors on the part of the die-cutter, known as blundered legends; and they often include severely damaged letters. Coins were also often carelessly struck, resulting in further difficulties of reading; and part of the legend of an individual specimen may simply be off the edge of the coin. The progressive study of ever more specimens has mitigated some of these problems; and the readings of earlier scholars, often accompanied only by drawings, should be treated with great suspicion if they cannot be verified. 20 G. Bradley, in A. Cooley (ed.), The Epigraphic Landscape of Roman Italy ( BICS , Supp. 73, 2000) pp. 3-17, 'The colonization of Interamna Nahars'. 21 See Concordances I, under Campania. 22 See Crawford, CMRR (1985) pp. 47-48.</page><page sequence="24">INTRODUCTION 7 By way of example, von Planta in 1897 (213, b) printed separately [..Ąnom, which is simply an incomplete version of loukanom ; Antonini has been misled by incorrect descriptions of the coin legends of the Fenserni and of Allifae (see Fenserni 1, Allifae l);23 Stuart-Smith wrongly supposes that part of the coinage of Aesernia belongs before the foundation of the Latin colony in 263 BC (not 268 BC) and takes an s, that has simply been reversed because of the difficulty of engraving in mirror-image, as a z.24 As a corollary, the level of our description of coinage varies according to the level of the problems posed today by the issue in question. A category that also needs thinking about as a whole is that of inscribed helmets, where the text is sometimes prominently displayed, sometimes hardly visible: in our view, only Lucania or Brettii or Sicilia 3 and Lucania 1 are certainly dedications; the other inscriptions are marks of ownership. * * * The texts are ordered first by region, roughly from north to south, and then by the Roman community to which they may be assigned on geographical grounds, and numbered separately within each such community. This is easily justifiable in the case of inscriptions of the fourth to first centuries BC, where there is in almost all cases good evidence of continuity from the immediately pre-Roman period to the Roman period. Some justification is perhaps required in the case of inscriptions of the sixth to fifth centuries; and the decision flows in large measure from the argument that the historic communities of peninsular Italy were formed in the early Iron Age.25 Some inscriptions, for which no precise provenance exists, are placed in a general regional category, at the beginning of each region or group of regions. Just as we use standard epigraphical conventions, in deliberate contrast to some recent linguistic publications, so we also broadly follow the model of CIL, placing first the coinage of a community, as the most powerful evidence of its collective existence, then public and sacred inscriptions, then inscriptions of private individuals, mostly funerary, finally instrumentum domesticum; we deviate from the practice of CIL, for obvious reasons, in keeping together inscriptions from the same site; in the case of large bodies of material we begin with the urban centre and then deal with its territory. The only exception to this approach is that we place 'South Picene' inscriptions first in the texts from the territory of each community: since one of our aims is to insist on the analysis together of the linguistic and archaeological evidence, we have decided to place South Picene inscriptions found, for instance, in the territories of the Paeligni with the inscriptions of the Paeligni. Examples of the same tile or pot stamp, although found at different locations, are presented together; and, where specimens come from more than one community, position in the corpus is determined by the place with the highest number of examples, which is deemed to be the place of production: place of production takes precedence over place of discovery. We print a single text, with specimens sorted by dates and circumstances of discovery, and all information specific to each specimen grouped under that specimen; only a selection has been illustrated; and our initial hope that it would be possible to classify specimens by the stamps from which they were produced has not been fulfilled. Each graffito is normally accorded a separate entry, as important evidence for the geography of language use; but graffiti on the same pot are listed together; and in a few cases we have, as an aid to 23 Quaderni dell'Istituto di Linguistica dell'Università di Urbino 6 (1989) pp. 33-91, 'Materiali per un'indagine preliminare sull'alfabeto osco', at pp. 49 (Fenserni), 51, 53 (Allifae: also M. Janda, in H. Rix (ed.), Oskisch-Umbrisch (Wiesbaden 1993) pp. 147-54, 'Die Inschrift des Helms von Canosa und der Buchstabe ß des Südpikenischen', at p. 153, with two further inaccuracies). 24 Phonetics and Philology. Sound Change in Italic (Oxford 2004) pp. 97-98; the variant legends are merely the result of incompetent engraving; and Suessa was not Samnite (nor Cales)! 25 See M. H. Crawford, in D. Braund &amp; C. Gill (edd.), Myth, History and Culture in Republican Rome (Exeter 2003) pp. 56-72, 'Land and people in Republican Italy'; A. Ziolkowski, Archeologia (Warsaw) 56 (2005) pp. 31-51, 'The aggeres and the rise of urban communities in Early Iron Age Latium'.</page><page sequence="25">8 IMAGINES ITALICS understanding, grouped together all the graffiti from a single archaeological context, notably in the case of Monte Vairano. In such a case, of course, whether an uncertain mark is alphabetic or not makes almost no difference to the overall picture. Above all, we hope to provide accessibility to the material for non-specialists: we both transcribe the texts according to the systems now in place for the different alphabets and employ the conventions now standard for epigraphical texts; and, as far as is possible, we provide an English translation, justifying it briefly where necessary. All translations should be understood as coming with a health warning. Both here and elsewhere, we have tried to show the range of possibilities, where several exist, and to highlight un-resolvable problems. We have tried to offer a date, preferring a wide date-range to misleading precision, and to explain on what it is based. There is at any rate some evolution in orthography both in the Osean alphabet and in the Greek alphabet used in the south, and some evolution in letter forms in these as well as in the Latin alphabet. And although it has generally been believed that most Italic inscriptions are impossible to date, there are a number of cases where attention to the archaeological evidence can offer considerable help. Although the nineteenth-century excavations of Corfinium were primitive in their methodology, to say the least, there are some inscriptions for which the associated finds provide some indication of context. A particularly dramatic case is that of Capua 36, where the vases in the tomb concerned can be identified and dated with great precision. The Rhodian amphora handles found in the habitation area near the sanctuary of Mefitis in the Valle d'Ansanto can be identified and dated. And although both the excavation and the publication of the site of Rossano di Vaglio leave something to be desired, the archaeological evidence for the date of the complex is nonetheless very good, and has been confirmed by further excavation in recent years. In what follows, we discuss a number of problems, in the order in which the texts appear in the corpus; the discussion naturally varies considerably in length, depending on the amount of material and on its intractability. But we think it important for our readers to consider the problems of the material as a whole, before plunging into the corpus, not least because much of what we have to say is relevant to more than one part of the corpus. ITALIA 'Italic', 'Sabellic', even 'Osean' (despite Livy X, 20, 8), and 'Osco-Umbrian', as names for the languages with which we are concerned, are all modern terms of art, as indeed is 'South Picene' (see below, p. 10). In our view, however, we should be guided above all by the usage of those who used these languages. There is a very large measure of correspondence between them and the peoples who fought against Rome in 91-88 BC. They called their state and their capital Italia, and themselves Italici, and we have no hesitation in calling our volumes Imagines Italicae.26 We place therefore the coinage of the war against Rome first in the series; it has in any case nothing to do with the Paeligni, where it is often classified, even if much of it was struck at Corfinium, chosen as their 26 It should be obvious that terms such as 'Oenotrian' are totally unusable as a label either for a culture or for a language: see E. J. Bickerman, CPh 47 (1952) pp. 65-81 = Religions and Politics in the Hellenistic and Roman Periods (Como 1985) pp. 401-17, 'Origines gentium', removing the ground from under the feet of H. Rix, in Otium. Festschrift für Volker Michael Strocka (Remshalden 2005) pp. 323-30, 'Alphabete in vorrömischen Italien'. In general, what the literary sources have to say about the origins of the peoples of Italy belongs in the realm of mythology. If one thinks of this book as lying between linguistics, epigraphy and archaeology, and indeed as seeking to break down the barriers between them, it is to say the least unfortunate that in linguistics 'proto-Sabellian', for instance, refers to a phase that may be postulated, but for which no evidence is visible, whereas in archaeology 'proto- Villanovan' is a visible phase prior to 'Villanovan'.</page><page sequence="26">INTRODUCTION 9 capital by the Italici. We place next as Italia 2-9 (?) a group of texts usually classified as Lucanian, on inadequate grounds, holding rather that they are the product of the administration of Italia.27 VMBRIA28 It is one of the ironies of history that the region that has produced the queen of Osco-Umbrian texts, the Iguvine Tables, should be so poor in other material. There have been some new discoveries, but nothing on the scale of the material from Samnium and Lucania, and it is hard to imagine that the situation is likely to change dramatically. Many of the cities of Umbria had in the classical period relatively small territories, and it may be that the need for writing was slow to make itself felt: virtually all the material is public or sacred. But the two cities that almost reach or reach double figures in inscriptions, Mevania and Tuder, not only have relatively large territories but are also close to Etruria, from whose different scripts the Umbrian alphabet was borrowed. There is perhaps just enough material from Tuder to say also that there the private prevails over the public or the sacred. But if proximity to Etruria was important to Tuder, it remains puzzling why Perusia, rich in Etruscan inscriptions, did not influence the epigraphic habit of its neighbours. It may be that the use of funerary tiles at Tuder is due to Etruscan influence;29 there is probably not enough evidence to argue that two-letter abbreviations of names are particularly characteristic of Tuder. Two other points are worth high-lighting: first, Umbria provides the only examples of gamonymics in the Italic languages, apart from Aeclanum 3 and Bouianum 93 and perhaps Teanum Apulum 2; and, given that with the re-reading of Asisium 3 Assisi no longer provides an example of the letter b, its presence at Tuder is presumably to be attributed to proximity also to Latin-using areas. SABINVM and neighbours Recent discoveries have revealed a body of archaic material unimaginable a generation ago. We shall see shortly that Campania was in the archaic and classical periods an area of alphabetic experiment, and the same is true of western central Italy, though the experimentation here centred on what to do about the letter f where Latin adopted the expedient that is with us to this day. Before coming to the letter /, there is a different problem to be faced, the uses of the 'quadro vuoto', Phoenician 'hêt', and the 'finestrella', Phoenician 'sāmeķ'.30 Further discoveries are of course likely to upset any general statement, but at the moment we know of no evidence that decisively contradicts the hypothesis that the 'quadro vuoto' always represents h, the 'finestrella', presumably used because it was available, always represents /. The latter is attested at Tolfa (Caere 1), at Satricum (Satricum 1), on the flask from Chiusi (Sabini 1), on the kylix in Florence (a variant: Sabini 2), at Magliano Sabina (Forum Nouum 1), on the vase in Uppsala (a circle: Capena 1). A variety of derivative forms is then used for I in the 'South Picene' alphabet. In our view, the choice of the sign Ť for /in Faliscan is best seen as designed to create an / that avoids the possibility of confusion with Etruscan v. It seems that at Anagnia, on the other hand (see below, p. 16), hv was used for /; and that hu was used on the bowl from Minturnae (Minturnae 1), wherever the Italic inscription on the outside was actually inscribed, though that depends on equating the name with Afidius 27 See M. We place between Umbria and Sabinum a group of three inscriptions, from Ariminum, Tolfa near Caere, and Satricum, which are too isolated for their alphabets or languages to be characterised with certainty, but which document some of the problems just discussed: thus the inscription from Tolfa certainly, and the inscription from Ariminum possibly, document the form of / that settled down as unique to Faliscan, namely Ť. Faliscan Ť, then, can be seen as derived from Etruscan v; but a similar sign, derived however from u, was used for ú in the 'South Picene' alphabet, which therefore 'needed' a different sign for f The solution adopted takes us back into the mainstream of the Italic languages. For the most influential solution for the letter / in the Italic world was 8; its occurrence at Poggio Sommavilla, Forum Nouum 2 (600 BC), perhaps suggests that it was invented by the Sabines and passed from them to the Etruscans, where it seems to have appeared c. 550 BC;32 if that is so, its appearance at Anagnia will presumably be in a piece of writing in the Etruscan alphabet. The 'South Picene' alphabet, on the other hand, adopted the letter 8 in the form of two dots because it was much easier to write it in that way (on the relationship in general between Sabine and 'South Picene' see below, p. 11). The letter seems to appear in the form of three dots at Satricum. To be placed beside the possibility that it was the Sabines who invented the letter 8 is to be placed the fact that the Sabine alphabet, unlike the Etruscan, used o; this may indicate a direct derivation from a Greek alphabet.33 With the patterns just discussed in mind, in contrast to Sabellische Texte, we include certain texts from Capena, which display affinities with 'South Picene'; Capena, like the territory of the Faliści, is of course also an area with Etruscan material.34 We do not think that there are sufficient grounds - the presence of q and of V = A - to justify regarding the new inscription from Magliano Sabina as in the Sabine, rather than the Faliscan, alphabet.35 PICENVM 'South Picene' is the most problematic of the alphabets and languages that we include (we use the term without quotation marks hereafter). In posing for Vestine, Marrucine and Paelignian the alternative of 'Samnitisch mit Substrateinflüssen oder samnitisiertes Südpikenisch', however, Meiser excludes a possibility that we regard as plausible, to which we come in a moment.41 It is taken for granted by archaeologists working in these areas that there was substantial continuity of population and community from the archaic to the Hellenistic period;42 and there is a remarkable example of continuity of toponym in the case of the pagus Boedinus (Superaequum 2). The central problem is very simple: why does one find such a group of inscriptions, over such a vast area and for such a short time, most of them around 500 BC, all of them - apart from three to be discussed immediately below - from a funerary context, all of these - apart from two from the territory of the Praetuttii (see below, p. 13) - monumental stelai? Sgattoni, L 'Abruzzo antico (Lanciano 1979) pp. 89-92, for the implausible claim that the Penna Sant'Andrea stelai were found 'sulle "piane" di Monte Giove', and came originally from the sanctuary on Monte Giove.</page><page sequence="29">12 IMAGINES ITALICS Giovanna Rocca has argued that a Sabine alphabet underlies the South Picene alphabet (n. 33); and James Clackson observes that in South Picene -kupri and qupíríh plausibly = 'well', whereas ciprum = 'good' in Sabine (Varro, LL V, 159; note that cubr- = 'good' in Umbrian).44 At this point one recalls the root safin- in the texts from Penna Sant'Andrea. The hypothesis we should like to offer is that most of eastern central Italy was briefly conquered and held around 500 BC by a group of Sabine warrior-chiefs, importing their language, just as the Etruscans did to Campania and the Po valley. Possible? Do not forget that the men who dedicated at the great sanctuary of Pietrabbondante, in central Samnium, on at least one occasion did so after they had fought a successful war against Taras, though not admittedly a war leading to conquest. It is worth recalling that, in an adventurous mix of very much later literary, with archaeological evidence (the latter mostly from necropoleis), M. Cristofani has argued that centres of population develop in Sabinum in the eighth and seventh centuries BC, along the Adriatic sea in the seventh and sixth centuries BC, under not very clearly defined Sabine (and Umbrian) influence.45 We should like to suggest, therefore, that we are dealing, not with a South Picene substrate overlaid by Osean, but with languages spoken continuously along the Adriatic from the early archaic period onwards, influenced over a century or so by the language of a conquering elite. We are struck by the fact that, as far as we know, the only epigraphic attestation of Talus as a praenomen, attested as Sabine by Festus (492 L), is in South Picene country (Falerio 2); but, in view of what we say above (p. 8, n. 26), only very hesitantly cite Festus, Pauli Ep. 235 L: 'Picena regio, in qua est Asculum, dicta, quod, Sabini cum Asculum proficiscerentur, in uexillo eorum picus consederať, the region of Picenum is so called because, when the Sabines set out for Asculum, a magpie (picus ) settled on their standard. Why did the Sabines lose their Empire? We were initially tempted to down-date Romulus by three centuries or so, and say that they had of course to go and try to get their women back from the Romans. But perhaps they just saw that the line taken by Appius Claudius promised, to borrow a phrase from John North, more and better dinners. In the end, that decision was vindicated; but it did take another four centuries. A problem is posed by the apparent chronological isolation of the two 'pilos' helmets with South Picene inscriptions, conventionally dated since 1976 to c. 300 BC (Interpromium A and B). But since the bowls of Type B and Type C are the same and since the bowls were often re-cycled with new cheek- pieces, none of the evidence adduced by F. Coarelli prevents the bowls in question being of c. 350 BC or earlier;46 a helmet of Type C from Forentum is dated to c. 350-330 BC;47 and a helmet which adopts the principle of 'falere' suspended from a bowl made without join has been found in a late fifth century BC grave in the Marne valley.48 It must also be said that the attachment of the cheek pieces, both on Interpromium B and on the helmet from the Bologna, Benacci 953, tomb, looks extraordinarily incompetent, and one wonders whether the helmets in question were not originally made without cheek- pieces, at an even earlier date than c. 350 BC. In discussing a terracotta model of a 'pilos' helmet without flaps in the Louvre, of c. 350-325 BC, S. It is clear in any case that late South Picene inscribing moves into a different medium from those used earlier; it may also have been geographically very restricted: although the three objects in question come from Bologna, near Chieti (?), and Canosa, all are highly transportable and may all have been made in a very restricted area, which of course need not be that of any of the find-spots. Faute de mieux, we place the two helmets just before the bracelet. One linguistic feature is worth noticing, namely the likely presence in South Picene of the future imperative, characteristic of Latin legislative texts from the Twelve Tables of 450 B.C. onwards, namely haligatú and de[datu] (Superaequum 2, Asculum Picenum 3); the form is also present in the Tortora stele (Blanda 1), and its attestation in the Italic languages c. 500 BC is of the highest importance for our understanding of early Roman legislative practice.52 PRAETVTTII Like Picenum, the area has produced no monumental epichoric texts between those in South Picene and those in Latin. The vase from Castrum Nouum, the text on which was not recorded, is presumably a parallel for that from Campovalano (Interamnia Praetuttiorum 6, Castrum Nouum 1). VESTINI Of the different communities of the Vestini, Peltuinum was in the Imperial period described indifferently as a praefectura or as a municipium ; rather than invoking elaborate constitutional theories, we prefer to suppose that carelessness or inertia perpetuated the title of praefectura in the period of the municipium .53 Although there is no explicit evidence, the once-upon-a-time status of Aveia and Peltuinum as a praefectura suggests that the Vestini Cismontani were dues sine suffragio from 301/290 BC, while the Transmontani remained socii, producing coinage (Vestini 1). 5 The evidence for magistracies, all from Incerulae, appears to shows aediles in three groups of two and two groups of two, presumably functioning in three and two successive years respectively (Incerulae 1-2). 49 S. MARRVCINI The Marrucini formed in the Roman period a single municipium, Teate, now Chieti; one of us has argued that Interpromium belonged in the pre-Roman period to the Paeligni.56 Apart from the evidence for cult provided by the Rapino bronze, the inscriptions attest a priestess of Ceres and a priestess of Venus; the balance of probability in our view is that Teate Marrucinorum 5 is the tombstone of two private individuals. For the culture of Teate in the pre-Roman period, note the bronze jug, with the graffito x^Àic, 'neat wine', on the bottom.57 PAELIGNI Despite the fact that almost all known inscriptions from Corfinium come from excavations, the primitive nature of those excavations means that evidence for chronology is almost wholly lacking. A cautious view will be that the inscriptions as a group belong to the period between 150 BC and shortly after the Social 58 War. 58 Attempts to use the 'archaeological' evidence to date individual inscriptions are largely doomed: in the case of those of ob. ouiedis. I (Corfinium 23) and arghillus salauatur (Corfinium 9), the evidence of pottery and coinage suggests dates of c. 150 BC and c. 100 BC respectively; but the letter forms of the first inscription suggest rather a date after the Social War. The problem of chronology is particularly acute in the case of the pristafalacirix inscription (Corfinium 6). There is in any case no warrant for a confident dating to 90-80 BC;61 in our view, the flatfish c and s, the spread m and the open p, all suggest a date earlier than 100 BC; and if the 'balsamari' apparently found beneath the tomb belong with it, that too would suggest a date before, rather than after, 100 BC. It has traditionally been held that the features are borrowed from early Latin poetry; but E. Dupraz has argued recently that they are an inheritance from South Picene.63 His objections to the traditional view are weak, since the adoption of the Latin alphabet shows the whole area under strong Latin influence from the third century BC onwards. But the metrical features in question do not seem to us to be particularly distinctive, and we suppose rather that they were widespread in early Italy. As far as magistracies are concerned, at Interpromium we have a pair of meddices, and three pairs of men, the indication of whose office, if any, was lost in the cutting down of the block; at Superaequum, we twice have groups of three men, presumably magistři ; at Corfinium, we have a pair of meddices , as well as an anceta, perhaps a sacra[crix], not to mention a salauatur and a nutrix; at Sulmo, we have a group of four men, whose office is not identified, as well as numerous ancetae. We follow the view that the word an(a)c(e)ta means 'priestess'.64 The Paeligni share with Teanum Sidicinum the presence of large numbers of funerary inscriptions, rare in the Italic world outside Umbria in the Hellenistic period;65 two of them are double commemorations. We do not share the view that the cognomina char{ito ) and herclit(is) are those of freedmen;66 Herakleides may have reached Paelignian via Etruscan herclite, and we regard both names as deliberately chosen for their Greek intellectual resonances. The evidence of provenance for Sulmo allows us to see that the town possessed in the pre-Roman period south, east, and west necropoleis, and that the town occupied much the same area in the pre-Roman, Roman and medieval periods. Similarly, at Corfinium, the principal necropolis begins where the medieval and modern town ends. Sulmo, Corfinium and Superaequum, indeed, clearly formed already in the pre- Roman period urban centres with villages in the territory. MARSI We have on reflection excluded the famous caso cantouio inscription, since it is clearly in a form of Latin.67 The Velitrae bronze, hitherto regarded as Volscian, is in our view not so (see [Velitrae 1]); since meddices are attested among the Marsi, it may come from there, or from the territory of the Aequi. HERNICI A language recently added to the family of Italic languages, Hernican is so far attested from the discoveries in two sanctuaries of a single city, Anagnia. We have decided to include all the texts from the two sanctuaries in question, even though the language of some of them cannot be identified with certainty. One text seems to use hv for/(Anagnia 13).69 The texts provide no evidence for the institutions of the Hernici. CAMPANIA The most complex linguistic and institutional situation is undoubtedly that of Campania, where the languages in use from the archaic period included Greek, Etruscan and no doubt various Italic languages.70 We deal first with the linguistic situation, noting that the category 'Pre-Samnite' in ST is deeply unsatisfactory: the only long text from Campania is most likely to be that of an immigrant from the north (Ps 3 = Capua 35); Ps 1 = Nerulum 1 is misread, Ps 2 = ? Scolacium 1 ? is of very dubious authenticity (for Ps 20 = Blanda 1, see below, p. 50). The Etruscan language is not certainly known to have been written other than in the Etruscan alphabet, except once at Vulci, on a vase probably painted by a Greek;71 but the Italic languages were initially written in the Greek and Etruscan alphabets, as well as the 'alphabet of Nuceria'. This last is now known from three inscriptions, probably of the second half of the sixth century BC (Surrentum 2-3; Nuceria Alfaterna 3), and was clearly an experiment that went nowhere.72 It adapted the script of the Ionic Dodekapolis and colonies, conveniently available after the foundation of Velia c. 540 BC, using a variant of their xi to represent s ; the principal features of the alphabet are its rectangularity and ornateness, and the impression that the engraver was looking along the line, rather than upwards at it.73 Given the implausibility of Pafius as a nomen, the sign 3, that has hitherto been taken as representing/ is in our view the third letter of the alphabet, representing c or g, as in early Latin. A feature of the use of the Etruscan alphabet to write Osean, which lasted till c. 400 BC or later at Saticula and Nola (the evidence shows no clear boundary with the Osean alphabet), is the presence of the so- called syllabic interpuncts, used in the Etruscan language to indicate 'abnormal' syllables. Syllabic interpuncts were, however, not in the end (see below on the coinage of Hurietes) transferred to the Osean alphabet. Isolated cases of Etruscan v occur later at Teanum Sidicinum (Teanum Sidicinum 1), in the coinage with vei nav (Campania 9), and in the coinage of Nuceria Alfaterna (Nuceria Alfaterna 1), of Etruscan 0 in the coinage with irnQiin (*Irinthum 1). There are on the other hand also late occurrences of the use of the Greek alphabet, rather than the Osean alphabet, at Capua (Capua 44) and Cumae (Cumae 17, 22-4). A further feature of archaic Campania is the very large number of Osean names which occur in texts written in the Etruscan language.74 It is in our view in Campania that the Osean alphabet was developed, presumably contributing to a shared sense of identity between Campania and the areas to which the alphabet spread; Oscan-speakers further south persisted with the use of the Greek alphabet, though not always the script they had first used.75 As far as the Oscan-Greek alphabet is concerned, we share the view of M. Cristofani, arguing for continuity in the use of one Greek script or another, from the early fifth century BC inscription of trebis onwards (Salernum 2). 76 It may be helpful to tabulate the communities where the presence of the Etruscan and/or an Italic language is attested, of the Greek alphabet, and of archaeological evidence for the archaic and classical periods (see the Table overleaf). The inscriptions are almost without exception on vases, and one must, as always, remember that they may not have been engraved where the vase was found, though for most sites the volume of evidence is such as to dispel uncertainty. We omit Cumae, where a direct transition from the Greek to the Osean language is sufficiently attested, and Neapolis, and a couple of smaller sites. We also, while regarding it as probable that Saticula is to be assigned to the territory of the Caudini, treat the community as belonging to Campania, because of the volume of early Italic inscriptions. In the case of Cumae, it is worth observing that the stamp tiv0 used at Pithecusae c. 300 BC presumably represents a name of Etruscan origin;77 and that tcctcciec is probably of Etruscan origin, Greek in form.78 There is also evidence for the survival of Etruscan names at Neapolis: XapSioc (Miranda 138; not in LGPN), Tapxioc (Miranda 152), TtvScop (Miranda 141). Note also that the graffito e of c. 525-500 BC, from Pozzilli, near Venafrum, published as Osean,79 is as likely to be Greek or Etruscan. We exclude Eburum, since, although it has Etruscan inscriptions, it has no Italic inscriptions. Crawford, 'Language, geography and the Roman conquest of Italy' (forthcoming); also the Appendix.</page><page sequence="36">INTRODUCTION 19 The standard view is that the Osean alphabet was essentially derived from the Etruscan alphabet, and the direction of writing and the similarity of the letter forms, as well as the fact that neither alphabet uses o, are in its support. But, as H. Rix has observed, the Osean alphabet contains b and d,m whereas the Etruscan alphabet does not; and indeed all of the numerous Etrüscan abecedaria precisely from Campania run simply a, c, e , ... 102 One may add that the value of the third letter of the Osean alphabet is the Greek value, not the Etruscan value; and that the Osean alphabet recovers also the rectangularity of Classical Greek letter forms. But there are fatal objections to Rix's migrationist view, that the Osean alphabet was in part derived from the South Picene alphabet:103 the latter is not attested in Campania, since the 'alphabet ofNuceria' is derived from the Ionic script. Even if 3 did represent / in the 'alphabet of Nuceria', which we reject (see above), a derivation from the South Picene alphabet would be the least likely hypothesis: the alleged exemplar is both dissimilar and in any case represents digamma, whereas /in the South Picene alphabet is represented as :.104 Furthermore, although the South Picene alphabet includes differentiated versions of i and u, the Osean alphabet did not include these at the beginning, but introduced them only c. 300 BC (see below, p. 22). As for the sign E =/ at Tortora (in Lucania, not Bruttium), which otherwise uses the Achaean script, it is in our view invented ad hoc, and could only be supposed to derive from the 'alphabet ofNuceria' by hypothesising the name Pafius (see above).105 E at Tortora =/ cannot derive from the South Picene alphabet, on the same grounds of dissimilarity and different phonetic value as apply in the case of 3 in the 'alphabet ofNuceria'.106 The Achaean script in use at Tortora is, apart from f identical with or a very slight variant of, the first two phases identified by Ann Jeffery.107 The first systematic and large-scale attempt to write an Italic language in Campania is the production, alongside the coinages of Cumae and Neapolis, of those of the Campani, Allifae, Phistelia, the Hurietes, the Fenserni, and Nola, between 415 and 380 BC. Allifae and Phistelia also issued prolific silver fractions after a gap of two generations; Nola issued silver fractions and bronze from c. 300 BC; and Cumae produced a tiny issue of didrachms c. 300 BC, in a period in which as a community of dues sine suffragio it should not have done so; we wonder whether it was brusquely told to stop? 101 (n. 26) p. 325. 102 For an Italic text in the Etruscan alphabet, using p for b, see Saticula 1. 103 (n. 40) p. 160, claiming that the 'pre-Samnite' texts from Campania 'offenbar (!) einem südpikenischen Dialekt angehören', p. 167: (1) -es for -eis! -eis occurs intermittently throughout Osean; (2) Capua 35 was almost certainly not composed by a native of Capua; (3) the existence at Blanda and in South Picene texts of a similar form of the 3rd plural past does not require a wave of migration to explain it. (For rejection of the view that final -h in púiieh and in upsatuh on the one hand and in South Picene on the other hand have similar antecedents, see H. Eichner, cited on Capua 45; and p. 24, n. 118.) G. Colonna, in F. Senatore &amp; M. Russo (edd.), Sorrento e la Penisola Sorrentina (Rome 2010) pp. 25-39, 'Presentazione del volume M. Triantafillis, SE 73 (2007) pp. 482-89, is very selective. 104 Poccetti (n. 72) pp. 45-7, is misleading on the coinage of Allifae, the supposed coinage of Venafrum, and the date at which B =/ appears at Rossano di Vaglio. 105 Contra Rix (n. 26) p. 326 (for '(1.5)' in col. 2 read '(2.5.1)'); P. Poccetti, in G. de Sensi Sestito (ed.), La Calabria tirrenica nell'antichità (Soveria Mannelli 2008) pp. 279-301, 'La documentazione linguistica preromana lungo la fascia tirrenica a sud di Velia'. 106 MC 1, AP 1, TE 2, TE 5 = Vrbs Saluia 1, Asculum Picenum 1, Interamnia Praetuttiorum 4, Interamnia Praetuttiorum 1; to talk of a 'digamma apicato', with Triantafillis (n. 30) p. 259; SE 73 (2007) pp. 482-8, disguises the differences. 107 See LSAG (1990) p. 248, fig. 42.</page><page sequence="37">20 IMAGINES ITALICS It is essential for the understanding of the phenomenon in which we are interested to realise, as has been clear since 1979, that all the coinages were produced at Neapolis, in other words by the personnel of a Greek mint for Oscan-speakers. 108 From c. 475 and c. 450 BC respectively, the two Greek poleis in Campania, Cumae (Kyme) and Neapolis, had produced coinage of a normal Greek type: silver two-drachma pieces, weighing about 7.60 gr., and a variety of fractions. With the Osean take-over of Cumae, coinage of the same type continued to be produced there, with the two-drachma piece weighing about 7.50 gr. (Cumae 1); it was also produced, with the two-drachma piece weighing about 7.30 gr., in the names of Allifae, Capua, and Nola, as well as of three communities, Phistelia, the Hurietes, and the Fenserni, which cannot be securely localised. 109 The production of all the coinages at the single mint of Neapolis emerges from the pattern of die-sharing identified by Keith Rutter: thus, Capua shares two obverse dies with Cumae, one with Neapolis; Neapolis shares two obverse dies with Nola; Nola shares four obverse dies with the Hurietes, one with Allifae; the Hurietes share two obverse dies with the Fenserni. In addition, reverse die #119 of the Hurietes was re- engraved with the ethnic NEOFTOU - TH2 (the second half is retrograde), also perhaps (in intention) reverse die #136. (A die originally of Thurii was used for the coinages of the Hurietes and the Fenserni; and a die originally of the Hurietes was used for the coinage of Poseidonia: compare below, p. 50.) A recently published coin attests the hitherto missing die-link between Neapolis and Phistelia (see Phistelia 1). Nola cannot in any simple sense be the successor of the Hurietes, as is sometimes supposed, since the two coinages are in part contemporary, and Nola is in any case mentioned already by Hecataeus, fr. 61 Jacoby, the name being perhaps the Greek and Latin form of an Osean word for 'new' (city). But we wonder whether there was a period in which there was a double community, with a newer, Italic community beside, and eventually superseding, an older, Etruscan community: both the legend AupisTsc, which occurs only on the first reverse die, and the legend urina vel sim., which is the normal form, are distinctively Etruscan (see Hurietes 1: the ethnic re-appears in ET Cm 2. 24 (Capua): mi mamerces urinates); and the site of Nola is one of the most prolific in Campania in Etruscan inscriptions, precisely in the period of Hecataeus. The coinage of the Hurietes is also by far the largest of the coinages in question, and it would be odd for there to be no substantial, and Etruscan-using, settlement to correspond to it. (In contrast, it is not embarrassing that we cannot identify a settlement to correspond to the tiny coinage of the Fenserni.) It is also relevant that according to Vellerns I, 7, the Nola of his day had originally been an Etruscan foundation (the claim to a Chalcidian origin in Justin XX, 1, 23, and Silius Italicus XII, 161, is a piece of kinship diplomacy, invented in the context of relations with Neapolis). We surely know too little to exclude a priori the possibility of a double community at Nola (see also below, p. 32, on Allifae, p. 24, on the vereia). Before we come to a consideration of the origin of the Osean alphabet, it is important to remind ourselves that coin legends are liable to error in engraving. It is thus important to realise that some supposed diacriticals are an illusion: the two alleged cases of ú on the later fractional coinage of Phistelia are die-breaks (Phistelia 1); to the dots attached to i and u on the coinage of the Hurietes we return below. Our sources emphasise both the Oscanisation of Cumae and the survival of Neapolis as a Greek polis ; of the coinages, that of Neapolis remains Greek in language, like the polis, though individual Osean names appear on it (see the Appendix); that of Cumae also remains Greek in language, and one cannot see either reversing or inverting of individual letters, or legends from right to left, blundered legends and incomplete legends, as evidence for anything except incompetent die-cutting (see Cumae 1); in fact, the later coinage 108 N. Valenza Mele, in M. Tagliente (ed.), Italici in Magna Grecia (Venosa 1990) pp. 23-33, 'La necropoli di Cuma', goes too far in claiming that the coinage shows the 'dipendenza economica' of Cumae on Neapolis, and makes a surprising mis-use of Livy IV, 52, 6. 109 N. The legends of Capua itself, the Campani, are also Greek in alphabet and morphology, again with reversed letters, and reversed and incomplete legends. In one case, one can prove that a legend that appears from right to left on the coins was cut from left to right on the die (see Capua 1). The character of the coinage of Nola is similar. None of these three mints, of course, needed a letter that was not readily available in the Euboic script, though it is interesting that Nola, where the die-engravers seem to have aimed at NfìAAlQN, of the men of Nola, used omega for the first syllable, as well as for the genitive plural. It is also interesting that Nola, when it resumed the production of coinage c. 300 BC, when it was a fully Oscan-using community, stuck with coin legends in Greek. The first cases to be considered are those of Phistelia, Allifae and the Fenserni, all of which - sooner or later - used 8 for/ (for legends irretrievably blundered by the engravers, see the entries in question). The earliest are the didrachms of Phistelia, of 405-400 BC, with fistluis, all of the letters apart from 8 equally forming part of the Euboic and Etruscan repertoires, 8 only of the latter. In the case of the didrachms of Allifae, of 400-395 BC, the legend is AĻIOHA. The second letter becomes Osean /, but again forms part of the Euboic and Etruscan repertoires; the fourth letter is not, as hitherto reported, phi on its side - the apparent hasta is formed by the 'whiskers' of die-breaks - but omicron, used for ou = f , / thus being represented by f and h, a Greek variant of Etruscan vh. By the time we get to the coinage of the Fenserni, of 395-390 BC, we have a perfect Osean legend, fensemum, all of the letters apart from 8 again forming part of the Euboic and Etruscan repertoires, 8 only of the latter. 1 10 There are no later issues of the Fenserni; but there are substantial issues of fractions, c. 300 BC, of Phistelia and Allifae; the former are in part deliberately bilingual, Osean and Greek, with initial / represented by phi. 1 1 1 The largest coinage is that of the Hurietes, where the coinage begins with the legend M/piETEC, but then seems mostly to have aimed at urina, with four instances of upicxvoc (voļioc ). Both urina and /ïupiETEC are distinctively Etruscan in form, and it is possible to explain the dot that frequently occurs above the u of urina as an Etruscan 'syllabic' interpunct. The dot above the z', however, cannot in fact be an Etruscan 'syllabic' interpunct: although undoubtedly deliberate, it is of extreme rarity, and we would tentatively explain it as a misunderstanding by a Greek die-engraver of the principle involved, just as other engravers went for a Greek form. On the whole, however, the elite of the Hurietes succeeded in imposing a correct Etruscan legend; its gradual 'Oscanisation' may be seen in the progressive abandonment of the interpunct above the u. Other supposed early uses of diacritical marks with i or u are also illusory: Saticula 6: spuriieis culcfham; Nola 6: luvcies cnaiviies', Stabiae 1: ahtica' Surrentum 4: fipivEic;"2 and the recently alleged example in the Etruscan alphabet on a 'coppetta' of c. 500 BC (!!!) from Sorrento:113 it looks very accidental, and one in any case wonders if the inscription is ancient. What seems to have happened, then, is that the personnel of the mint of Neapolis, presumably in consultation with the elites of the communities in question, living in a multi-lingual and multi-alphabet society, built on both the Etruscan alphabet and the Euboic script. In the case off, after an experiment with the Euboic script, they definitively adopted 8 from the Etruscan alphabet.
