Russo (n. 71) pp. 85-92, followed by Triantafillis (n. 30) pp. 47-9, 325, 338; von Planta 177, oo, ca.ive is Etruscan.</page><page sequence="39">22 IMAGINES ITALICS the coinages of Campania, we have an Italic coinage using the Etruscan letters a, e, h, i, l, m, n, r, s, t, u,f but with some cross-fertilisation with Greek alphabetic practice, in the experiment with o h=f and with the recovery of the rectangularity of the Greek alphabet. It is this cross-fertilisation that in our view explains, in the next phase, the assignation of the Greek value to g and the recovery of b and d. The details of that next phase are mysterious, but we might suppose that it took place in the context of the administration of a sanctuary such as that of the Fondo Patturelli at Capua, or a similar sanctuary at Nola, perhaps in the unexcavated garden of the Palazzo Vivenzio. It is easy to see how, to people aware of the possibilities offered by the Greek alphabet, it might have seemed rather silly to write metis, rather than medis' vipis, rather than vibis. We suggest also that the massive coinage with the legend urina embedded the Etruscan form for r, with the consequent need to invent a new form for d. Why did Oscan-speakers not adopt o, a sound that the Osean language used? We offer two not mutually exclusive hypotheses: the coinage with urina, if the pronunciation was *orina, may again have embedded Etruscan u; and if Greek vcoXa = Osean *nuvla, people may have thought that in that particular case omega corresponded to Etruscan u, representing Osean long o, but hesitated to adopt omicron because of an awareness that in some Greek scripts omicron and omega were not distinguished, with the eventual addition of a diacritical to w as a more rational strategy. With the disappearance of the supposed early occurrences of diacriticals, there finally disappears also the notion that / and ú were borrowed as part of the South Picene alphabet,"4 and we can return to the view that in c. 300 BC the Osean alphabet was modified with the addition of diacriticals to create i, as well as i, ú, as well as u, with the two letters placed at the end of the Osean alphabet, following/ in the same relative order as that in which they occurred in the form without diacritical. The new vowel signs, í and ú, were evidently introduced to represent a distinction important in the Italic languages.115 (1) Where Latin only has 4 vowels, i (short and long) and e (short and long), Italic languages generally have six vowels: i (short and long), e (short and long) and a vowel in between the two, transcribed for Osean as i. One could broadly compare the different Osean vowels with the difference between French i, è and è (with French é corresponding to Osean í). What is sometimes confusing for modern readers is that whereas in French orthography the sign e is used to represent two different vowel sounds, in the Osean alphabet (and in the Latin alphabet when used to write Osean) it is the vowel sign i that corresponds to a range of pronunciations. This may partly reflect a wide variation in the sounds represented by the letter / in the languages of Italy: note that sometimes Latin short i when transcribed into Greek is represented by a short e, as Teßepic for Tiberis. We know from etymology the history of these vowels in Italic and Osean. The Osean short í relates to a Latin short i, an Osean long í (sometimes written if), relates to a Latin long e. The Osean short unaccented i derives from other sources: most notably it is the outcome of an original - ios at the end of a word; Osean long unaccented i (sometimes written ii) derives from an original long i. Osean short e has the same origin as Latin short e, but Osean long e (sometimes written ee) arises from secondary developments: it sometimes derives from an original short e before two consonants, one of which was lost. The picture is further complicated by the presence of the original diphthong ei, which is preserved in Osean (written as ei after the introduction of the letter i) but which in Classical Latin becomes a long i, even though it is still written as ei in some Latin inscriptions as late as in the imperial period. The following word equations between Osean and Latin show the relationship for each vowel or diphthong: Osean short i Osean dekis Latin Decius Original dekios Osean long /' Osean viíbis Latin Vībius Original vībios Osean short í Osean píd Latin quid Original kwid Osean long / Osean lígatúís Latin lēgatis Original lēg- 1 14 Contra Rix (n. 26), at p. 328. 115 The account of them here is by James Clackson.</page><page sequence="40">INTRODUCTION 23 Osean short e Osean estud Latin esto Original estõd Osean long e Osean teerúm Latin terra Original tersom Osean diphthong ei Osean deíkum Latin dico Original deik- Long vowels in the Osean script are written as double vowels and seem to have become limited in occurrence to initial syllables of words (the only real exception is tristaamentud, and the long vowel in the middle of this word may well reflect Latin influence, see further below for discussion of a group of apparent exceptions to the rule that the double vowel is limited to the first syllable in a word). It may be that initial syllables were stressed and length was only distinctive in stressed syllables. Osean written in Latin and Greek script also developed conventions for representing the different vowels. In Greek script the digraph £i (at this time no longer pronounced as a diphthong in Greek), came to be used to represent the vowel written with the sign í in Osean script, and the diphthong from original ei was represented by tļi. The table below gives a summary of the different ways of writing Osean in the three different alphabets. Osean written in the Osean alphabet earlier than c. 300 BC and some Osean inscriptions in Greek script show a less developed system (see below, p. 55); note that Campania or Samnium 6 shows i, but not ú, and that í is more consistently used than ú in Capua 20 and Atina 1. Osean writing Original 6 source of the Osean Vowel (in probable : - - 1 . T Latin . 1 ■ n Greek , 6 , , . ■ • ' in Osean in . T Latin . in ■ n Greek , vowel , phonetic , . transcription) ■ ' from -ios and other [i] i i v sources long i [ij » or» i 1 i [i] í i v, e long e [ij í or íí i sii t, £ e [e] ę ę s e before some [e ] e or ee e e combinations of consonants ei ral I ei | ei 1 ņt, et Osean also uses the letter i to represent a y-sound, much as in the Latin alphabet i can represent the same sound in words such as iam, or maior. In Osean, where i is used to represent the y-sound it is often written ii, for example in púmpaiians, the same word as Latin Pompeianus, or heriiad 'may he want', although spellings with a single i, as búvaianúd (Latin Bouiano), also occur. Note that the long i is written ii, which distinguishes it from the use of ii to represent y; and that the diphthong ei is written ei, with the second element being i. Osean names present particular issues with the writing of the vowels. This is because Osean developed two separate declensions for names corresponding to Latin names ending in - ius. Osean has one declension for praenomina, as for example lúvkis or pakis, which correspond to Latin Lucius and Pacius, and a different declension for nomina gentilia, as kluvatiis corresponding to Latin Clouatius. The nomen gentile is written with repeated -iis or sometimes with the second vowel written with an accented i, as viínikiís; these names are usually spelt with final -ies in Latin script and -ieç in Greek script. When the nomina gentilia decline, they tend to keep the double ii or ii of the nominative, even though it is not clear whether this actually represented anything in speech. Thus the name pakis kluvatiis might be attested in the following case forms: nominative: pakis kluvatiis or kluvatiis genitive: pakieís kluvatiieis or kluvatiieis dative: pakiúí kluvatiiúí or kluvatiíúí. (2) Where Latin has two separate signs for the vowels o and u, the earliest Osean inscriptions have only one, u: the Osean alphabet follows the practice of the Etruscan alphabet, which has no separate letter o (see above, p. 19). Oscan-speaking scribes made up for this deficit by introducing the letter ú into the alphabet. However, it is important to note that ú only partly corresponds to Latin o. Just as long e in Osean had merged with original short i, an original long o merges with short u and both are written with the</page><page sequence="41">24 IMAGINES ITALICS vowel sign u (sometimes written uu to indicate length). Osean also preserves the original diphthong ou, which in Latin becomes a long u; in Osean this is written úv, as in the praenomen lúvkis, which corresponds to Latin Lucius. Note that the Osean vowel ú is also found in final syllables as the result of a change affecting original long a when at the end of the word. The following table represents the outcome of different o and u vowels in Osean and their writing in the Osean, Latin and Greek alphabets: Osean writing Original source of the Osean Vowel (in probable : - - 1 : - - - : 1 - : - - - - vowel phonetic transcription) in0scan in Latin inGreek U [u] U U 01), D, O long o [uj u or uu u od, d, o o [o] ú o o, co long a (at end of word) [oj ú o o, co OU [OW] ÚV OU f 9 of, OD, COD (3) It is very likely that the other Italic languages of the fourth century BC and later shared the same system of vowels as Osean, although such a sophisticated representation of the vowel system was not developed in any other contemporary community. The South Picene alphabet also has extra vowel signs which are transcribed as í and Ú, but these do not correspond exactly to the Osean vowels transcribed in the same way (note South Picene suai pis but Osean svaí and pis); South Picene also has a vowel sign for o. The Umbrian vowel system is especially complex owing to a widespread loss of diphthongs and because of the fluctuation in orthography. A particular problem is posed by inscriptions where one would expect the Osean genitive in -eis or - eis , but find instead -es. We doubt whether it is very helpful to regard -es as the survival of a pre-Samnite substrate;116 and reject for Osean the attempt of Colonna to argue that texts such as bruties esum mean 'I am Bruttius', and that the identification as being Bruttius is equivalent to claiming to belong to Bruttius.117 There is no doubt that at Capua, perhaps around 300 BC, upfals salaviis minies was written in order to convey 'Ofellus Salauius, son of Minius', and that -es was useable as a genitive.118 (Prosdocimi argues against Eska and Wallace that -es is as likely to be a survival from Indo-European as it is to be a monophongisation of -eis.U9) James Clackson also observes that in a world where the dominant written language was Etruscan, Oscan-speakers may have used -es endings, just as Celts would later inflect their names in Latin: we translate such texts as bruties esum as 'I belong to Bruttius'. Four texts probably show loss of final 5 before initial 5 in the next word, irela{s' veltinei(s' ahtica{s' vibiei(s ) (Saticula 5, 2, Stabiae 1, Cumae 16). Upsilon occurs occasionally for u throughout the Oscan-using world (Campania or Samnium 6, Hurietes 1, Atina 1, Teruentum 4, Bouianum 97, Fagifulae 2-3); the availability of the form no doubt encouraged its adoption in a loan-word from Greek in Abella 2. * * * The meaning of the word ver eia has perplexed scholars since the nineteenth century.120 A recent attempt by H. Rix to cut the Gordian knot is in our view unsuccessful. Jean-Louis Ferrary has • 123 reminded us of the presence of two fora at Capua, and of the parallelisms • suggested by Heurgon: senātus plebs forum: Albana forum: Seplasia Osean Etruscan124 We suggest that the senate at Capua could use the Seplasia, but (mostly) chose not to, so that it was (potentially) a forum of the people, of two communities that were for certain purposes one. The presence at Capua of a meddix of the people and a meddix of the vereia, an elite with Osean origin and core, would of course also give its full meaning (' the other') to Ennius' 'summus ibi capitur meddix, occiditur alter'.125 A problem particular to Cumae is the existence of three inscriptions bearing a single name, followed by m.v. Mm ( inim ) m.X. and a verb in the plural. The letter x does not exist in the Osean alphabet, and it is hard to see it as other than the numeral '10', as on one part of the Social War coinage, as opposed to the use as numerals of the letters of the Osean alphabet, as on the sortitorium in Chieti (Histonium 6) and on another part of the Social War coinage. The abbreviation m. may plausibly be supposed to be for meddix, v. for vereiias, but the temptation to relate m.X. to the meddix degetasius of Nola, who occurs in a completely different context, should be resisted, quite apart from the impenetrability of such an abbreviation.126 We have therefore a meddix vereiias and ten meddices; we should not want to exclude the possibility that the meddix vereiias was one of the ten, translating 'the meddix vereiias and (in fact) the (body of) ten meddices (as a whole)' (see now Cumae 4 bis). Even in the age of Cicero, the Romans lived quite happily with the idea that there were some things that only patricians did, some things that only plebeians did, and some things that the people did. Despite what we have said above about Nola as a double community, the vereia is not yet attested there, and it may be that its separate existence did not survive into the Hellenistic period; but a vereia as an elite would fit both the occurrences at Pompei (Pompei 24-5). We return also to La Regina's suggestion of 1981 that it was a cavalry elite, drawing attention to the abundant evidence from Paestum for an elite cavalry wearing Chalcidian helmets, though we think he goes too far in describing such cavalry as mercenaries.127 It is precisely a Chalcidian helmet that belonged to a vereia, that we argue to be under the control of Metapontum, operating at Campsa in the territory of Thurii (Metapontum 1). Puzzling on any showing is what appears to be a label for water taken from Pallanum (Pallanum 1), belonging to a Lucanian vereia ; we suppose this to be a detachment of Lucanian cavalry. One of us hopes to return to the problem, and we simply note for the present that the text provides no evidence for Lucanian territory in the Sangro valley; as for the attempt to buttress this idea by reference to a medieval toponym 'Lucana' in the area, we observe that no-one has so far as we know yet attempted to argue for Lucanian territory near Rome on the basis of the Ponte Lucano below Tivoli (see below, p. 47). We mention for the sake of completeness a graffito ve at Bouianum (Bouianum 63), but think it hazardous to suppose a vereia there rather than a name. On the other hand, the tile with fepsKO at Vibo Valentia presumably relates to a vereia, but it is hard to do much with such an isolated piece of evidence (Vibo 3). A probably separate problem is posed by a group of texts consisting either of the letters fe, or fe followed by further text, sometimes with interpunct, sometimes without interpunct or space.128 The examples are: f e ku, Lucania or Brettii or Sicilia 4 (from Lipara) fE- Kap, Potentia 42 fE k/t, Metapontum 2 f e, Thurii Copia, Thurii 2 f e, Caulonia, Caulonia 3 fE, Caulonia, Caulonia 4 fE- tou, Nuceria Brettia 2 Of these, the example from Thurii Copia, and the two from Caulonia, perhaps have nothing do with a vereia: the new inscription from Caulonia (Caulonia 1) suggests that at any rate there fe is an abbreviation for Venus, and that the text on the tiles indicates that they were made for her temple. But none of this changes the nature of the problem. All of the other cases under discussion are on one or another form of instrumentum, all but one on tiles, the exception from Metapontum being on a loom-weight. The least bad solution seems to us to be that f s represents the praenomen Vettius. * * * We discuss at this point a number of features of individual sites in Campania, relevant to their linguistic history, including some problematic aspects of the Osean epigraphy of Pompei. Capua 17, 18, 20 and 29 locate the iúvilas , which supports such a view. If then, the iúvilas were not the stelai, what were they? The word appears most likely to be an adjective, not relating to Jupiter, but to *diovio-, itself an adjective relating to Jupiter.129 The texts on the stelai seem in general to prescribe (either directly or via the future indicative) or record ritual acts; and we suggest that the images perform the same function. The images are the only element, apart from that of outward form, that links the first text, Capua 3, otherwise with only the name minasteis) nasenni(eis), with the stelai as a group. We have placed at the end the three stelai that record ritual acts, a feature that separates these texts from the rest (Capua 27, 28, 29). 130 The texts on the later, tufa stelai are in general longer and more complex than those on the earlier, terracotta stelai; and we suggest that a process similar to that postulated by Mary Beard for the Arval Acta was going on, that the act of writing came to be an end in itself.131 We also suggest, since the texts are beginning to get longer already in the terracotta phase, that the new medium was chosen in part because terracotta stelai were not suitable for them. Some stelai were found with votive terracottas. And one thing that is certain about the Fondo Patturelli is that it contained a large number of altars. The tufa stelai would function rather well as identifiers of altars; 129 See Untermann, Wörterbuch, s.v. diuvilam. 130 See A. La Regina (n. 16) pp. 307-8, for the observation that where a meddix is cited, he is specified for future celebrations (Capua 17, 21, 22), named for past celebrations. 131 See M. Beard, PBSR 53 (1985) pp. 1 14-62, 'Writing and ritual: a study of diversity and expansion in the Arval Acta'.</page><page sequence="46">INTRODUCTION 29 whether the ritual had always taken place on such an altar, originally anonymous, is a question that admits of no answer. If a iúvilú was indeed an altar,132 perhaps relating to a female companion of Jupiter, tayffud, with an object of tufa, in Capua 22, should perhaps mean 'with a statue of tufa', perhaps one of the matres. As far as chronology is concerned, although the close link posited by Rix between the images on Capua 11- 12 with early third-century BC coins cannot stand, a date for those images in the late fourth century BC on stylistic grounds would not be inappropriate. One might then place the stelai using the unreformed alphabet between c. 325 and 300 BC (note Capua 7), and those using the reformed alphabet between c. 300 and 250 BC. One might wonder whether the adoption of the practice was an assertion of identity, in reaction to Capua becoming part of the Roman world. It is then a sobering reflection that each of the pairs Capua 21-22, 25-26, 27-28 relates to the same people, a remarkable level of duplication in such a small sample. The two texts in each case record different ritual acts, as is also the case with the two sides of the opisthographic stele, Capua 24. So it may be not that we have only a tiny fraction of the stelai that once existed, but a relatively complete set of records of rather infrequent ritual acts. Three further remarks. If the translations offered of the texts are correct, we have records of four months of the Capuan year, March, Faleniae, Luisares, and Sollemnes, as well as of an intercalary month. And while we have a reference to a gate, perhaps of the complex as a whole, the other topographical indications are of a grove, a sakaraklúm, Jupiter Flagius and perhaps a road round something, presumably all only parts of the complex. Finally, it is worth citing the structure of the entries in the Etruscan calendar on the Tabula Capuana: a date (including the Ides and a 'Jovian day', a theonym in the genitive, ilucu = a festival, a verb expressing obligation.133 The similarities may be more than generic, and may suggest substantial continuity between Etruscan and Osean Capua.134 Atella and Calatia See E. Laforgia, Il Museo Archeologico dell'Agro Aíellano (Naples 2007); C. Rescigno, Orizzonti 3 (2002) pp. 99-104, 'Ricerche sull'urbanistica dei centri campani: Calatia'. Suessula A puzzle is represented by the absence of Osean inscriptions in any alphabet from Suessula, near modern Cancello, despite the presence of a substantial body of inscriptions in the Etruscan language. Gianluca Tagliamonte has suggested to us that Suessula was in part superseded by Acerrae, perhaps because of flooding of the River Clanis; Daniela Giampaola argues that Suessula declined as Acerrae urbanised. But we wonder whether the city ceased to exist as a major player at the end of the Classical period rather as a result of the imperial ambitions of Capua or Neapolis. Livy VIII, 14, 1 1 (see commentary of S. Oakley), reports Suessula as being given ciuitas sine suffragio in 338 BC, which shows that it was at any rate then independent and perhaps suggests that it was in the orbit of Capua. On the other hand, the Etruscan name tinQur is attested both at Suessula and at Neapolis and Pithecusae (see above, p. 17), presumably transmitted to Pithecusae via Neapolis. Certainly Suessula seems to be no more than a geographical location during the Hannibalic War; and both it and Acerrae (given ciuitas sine suffragio in 332 BC: Livy VIII, 17, 2; Vellerns 1, 14, 4) are close to epigraphically non-existent in the Roman period.136 132 So also A. Allifae and Phistelia It has often been held that pre-Roman Allifae was not in the plain. But the narrative of Livy, with its three references to ager Allifanus (XXII, 13, 6; 17, 7; XXVI, 9, 2) and its statement that 'Fabius camped above Allifae in a high and fortified position' (XXVI, 18, 5), implies that the city was then on the plain; there are traces of pre-Social War settlement under the modern city; and a pre-Roman settlement has now been identified just 1 km north west of the modern city.145 The Conca d'Oro and Croce S. Maria necropoleis, in use from the seventh century BC to the Hellenistic period, lie to the north of modern Alife;146 and the Conca d'Oro necropolis lies c. 1.5 km from Alife, c. 3 km from Piedimonte d'Alife. Discoveries at Alife are inseparably bound up with the Egg family, who 'excavated' over a period of over 50 years in the necropoleis.147 Their textiles factory at Piedimonte was founded in 1812 by Gian Giacomo Egg, from Ellikon, near Zürich. The articles just mentioned show that the provenance of the fractions of the obol of Phistelia, a type of coinage that does not travel, is exactly the same as that of the fractions of the obol of Allifae; and we suggest that the fiistlus are one half of a double community of Allifae-Phistelia, on the partial model of the case of the Hurietes and Nola, discussed above.150 In any case, we do not think that Phistelia has anything to do with the Plistia/Plistica of Livy IX, 21-2. We would suppose that the absence of Osean texts from Allifae-Phistelia, after prolific coinages c. 400 BC and c. 300 BC, is to be explained by unattested, but massive confiscations of land. Pompei The eituns inscriptions (see Fig. 2) It has been largely accepted since Nissen in 1877, that the inscriptions represent instructions to soldiers during the siege of Pompei during the Social War, a view that we follow: 151 the inscriptions are clearly official, in letters c. 10 cm high, and sometimes painted so far above the ground that a ladder would have been necessary. It is also often held that the instructions are to go along a particular street, described as 149 For the beginning of the excavations see M. Visco, NSc 1880, pp. 82-3; G. Egg, NSc 1881, p. 168-70. 150 For a possible Osean etymology of Allifae, see H. Rix, in P. Poccetti (ed.), L 'onomastica dell'Italia antica (Rome 2009) pp. 497-506, 'Le relazioni tra onomastica e lessico nelle lingue antiche dell'Italia centrale', at p. 505. 151 The aberration is E. Campanile, m L'incidenza dell'antico. Studi in memoria di Ettore Lepore II (Naples 1996) pp. 361-75, 'Le iscrizioni di Pompei attribuite al periodo della Guerra Sociale', who argued that the inscriptions could not relate to the Social War, because the families of the men named in them could not have lived with such records of the great defeat. It would have been easy for Campanile to discover that the inscriptions in question were covered with plaster or stucco already in antiquity. He farther rejects the communis opinio on the grounds that two of the destinations mentioned are not a section of wall; but it is easy to hold that the men from one or more quarters could have been held as a reserve at a central or strategic point.</page><page sequence="51">34 IMAGINES ITALICHE Figure 2 Map of Pompei, with the locations of the eituns inscriptions. Drawn by Frances Morton 'this' street in the text, and identifiable from the position of the text.152 But this interpretation gained ground in a period when it was believed that eituns meant eant or eunto, or iter est, whereas it is now agreed that eituns is the plural of a noun like Latin nouns in -o, -onis,153 even though there is no agreement on whether it means iurati, cons cripti, euocati or whatever. One objection to the hypothesis that amvianud means ' by this street', namely that the good people of Pompei would surely not need to be told which street to use to get to a particular bit of wall or wherever, has been countered with the further hypothesis that the instructions were for defenders from elsewhere, perhaps mercenaries (who would have to be Oscan-reading, and for whom these rather cryptic instructions would be pretty useless), a Pelion upon Ossa for which there is naturally no evidence. But there is a further objection, which is that the inscriptions would be completely useless to defenders from elsewhere at night, except either side of a full moon. On the other hand, Osean amvianud is the ablative of a caique of the Greek word amphodos, and there is a group of inscriptions from Stratonicea and Smyrna in Asia Minor, which are in some cases astonishingly close to our texts and which assign the men of a particular quarter to a particular section of wall, and in four cases out of five describe the quarter as an amphodos. 154 Instructions as to which section of wall, or mustering point in the town, was the destination of the men of a particular quarter, to be read and absorbed as soon as they were put up, make sense also at Pompei, and lead us to reject the alternative interpretation of the texts 152 I. Poccetti, Ath 66 (1988) pp. 303-28, 'Riflessi di strutture di fortificazioni nell'epigrafia italica'; F. Antonini, as describing - but it is not clear for what purpose - areas of the town, lying between the inscriptions and the sections of wall or other places mentioned in the inscriptions.155 Alphabetaria Garrucci's mendacity would in any case make him suspect as a witness to the alphabetaria on the outside of the west wall of the Casa del Fauno: he claims that 'M. Mommsen a inséré dans le Bulletin archéologique de Naples (an. IV, p. 17) trois lettres d'un alphabet osque copié par lui, je ne sais dans quel endroit de Pompei',156 when Mommsen had in fact localised the letters perfectly correctly, both in Bullettìno Archeologico Napoletano 4 (1845-6) pp. 113-18, 'Iscrizioni osche nuove o corrette', at p. 117: 'sul muro esteriore della casa del Fauno nel vicoletto parallelo alla strada di Mercurio', and in Unteritalische Dialekten (1850) p. 188: 'auf der Aussenwand der casa del Fauno ... in dem Gässchen, das der strada di Mercurio parallel geht'. In our view, the only reliable source for these graffiti is Fiorelli, Monumenta Epigraphica Pompeiana ; and there were never more than four. In order to understand the relationship between Fiorelli and Garrucci, we need some chronology. As he makes clear in the preface to the first edition, Fiorelli began work about nine years before 30 December 1854, the date of the preface, say the beginning of 1846. The first edition bears the date 1854, the second edition, without the magnificent plates, but with the texts of the inscriptions inserted in the letterpress, bears the date 1856. Garrucci was in exile after 1853; his first edition bears the date 1854, his second edition the date 1856. We do not know when Garrucci began work, but Fiorelli's first edition gives cross- references between his numbers and those of Garrucci's first edition, and it is evident that the two authors were in communication with each other. It is also evident both that the drawings of the four alphabetaria published by Garrucci were furnished to him by Fiorelli and that Garrucci was unconcerned to represent their actual size: Fiorelli's no. 9 is very large indeed, compared with the other three, whereas Garrucci's no. 2 represents it as of about the same size; and even Fiorelli's nos. 10-12 are carefully distinguished in size, whereas the same is not true for Garrucci's nos. 1 and 3-4. It is furthermore evident that the drawings were copied free hand for Garrucci, not mechanically reproduced. For all these reasons, we have no trust whatsoever in the way Garrucci represented the final letters of the four alphabetaria. Obviously, we cannot be absolutely certain which of the four had been seen by Mommsen, but we give reasons in the entry concerned for believing it to be Fiorelli's no. 9 (Pompei 74). Zvetaieff saw two alphabetaria, his pl. XVII, 20-21, of which no. 20 is again very large indeed, and is evidently Fiorelli's no. 9; he also decided to insert drawings of three alphabetaria that he believed he could not see, and claims to reproduce his nos. 22, 23, 24, from Fiorelli; the drawings, however, are in fact taken not from Fiorelli, but from Garrucci. We regard it as in the highest degree unlikely that a graffito had become visible to Zvetaieff that was not visible to Fiorelli, and in fact it is clear that Zvetaieffs no. 21 is the same as Fiorelli's no. 1 1, with the largest letters out of nos. 10-12 and with identical spacing; and that Zvetaieff has been misled by the garbled version of Fiorelli's no. 11, that appears as Garrucci's no. 3 = Zvetaieffs no. 23. We therefore offer the following concordance: F., pl. X, no. 9 = G., pl. I, no. 2 = Z., pl. XVII, no. 20 = Ve. 69, d = Antonini 9 = ST Po 101 = Pompei 74 F. no. 10 = G. no. 4 = Z. no. 24 = Ve. 69, h = Antonini 5 = ST Po 91 = Pompei 76 F. no. 1 1 = G. no. 3 = Z. nos. 23 + 21 = Ve. 69, g + e = Antonini 7 + 8 = S7To 99 + 100 = Pompei 75 F. no. 12 = G. no. 1 = Z. no. 22 = Ve. 69, f = Antonini 6 = STPo 98 = Pompei 77 155 Quaderni di Studi Pompeiani 1 (2007) pp. 47-1 13, 'Contributi pompeiani', at pp. 47-57. 156 Graffiti (1856) p. 79.</page><page sequence="53">36 IMAGINES ITALICHE On an unrelated linguistic matter, it has been held that at Pompei, in contrast to the rest of the Oscan- using world, final -m is often omitted;157 but R. Antonini has shown that this is not in fact the case.158 Tile stamps In organising the abundant stamps from Pompei, we have within individual entries listed pieces as far as possible in the order of their discovery; of the different entries, we have placed first those stamps from a building in the area of the Large Theatre and the Temple of Isis, probably the Temple of Isis itself (late Hellenistic), the Basilica (c. 125-100 BC) and the Casa del Fauno (Phase 2: c. 125-100 BC, accompanied by re-decoration of the whole).159 This gives us an approximate date for Pompei 99, 100, 101, and perhaps 104 and 118; we assign other stamps to a similar date. Concentrations from Reg. I, 9, 5; I, 10, 7; and I, 14, 2, provide no useable chronological information; and we have grouped stamps in intaglio, in relief in a rectangular frame, in intaglio in a semicircular frame, in relief in a semicircular frame. A number of stamps are clearly in the nominative, and we have treated this as the default case, also for stamps from other sites, 'So-and-so (made this)'. The stamps d, .d.t, and v. bla occur also on corner tegulae; and many of the stamps ni. veela come from the Villa della Pisanella. But in general it is clear that many of the tiles now known had been broken and re-used in antiquity, and the context of their discovery is not informative. For possible Pompeian funerary inscriptions, see p. 15,'n. 65 on the Paeligni. Osean to Latin How long did Osean survive at Pompei?160 Let us return to the alphabetaria on both the west and the east walls of the House of the Faun, in both cases apparently near the road that runs in front of the house, in the pavement of which is the Latin inscription HAVE (see below). The graffiti have long gone the way of most graffiti on plaster discovered in Pompei in the nineteenth century, i.e., the plaster has fallen off the wall; and dating is impossible. We want to emphasise one point and pose a series of questions. The point to emphasise is that as evidence for the use of Osean, the graffiti are not strong: there is only one complete alphabet, in awful writing, whereas the rest break off after four or five letters. The questions are: the alphabetaria have generally been linked with the Samnite character of the house; but would anyone who is English suppose that the appearance of a graffito in Welsh on the outside of their house revealed anything about them? Are the graffiti there because there was at some point a school behind the house, whose pupils flowed homewards down the lanes on either side of the house? Or is there some reason why graffiti there of the Oscan-using period survived, whereas similar quantities elsewhere did not? Much has been made of two different examples of the use of Osean and Latin in close proximity, the Latin inscription HAVE set in the mosaic pavement in front of the Casa del Fauno, and a supposed bilingual electoral programma of the period of the Pompeian municipium between the Social War and the Sullan colony. The mosaic inscription is generally taken as reflecting the wishes of the owners of the house, which is of course one of the grandest of the Samnite period of Pompei, and it is dated to the second century BC by Alison Cooley, between the Social War and the Sullan colony by Fausto Zevi, in both cases without 157 Coleman (n. 1 18) p. 35. 158 Antonini (n. 155) pp. 57-73; see also Pompei 31. 159 See F. Pesando &amp; M. P. Guidobaldi, Pompei, Oplontis, Ercolano, Stabiae (Rome &amp; Bari 2006) p. 54; pp. 192-93. 160 A. Landi, in A. de Franciscis (pres.), La regione sotterrata dal Vesuvio (Naples 1982) pp. 211-27, 'Lingue in contatto e circolazione sociale a Pompei', discusses almost entirely Latin and Greek, without attention to chronology or awareness of parallels; the claim, p. 212, that 'l'osco sopravisse a Pompei, come risulta dai graffiti scoperti sui muri la cui decorazione è dell'ultimo stile', is made without documentation, and is taken over by A. Cooley, in ead. (ed.), Becoming Roman, Writing Latin? (. JRA Supp. 48, 2002) pp. 77-86, 'The survival of Osean in Roman Pompeii', at p. 84; the claim is probably based on Vetter 62 and 60, discussed below.</page><page sequence="54">INTRODUCTION 37 Figure 3 Drawing by Theodor Mommsen of the arrangement of the dipinti and graffiti on the Casa di Francesco II ' ' .* "/ 2 3 /Vļ - M AR/ l/M AíDřA^f I - " s . i íkô-VOS &lt; LICIV I ' unA"** s CAW/no.lw U, ' numil M'-- m mié 'TSMítiV U, &lt;3 _ 3B -3 p . LA' '• " / / - " &gt; % .</page><page sequence="55">38 IMAGINES ITALICS argument and without discussion of the archaeology.161 It is of course the case that the Tabula Heracleensis documents for a later age the obligation, in a Roman community, on property owners to maintain half the road (and presumably also the sidewalk) in front of their properties, an obligation archaeologically attested at Fregellae, before 125 BC; but it is clear that the mosaic pavement in fact butts up against the threshold of the Casa del Fauno, and could be of any date. As for the bilingual electoral programma, which is supposed to represent canvassing for a Ma. Herennius as Illluir, in both Osean and Latin, we depend on Garrucci for our information;162 well, not quite. There remain traces of the Osean text, Pompei 31, but none of the Latin. Garrucci was undoubtedly quite a card, a Jesuit, but exiled from the Kingdom of Naples on suspicion of subversion in 1853, as it tottered to its end. But, as we have seen, when he published the alphabetaria on the west wall of the Casa del Fauno, he mentioned one seen by Mommsen and claimed that Mommsen had not located it, when Mommsen had in fact done so twice, with great precision. We can no longer check what Garrucci claimed to see of the Latin element of the programma, but within a few years of when he claimed to see the two inscriptions, they were checked by Carl Zangemeister, who recorded in CIL IV, p. 5, no. 48, in respect of the Latin text supposedly above the Osean one 'frustra saepius quaesivi', 'I have very often looked without success'. There can be little doubt that Garrucci, carelessly or malevolently, caused a suitable Latin inscription to migrate in his notes next to the Osean one. The drawing made by Mommsen remains the best representation of the layout of the texts (see Fig. 3). There is another feature of the so-called Osean programmata that is worth mentioning: they are in letters of similar size to the eituns inscriptions, and - insofar as we have been able to check - are sometimes, like the eituns inscriptions, at a height at which they would have had to be painted by someone standing on a ladder. We are fairly certain that they are not programmata; but they are surely official, like the eituns inscriptions, perhaps - like those - announcements to the citizen body, presumably made by IHIuiri of the municipium after the enfranchisement of the city and before the foundation of the Sullan colony. 163 The material on which the Osean texts of Pompei are inscribed is also important, in the context of chronology: Rosalba Antonini, who has done more than anyone else to make sense of the corpus of material, stated that the inscription recording the paving of the Via Sarina (Pompei 12) and the building inscription from the Temple of Apollo (Pompei 25) were of marble,164 without explicitly refuting Antonio Sogliano, who had stated on the basis of his archaeological knowledge that marble was not used for inscriptions in Oscan-speaking Pompei.165 Sogliano was right. But there is one inscription on marble from Pompei, the sun- dial (Pompei 21); the marble, along with the idea, was probably imported from the Greek world. Pompei 7 shows that some walls had been plastered before the Social War, and invalidates the suggestion of R. Antonini, that texts on plaster might be close to the eruption of AD 79. 166 Looking at the Osean epigraphy of Pompei as a whole, there are a couple of cases where an Osean text was certainly or probably replaced by a Latin text: the inscription of L. Mummius, in the precinct of the Temple of Apollo (Pompei 1), and the inscription of the mensa ponderaría in the Forum (Pompei 27). There are also a handful of cases where an Osean inscription certainly or probably remained in situ down to AD 79: the mosaic inscription at the entrance to the Temple of Apollo, the sundial inscription, and the roadmakers' inscription (Pompei 13). In the case of the first, its removal would have required major work; 161 Cooley (n. 160) p. 83; F. Zevi, in Studi sull'Italia dei Sanniti (Rome 2000) pp. 1 18-37, 'Pompei: Casa del Fauno', at p. 120. 162 Followed by Cooley (n. 160) p. 82; Adams (n. 12) p. 146. 163 See M. H. Crawford, in C. Berrendonner et al., (edd.), Le Quotidien municipal (Clermont-Ferrand 2008) pp. 171-81, 'The IlIIuiri of Pompei'; we do not understand why A. Willi, Gymnasium 116 (2009) pp. 573-98, 'Opfer des Lateinischen: zum Sprachtod in Altitalien', thinks that the office of Illluir lasted at Pompei till c. 50 BC 164 SE 45 (1977) pp. 324-6 and 327; whence Cooley (n. 160) p. 78. 165 A. Sogliano, Il foro di Pompei (Mem.Acc.Lincei, Classe di scienze morali, ser. 6, vol. 1, 1925, pp. 221-72) at p. 250. 166 (n. 155) p. 103, n. 189.</page><page sequence="56">INTRODUCTION 39 in the case of the second, the legibility of the record of erection of the sundial was irrelevant to its useability; in the case of the last, it could not have been removed without digging up the road, and the stone was probably very handy as a mounting block. Overall, however, what is striking is the number of monumental Osean inscriptions found re-used. Much has been made of the supposition that the Osean inscription from the so-called Samnite palaestra was found attached to the wall re-built when the Temple of Isis was extended after AD 63, and it has even been argued that it was re-inscribed then (Pompei 24). But the very first reports of its finding suggest rather that it was found on the floor. It might of course have fallen from the wall; but if that is so, it may simply have been used to build the wall; or it may have been used to make the floor. Does this seem far-fetched? Let us look at the inscription from the Porta di Nola, stolen from the site shortly after its discovery and now in the British Museum (Pompei 8). It is generally supposed that the inscription is the building inscription of the gate; but one can still see the gap in the cement out of which it was taken, and the inscription was as broken when it was placed there as it is now. It was simply building material, put in that position to cover a crack, before the whole gate was covered with stucco. The inscription of v. sadiriis. v (Pompei 18), so far from being part of a base with multiple statues, on display in AD 79, perhaps in Room 49 of the Casa del Fauno, is the bottom half of a single base.167 Of the other Osean inscriptions from the Casa del Fauno, Pompei 36 was found badly damaged in the 'atrio tuscanico', and the two halves of Pompei 20 were found separately, half in the 'atrio tuscanico', half elsewhere; all three inscriptions should be regarded as building material.168 Speculations about links between the nomen sadiriis and the Greek word Saturos, and about the statue after which the Casa del Fauno is named being a Satyr, not a Faun, have no point from which to start. The inscription relating to the Via Sarina was found in four fragments in three widely separated places (Pompei 12). A building inscription of two quaestors was found dumped in a cupboard under the stairs in the Casa di Cornelio Rufo (Pompei 19). 169 Yet another inscription had been cut into two halves, which were then used as the bases of the hinges of a double door (Pompei 42). It is as if the public memory of the Samnite city had been systematically cancelled. Finally, Vetter 62 = Pompei 146 and Vetter 60 = Pompei 147, and the notorious Osean graffito from the brothel (Pompei 46). We have the very gravest doubts as to whether the first two are either Osean or ancient. The graffito from the brothel is indeed Flavian; but it is again necessary to look at the Latin, as well as the Greek graffiti. The room in which the Osean graffito is to be found contains over 120 in Latin, one of which may be the Latin equivalent of the Osean name, and 8 in Greek: might our author be the last surviving Oscan-writing Samnite from up in the hills, down in Pompei for a night on the town? What one can see in the evidence, however, is traces of people playing with language and script. The Greek alphabets drawn from right to left belong in this category,170 also a small group of texts which we place near the end of the corpus for Pompei (Pompei 138-44). Herculaneum The pattern of language at Herculaneum, next door to Neapolis, is strikingly different from that at Pompei: M. della Corte has 854 entries for 1929-1941 at Herculaneum, some double, of which only 2 are Osean (Herculaneum 2-3), over 30 are Greek. Stabiae Appian, BC 1, 42, 186, where C. Papius captures Stabiae, Mineruium and Salernum, is a way of saying that he captured the whole of the Sorrento peninsula as far as Punta della Campanella, and does not imply that 167 Contra F. I.</page><page sequence="57">40 IMAGINES IT ALICE Stabiae was an independent community, rather than belonging as it did later to Nuceria Alfaterna; but the existence of a Via Stabiana outside Pompei (Pompei 13) perhaps favours the independence of Stabiae. Nola See M. Bonghi Jovino &amp; R. Donceel, La necropoli di Nola preromana (Naples 1969); also above, p. 20. It is striking, given the treaty between Abella and Nola (Abella 1), regulating the management of a shrine on their shared boundary, that three of the public inscriptions of Nola and Abella relate to the demarcation of boundaries. The only meddix attested at Nola is described as involved in the management of tithes; we imagine that this presupposes the existence of a meddix tuticus. Nola 4 appears to show multiple tenures of the office of meddix deketasis and the same man as both meddix deketasis and quaestor; but we think rather that ex-magistrates continued to characterise themselves with the title of their magistracy after demitting it, with the result that Nola 3 does not show two meddices in office together. Abella See in general T. Cinquantaquattro, AIONArch, n.s., 7 (2000) pp. 61-85, 'Abella, un insediamento della mesogaia campana: note di topografia'. Although the office of meddix is not explicitly attested at Abella, and it is the quaestor who heads the delegation that concludes the agreement with Nola (Abella 1) and who takes the initiative in Abella 2 and probably Abella 3, the recently discovered building inscription (Abella 3) is intelligible only on the assumption that there was an eponymous magistrate, presumably a meddix {tuticus). The man who appears as quaestor in all three texts may be the same person. Fratte di Salerno Fratte di Salerno is the Singapore of the ancient world, its mix of alphabets, languages and cultures perhaps unparalleled in ancient Italy, and emerging with dramatic clarity from G. Colonna' s 1990 publication of the Etruscan inscriptions found there.171 It may be that a measure of quantity in use there is revealed by the graffito on the body of a bucchero oenochoe of 525-500 BC, OOOOIII.172 VOLSCI Recent discoveries have revealed the presence of men with Italic names and of the Osean alphabet in the lower Liris valley, between Casinum and Aquinum (Casinum 1, Aquinum 2-5); 173 and it now seems that the Volsci living east of the Sacco-Liri valleys, while maintaining the characteristic Volscian institutional structure of the triple aedileship, adopted the Osean language and alphabet, perhaps in the context of Samnite expansion. This emerges also from a group of texts, from the upper Sangro valley, that in our view 171 In G. Greco &amp; A. Pontrandolfo (edd.), Fratte. Colonna (n. 171) p. 305, no. 6; for 0 = 10, see A. Johnston, Trademarks on Greek Vases (Warminster 1979) p. 31, withn. 18. 173 For a general account see A. M. Reggiani, in Aeimnestos: miscellanea di studi per Mauro Cristofani (Florence 2005) pp. 297-305, 'La media Valle del Liri fra Volsci e Sanniti', optimistic about the possibility of identifying places mentioned by Livy and naive over the relationship between archaeology and ethnicity.</page><page sequence="58">INTRODUCTION 41 clearly belong to Volscian Atina (Atina l).174 It is this alphabetic choice, alongside institutional continuity, that leads us to place the inscriptions of the Volsci here, between Campania, where the Osean alphabet was invented, and Samnium, the area of its principal diffusion. SAMNIVM This is not the place to rehearse the abundant literary evidence for the Roman view of Samnium; rather we concentrate on some problematic implications of the material presented in the Corpus.175 The first problem is the suggestion of La Regina that a unitary Samnite state, consisting at the very least of the communities of the Pentri, survived down to the Social War.176 Such a state may have existed down to 290 or 272 BC, but what we (think we) know of it is unaffected by the documentation to be found in the much later inscriptions. Leaving aside the improbability that the Romans would have allowed such a state to survive, the principal difficulty is the complete lack of uniformity between the institutional practices documented by the epigraphic practice of the different Pentrian communities, Bouianum, Saepinum, Teruentum/Pietrabbondante and Fagifulae.177 Before turning to that practice, however, we note that in Livy XXII, 24, 11, Numerius Decitius is thought of as belonging to Bouianum, but has no juridical relationship to an entity called Samnium, which merely provides the context in which he excels in birth and wealth: compare Teruentum 8-10. Turning to the epigraphy, (1) there is no case of a individual clearly documented in more than one of the Pentrian communities just named; (2) the tiles which we regard as tiles mentioning the meddices tutici of Bouianum (see below, p. 42), not of a Samnite state, are found almost exclusively in the territory of Bouianum, a few just down the road at Saepinum, none at Pietrabbondante; (3) the institutional practice in the territory of Saepinum and at Pietrabbondante is completely different from that at Bouianum, with no mention of meddices tutici on tiles and other items of clay, but the ethnic of Saepinum in the case of the former (Saepinum 1), tuv in the case of the latter.178 In our view, Saepinum was one city, with its own institutions, Bouianum another, Teruentum another, Fagifulae another. They are cities with in addition settlements and sanctuaries in their territories, attracting substantial investment, Collemaggiore and Santa Croce del Sannio in the territory of Saepinum; Campochiaro and perhaps Selva del Campo on the plain below, Colle d'Anchise (with a dedication as well as stamps of meddices), Macchiagodena, Molise, Roccaspromonte in the territory of Bouianum; Colle Vernone, Capracotta, Agnone, Macchielunghe, S. Bernardino, Vastogirardi, Schiavi, in the territory of Teruentum, in addition to the great sanctuary of Pietrabbondante; and Gildone and S. Giovanni in Galdo, in the territory of Fagifulae.179 It remains true, of course, that archaeological exploration has progressed far enough for us to be certain that the sanctuary at Pietrabbondante far outstripped the others in size and wealth. But we do not think this 174 See F. M. Cifarelli &amp; S. Gatti, Orizzonti 7 (2006) pp. 23-48, 'I Volsci: una nuova prospettiva', for homogeneity and continuity in material culture, in the orientalising, archaic and early classical periods, from Sora to Frusino and Interamna, and in the Melfa and Rapido valleys. For an interpretative model, we should perhaps turn rather to the Greek world, where the sanctuary of Olympia was open to all the Hellenes, but controlled by Elis. Why should a rather unimportant city like Teruentum have the most important sanctuary of the Samnites in its territory? One might as well ask the same question of Elis.180 Two ancillary remarks: we imagine that periodic markets always formed a large element in the finance of the sanctuary at Pietrabbondante, a view supported by the range of coinages found there; and we think that it continued in existence, albeit on a reduced scale, down to the time of Constantine.181 Caudium What had until recently been a linguistic blank, now, as a result largely of information generously supplied by Carlo Franciosi, sports three Italic texts (Caudium 1-3). Hirpini In the southern part of the territory of the Hirpini, round Compsa, there is an almost complete dearth of texts in Osean. (26-8), and those with name, m.t., name (Bouianum 1-3). We are unconvinced by the suggestion of M.P. Marchese, that the first variety offers a dating formula, but that the second variety identifies the man as the 'esecutore' of the building or whatever in question, since the distinction would be cryptic, to say the least.186 The abbreviation mz (Bouianum 21) shows in any case that at least that particular meddix tuticus was recorded in the nominative; if all were, the texts of the first two varieties must represent a sentence, 'The meddix tuticus, so-and-so, (???)', or 'So-and-so, the meddix tuticus, (???)'. A possible, if partial, parallel is provided by a group of tiles from Megalopolis, which state either (1) that Philopoimen had offered the tiles to Zeus, or (2) that the tiles had been made by a certain Homilos, that they were public, and that Polybios had offered them, or (3) that Aristion had offered the tiles, that they were public, and that they had been made while Thraseas was agonothete.187 A. La Regina suggested that in the third variety of stamps from Bouianum both names are those of magistrates, with the second name being that of a suffect. But if the first meddix tuticus had died, we do not see why he should be mentioned at all. Rather we think that the two names record maker (in the genitive) and magistrate. It remains uncertain, however, whether the information about the magistrate is to be completed '(was in office)' or '(offered)'. The fact that the tiles all come from a single community (see above) recalls the world after 90 BC, where a significant series of dated tile stamps likewise comes from a single community, namely Veleia.188 We know that weathered tiles were more valuable than new tiles,189 and wonder whether in two communities the powers-that-be had the bright idea of making it easy to be sure that tiles had gone through a winter. In contrast to the names on the tiles from Megalopolis, our names are heavily abbreviated, and we doubt that the inscriptions record the munificence of the men concerned; we prefer to suppose that the purpose of the stamp was to identify the year in which the tile was produced: '... so-and-so was in office'. We have in any case restored the names of all magistrates on tile stamps in the nominative. A number of stamps are too fragmentary to be assigned: S. Capini, SE 46 (1978) p. 427, no. 43 - G, 15 (Poccetti 79); p. 431, no. 52 - G, 24; p. 432, no. 55 - G, 27 (all beginning m.t. [-?-], all from Campochiaro, loc. Civitella); G. de Benedittis &amp; V. Ceglia, Prima dei Sanniti (Campobasso 2005) p. 65, no. 174 (m.t.[-7- ]), 'agro di Campochiaro, inv. BJ/D/152'; G. de Benedittis, in G. We have supposed that graffiti on pots from Campochiaro are dedications and therefore restored them as nominatives, also on vases from other sanctuaries. Help in delimiting the territory of Bouianum comes from an inscription seen and not copied, but certainly in Osean (Bouianum 117): it was found at S. Angelo in Grotte, which, given the absence of any but Latin inscriptions from Latin colonies, must belong to Bouianum, not Aesernia. Daniele, in Ruggiero, p. 646). The four areas under exploration from 03/10/1857 onwards, as reported by Antonio Imparato to Gaetano Genovese (there are résumés of progress at pp. 643-5, 647-9, 649-50), are: (1) 'verso il lato occidentale [of contrada Calcatello] sotto la falda del monte detto Saraceno a poca distanza dal Teatro, per scoprire gli avanzi apparenti di un edificio pubblico [Tempie A] precedentemente in parte riconosciuto nel sottoposto sentiero per Pietrabbondante' (Ruggiero, pp. 616, 617 X 2, 619, 629 [Genovese: it was necessary 'compiere lo scavo del pubblico edificio ricco d'iscrizioni osche molto pregevoli a fin di poterne rintracciare altre'], 630 x 2, 631, 632 x 2, 633 x 2, 634 x 2, 635, 636 x 2, 637 [if 'nel terzo dei sopraccennati cavamenti ...' is a mistake for 'nel primo ...'], 638, 639); (2) 'un fosso lungo circa pai. 60 e largo 6 [= c. 15.85 x 1.585 m] per rintracciare oggetti antichi e il sepolcreto ... [distant] dal primo [cavamente] circa pai. 150 [= c. 39.60 m]' (Ruggiero, pp. 616, 617, 619), in fact on the 'spianata che lo precedeva', i.e., was in front of it (p. 635); (3) 'un terzo punto discosto dal secondo [cavamente] circa pai. 50 [= c. 13.20 m] verso occidente ove si appalesa altro basamento di edificio' (Ruggiero, pp. 617, 618, 619 [given that (1) = Temple A and that (2) was in front of Temple A, (3) may be part of the portico between Temple A and the theatre]); (4) 'altro punto per provare la scoperta di qualche sepolcreto verso la china della collina, e proprio circa pai. 50 [= c. 13.20 m] lontano da altro sentiero pubblico ad oriente' [thus closer to Pietrabbondante] (Ruggiero, pp. 618, 619, 629 [Genovese: it was necessary 'tentare la scoverta del sepolcreto sannitico, pria nella falda meridionale della costa sul cui dorso io scopriva il sepolcreto romano, di poi in prossimità delle vestigia del teatro']). From 01/06/1858, there began: (5) '... il disterro del Teatro [= outside]' (Ruggiero, pp. 630 x 2, 631, 632, 633 x 3, 634 x 2, 635, 636 x 2, 637, 638) (6) '... uno scavo per l'apertura di un fosso ove si suppone poter esistere l'antico sepolcreto ... verso la china del monte Saraceno e dista dal Teatro suddetto andando verso oriente pai. 250 circa [= c. 66.00 m]' (Ruggiero, pp. 630 x 2, 631, 632 x 2, 633 x 2, 634 x 3, 636, 637 x 2, 638, 639 x 4, 640) (7) work at an '... altro sito lontano dal punto principale dell'indicato edificio pubblico, salendo verso occidente pai. 200 [= c. 52.80 m]' (Ruggiero, pp. 630 x 2, 632 x 2) (8) work '... nel sito interno del Teatro' (Ruggiero, pp. 632, 633 x 2, 634 x 3, 636 x 2, 637 x 2, 638 x 3, 639 x 5, 640 x 2) On 17/06/1858, there was observed: (9) 'A mezzogiorno ed alla distanza di circa pai. 700 [= c. 184.80 m] dal Teatro ... altri ruderi di antiche costruzioni sepolte' (Ruggiero, p. 636). On 07/10/1857, there appeared in (1) (Ruggiero, pp. 618-19) four fragments of Zvetaieff 16 = ST Sa 21 = Teruentum 5 and the two fragments of Zvetaieff 19 = ST Sa 5 = Teruentum 11, the six together taken by</page><page sequence="62">INTRODUCTION 45 Figure 4 Plan of the sanctuary at Pietrabbondante: the plan of Temple A to the right of the modern plan is that of R. Delbrueck, MDAIR 18 (1903) p. 156. Drawn by Frances Morton Imparato as a single inscription (despite different letter heights for the four and the two). On 14/10/1857 and 16/10/1857, the two remaining fragments of Zvetaieff 16 = ST Sa 21 = Teruentum 5 appeared; they are the last but one and the last to the left respectively, and it looks very much as if the excavators were working from east to west and found the fragments in order as they had fallen (the first four fragments are apparently recorded in order of size) (Ruggiero, p. 620). On 17/10/1857, the three fragments to survive of Zvetaieff 18 = ST Sa 3 = Teruentum 4 appeared, evidently copied by Imparato from the back of a paper rubbing (Ruggiero, p. 620). Between 01 and 07/11/1857, Zvetaieff 17 = ST Sa 4 = Teruentum 8 appeared (Ruggiero, p. 622); and between 08 and 14/11/1857, Zvetaieff 21 = ST Sa 16 = Teruentum 9 appeared (Ruggiero, p. 625). Between 15 and 21/1 1/1857, the now missing piece of ST Sa 3 = Teruentum 4 with [-?-]ft/r[-?-] appeared. None of the discoveries between 07/10/1857 and 14/11/1857 is assigned to a particular location; but it is likely that ST Sa 2 1 = Teruentum 5 was lifted from right to left some distance from the front of the temple, along with ST Sa 5 = Teruentum 11; that since the latter is unlikely to have stood on the front of the temple, it will have stood on a base to its front; that ST Sa 3 = Teruentum 4 lay closer to the temple than ST Sa 21 = Teruentum 5; and that since ST Sa 4, 16 and 19 = Teruentum 8-10, which are probably the same text, are unlikely to have stood on the front of the temple, they will have stood on a base to its front. ST Sa 3 and Sa 21 = Teruentum 4-5 probably form the upper and lower members respectively of the entablature of the façade, with a Doric frieze between them, decorated on the sides of the temple with lion- head spouts; and one may suppose that, when the temple collapsed, the columns fell outwards, taking Teruentum 4 further out than Teruentum 5. There survive the foundations of two altars in front of the temple, one in the centre and one to the left, taken as statue bases in the nineteenth century, and ST Sa 5 = Teruentum 11 may have stood on the central one of these. ST Sa 4, 16 and 19 = Teruentum 8-10 are clearly the facing of a base or a podium, and may be assigned to the altar to the left. (The block currently inside the foundation in the centre does not belong.) No trace was found of a central staircase onto the podium, that part having collapsed before the excavations began; but it is likely that there was one, and that it was replaced by the staircase at the left-hand</page><page sequence="63">46 IMAGINES ITALICS side, since the upper moulding of the podium was cut and the blocks forming the side of the podium re- organised, to accommodate it.190 Phase I of the sanctuary belongs to the fourth century BC, and consisted of a square enclosure 55 x 55 m = 200 x 200 Osean feet; the same dimensions are attributed to the tent in which was sworn the oath of loyalty before the battle of Aquilonia (Livy X, 38, 5-13). The fact that the square was not preserved in later re-structurings of the sanctuary of Pietrabbondante suggests that little significance should be attributed to the coincidence. 191 Phase II belongs to the third century BC, and consisted of an Ionic temple, elements of which have been found, but whose precise location within the enclosure is unknown: it will have been between the later temple (Temple B) and the theatre. Phase III belongs to c. 180 BC, and consists of a small temple (Temple A) to the east of the enclosure, in due course linked to the area of the enclosure by a portico; this is the temple that was excavated in the nineteenth century. Phase IV belongs to the second century: the Ionic temple had apparently been destroyed during the Hannibalic War, and was replaced in due course by the theatre, excavated in the nineteenth century, with a large temple above and behind (Temple B), excavated from 1959 onwards. The front of the podium had collapsed at some point, and two of the blocks carrying the building inscription on the podium had been built into the church of S. Maria in Pietrabbondante, before the point at which exploration of the site began. 60 feet of the podium, presumably the front 60 feet given the location of the inscription, were apparently built by C. Statius Claras (Teruentum 12), conventionally identified with the Statius of Appian, BC IV, 102, and in consequence dated to the 90s BC. The only full publication of the excavation of the podium is that of the coins by Strazzulla;192 and, without exception, the coins from those areas of the podium undisturbed by later collapse are of the third century BC (nos. 1-12; no. 61 probably also belongs to this phase). In other words, at any rate part of the podium, perhaps along with a cella, was built at about the same time as Temple A; and the identification between the Statius of the inscription and the Statius of Appian should not be made unquestioningly. It can however be maintained on the hypothesis that the podium was left at half of its eventual size between the early and late second century BC, with or without a cella (Strazzulla, no. 13, late third century BC, is from an early level below where the steps were built); and this fits with the fact that Statius (Claras) probably claims to have built 60 feet of the podium: 60 Osean feet = 16.5 m is in fact the length of the pronaos. It was then at this point that the enlarged podium was encased in new moulding. Two early porticos downhill from Temple B are attributed to Phase III, and dated by the numismatic evidence to the early second century BC (Strazzulla, nos. 156, 158, 159, 160; no. 157 is perhaps a coin with Head of Dionysus r. / Panther r. ( HNItaly (2001) 2667-72), but is a surface find); they appear to have been replaced by the theatre. This is conventionally, and probably rightly, dated to the end of the second century BC (Strazzulla, no. 166, is a denarius of M. Aburius Geminus ( RRC 250/1), with c. 25 years' wear); the location of Temple B will have been determined in relation to the two early porticos, that precede the theatre. Temple B in its final, extended, form is clearly later than the theatre. For the two inscribed blocks built into the church, see Teruentum 12; they are described by Caraba as 'in due diversi lati della chiesa'; by Mommsen as 'murati nella chiesa'.193 The third inscribed block was 190 M.-J. Pliny, NH III, 107 in our view attests Iuuanum and Cluuiae as belonging to the Carricini: Caretini (= Carricini ) supernates (= towards the Adriatic) &lt;Cluuienses&gt; et infernates (= towards the Tyrrhenian) Iuuanenses ; we do not regard as cogent A. La Regina's reasons for rejecting the testimony of Ptolemy III, 1, 57, 196 that Aufidena also belonged to the Carricini and, if it is accepted, that would imply the attribution of much of the valley to them. For the fantastic notion that the 'alphabet of Nocera' is attested at Aufidena, see above, p. 16, n. 72. Two texts without a home A small bronze tablet found at Pescara, with a text in the Osean alphabet, could of course in theory have been made anywhere in the area of use of the alphabet in question and then been lost at the harbour of Teate Marrucinorum (Vestini. Paeligni, Marrucini 1); but a weight with frentiais, that implies a place called *Frentiae (*Frentiae 1), is more problematic. There is no reason to associate the text with the Frentani; rather it recalls a group probably called the Frenetes near Barisciano, and the Pagus Frent[-?-] at Peltuinum, both in the territory of the Vestini.197 We wonder whether both texts might be the product of itinerant craftsmen accustomed to the Osean alphabet, and in any case place them together by themselves, to draw attention to the problem they pose. Frentani A substantial problem is posed by the coins with frentrei, which imply an otherwise unknown place called *Frentrum (*Frentrum 1; for frentiais see above). The coins are found in the northern half of the territory of the Frentani, including 4 from Monte Pallano, which was perhaps the location of a settlement known as *Frentrum. Venusia The inscriptions in the Osean alphabet are all on portable objects, and were not in our view made there; we obviously exclude the coinage of the Latin colony. L VC ANIA, BRETTII, MESSANA For the irretrievable fragility of the notion that there was a southern 'pre-Samnite' koine, see above, p. 16. We have briefly discussed the origins of the use of a Greek alphabet to write Osean in the territories of the Lucani and further south (see above, pp. 16-17); in the Hellenistic period, the only script in use was the Ionic, and for economy of effort, from this point onwards in the Corpus, Greek alphabet means Greek alphabet, Ionic script, four-barred sigma unless otherwise stated (for the letter / see below). Broadly speaking in the same period as the reform of the Osean alphabet, the Oscan-speaking users of the Ionic script in the south adopted the long vowel eta, with the consequence that 1-, not H, became the sign for A.203 We share the view of del Tutto Palma that the two reforms are independent of each other.204 It was long supposed, following Lejeune, that southern Osean used, like Umbrian, the order praenomen, filiation, nomen;205 but the general statement has been refuted by La Regina.206 For two early and one late text, all problematic, see Capua 35, Surrentum 2 and Laos 2; there is also an apparent occurrence on Delos of the order praenomen, filiation, nomen (see the Appendix, under Cumae). LVCANIA207 As we have seen, when Mommsen wrote Oskische Studien, he knew of no Osean inscriptions in the Greek alphabet; that we now know of getting on for a hundred is the result not so much of slow accumulation over the years, but of the inspired, deliberate exploration, by Dinu Adamesteanu and his successors, of the sanctuary of Mefitis at Macchia di Rossano.208 To it we come in a moment; but it is first necessary to consider the coinage of the Lucani. This has long been recognised as belonging to the period of the Hannibalic War; but apart from a diminutive issue of Punic half-shekels produced by a group of Lucanians at Metapontum (Lucani 1, no. 1), the coinage is in our view not in any real sense a coinage of the Lucani at all. Since in the case of all three groups only a part of the Brettian series is represented in the Lucanian series, it is clear that it is the latter that borrows from the former; the one Lucanian issue that is not represented in the Brettian series survives today, as far as we know, in a single specimen, Naples, Santangelo 3449; this bears in our view a Head of Athena on the obverse, not a Head of Nike; the style is horrible, and we regard it as a blundered copy of the Brettian issue with Bust of Nike 1. / Zeus in biga 1. If one turns to consider provenance, the Lucanian issues were hoarded with Brettian issues in the Ricadi, S. Vincenzo and Petelia hoards;209 isolated finds known to us are from Cersosimo, Scolacium and Caulonia (two specimens);210 V. Capialbi, based in Vibo Valentia, possessed four specimens with AYKANlQN;211 all of this again suggests on balance that the coinage of the Lucani was produced for them in Bruttium, by the Brettii, in other words for those Lucani who followed Hannibal in his retreat to Bruttium in 207 BC (Livy XXVII, 51,13). A corollary of all this is that the coinage of the Brettii from the Fourth Coinage onwards was also produced after 207 BC: it makes good sense that a much larger part of the Brettian coinage than hitherto should be ascribed to the period after the defeat of Hasdrubal in 207 BC, when Hannibal became much more dependent on his Brettian allies. The inscription from Serra di Vaglio (Potentia 39) is in our view Greek in morphology and syntax, Osean in idiom. Paestum Our sources regard Paestum as having been Lucanian before the Roman conquest;214 but lou- in louprļi at Paestum is Campanian Osean, not Lucanian Osean (Blanda 1 is anomalous); there are close links between the coinage of Campania and Paestum;215 and Fausto Zevi observes to us that the tomb paintings of Paestum and the cavalry represented in them find their parallels in Campania, not Lucania. Buxentum, Blanda, Laos and Nerulum Of the rest of Lucania, Buxentum, Blanda and Laos form a series of cities on or near the coast, the first presumably corresponding to Pyxous, but represented in the archaeological record in the pre-Roman period by the settlement at Roccagloriosa. Each community has produced one or two outstanding texts, for some of which slightly more of a context is to be found than has been supposed (for the future imperative at Blanda, see above, p. 13, for the alphabet used at Blanda, see above, p. 19). It is unfortunate that, although the Greek colony of Laos, founded shortly before 500 BC, is attested by the literary sources and by its coinage, it has not been found. The only possible trace is of a wall forming part of a harbour installation near the mouth of the River Laos, found by chance in 1962 beneath over 2 metres of alluvium.216 It may be that the original site became uninhabitable and was moved to Marcellina c. 350-325 BC, in a period when the Lucani were already in control. The discovery of a possible die (whether for making the blank or for striking the coin) from the earlier period in the later settlement is possible evidence for continuity between the two.217 The coinage of the Greek city circulated extensively in the area of Castelluccio, ancient Nerulum,218 from which comes the olla in Berlin inscribed in the Achaean alphabet (Nerulum 1). Rossano di Vaglio (see Fig. 5) The volume of inscriptions, despite the fact that the site was used as a quarry for centuries, is unparalleled elsewhere in Lucania, not at nearby Civita di Tricarico, not at Roccagloriosa, though these sites certainly include sanctuary areas, and we return to its status below. * * * The earliest account of the site is in a report of 27 October 1797, in the Casa Reale archive (fsc. 95 1):225 In una difesa della terra del Vaglio, nella provincia di Basilicata, denominata Bosco di Rossano, ridotta a culture, per alluvioni succcesse anni addietro si scommessero alcuni terreni chiamati calanche, e restarono scoverte alcune vestigia di antichi edifízi, e cavandosi in essi si rinvenne un candelabro di metallo ed un anello ligato ad una testa di ariete [presumably a steelyard weight], che come pezzi di antichità furono portati nel R. Museo. Colle alluvioni del corrente anno, smossi nuovamente quei terreni in calanche, si sono rinvenuti altri pezzi di antichità ben travagliati, consistenti in un lioncino di bronzo indorato alto circa 1 pai. [= 0.264 m]; in due statuette dell' istessa materia ed altezza, rappresentanti due dee; in una corniola ligata ad asse, rappresentante una testudine, in varj piattini di bronzo, ed altro. Lombardi adds the information that the area of Rossano di Vaglio had produced 'molti e bei vasi italo- greci', marble (= limestone) funerary inscriptions, coins of Magna Grecia (also produced by the modern excavations), and 'i resti di un aquidotto, che dividevasi in diversi rami'; he knew also that the candelabrum was 5 palms high = 1 .3 1 m. M. Lejeune observes rightly that there was much re-use of stone during the period of life of the sanctuary, with the consequence that many of the inscriptions were not found in their original position.226 But guesses about the original location of re-used stones are in our view fragile, and there is no ground for optimism even in the case of RV 08 = Potentia 34 and RV 49 = Potentia 27: they cannot be linked with each other on grounds of letter size or attributed with any plausibility to an original altar of c. 350-300 BC. Nor can RV 07 = Potentia 8 be attributed with any plausibility to the dedication of Room II. The essential problem is that the 1969 dating of the great double altar in the central court to c. 350-300 BC, and of Room II to c. 250 BC, has never been re-thought; in particular, no weight can be placed on 223 P.
