Drawn by Frances Morton the fact that the period of use of the altar was initially dated to between 350 BC and 250 BC, partly on the basis of the first coins found and what was then still widely believed about the dating of Roman coinage. (Life is not made easier by the fact that forty years on the coins remain completely unpublished.) The altar was clearly built before the pavement that surrounds it: 'le testate delle lastre di pavimentazione si affiancano ai blocchi di fondazione in maniera molto precisa, indicando quindi una priorità nella costruzione dell'altare stesso e, nello stesso tempo, la contemporaneità delle due parti dell'altare' (Adamesteanu &amp; Dilthey (n. 226) pp. 20-1). But the claim that the use of relatively soft stone to build the altar and very hard limestone to build the pavement reflects a different chronology is of no weight, since the use of a hard limestone for the pavement responds to an obvious practical necessity. Nor can any confidence be reposed in the attribution of eight inscriptions to the altar (RV 10, 25, 11, 12, 19, 20, 21, 26 = Potentia 35, 31, 17, 24, 11, 25, 14, 15) simply on the grounds that the stone is similar.227 This attribution 227 See Adamesteanu &amp; Lejeune (n. 225) pp. 77-78.</page><page sequence="70">INTRODUCTION 53 is in fact the other assumption that underlies the early dating of the altar. But we do not think that the excavators ever thought through the consequences of their postulation of an altar either (1) randomly dotted with inscribed dedications or (2) largely demolished, with the blocks re-used elsewhere, despite having a pavement lovingly fitted round it. The existing altar and pavement were surely planned as a single whole, including the drainage canal in the pavement, that makes a slight bend to avoid the northern corner of the altar; and we can have no idea of where the various re-used inscriptions originally stood. This interpretation is entirely compatible with the data from the original excavation of the altar: the long part of the altar and the pavement around it and Room II were built on virgin soil, as were 'l'angolo Nord-Ovest dell'altare, di fronte all'amb. IV' (read evidently 'l'angolo Ovest'), the only surviving element of the short part of the altar, and the pavement around it; the pavement surrounding the rest of the short part of the altar was built on 'terreno di riporto', presumably to create a level surface.228 The fact that the soil within the altar was at a higher level than the courses of blocks that survived shows that the higher courses had been removed during the centuries of use of the site as a quarry.229 The significance of the division of the altar into two is now lost, but there presumably was one, given the placing of a sword as an offering under the dividing wall. On other hand, we accept (1) that the present floor of Room III was laid at the end of the Republic; (2) that the second phase of the drainage canal that heads eastwards represents an even later intervention, since it cuts part of the south east wall of Room III; and (3) that the stoa was built under the Empire (Building B, later Room IV). The phenomenon of re-use of inscribed blocks is in our view fundamental: a period in which the sanctuary was simply a place for dedications by individuals was followed by a period in which it was a place managed by magistrates; and a re-building of the sanctuary involved the re-use of the earlier dedications. On the other hand, not only did the institutional life of the sanctuary continue, apparently uninterrupted, into the Imperial age (see RV 22), at least some of the Osean texts remained in situ and visible (see below: it may of course be that those who saw them could not tell that the language was Osean, not Greek). Although we shall obviously never know anything about the archaeological context of the inscriptions that came to light before excavations began, the evidence is in our view good enough to justify the assertions just made: (A) Re-used inscriptions: Potentia 11, 12, 13 (found separately in two pieces), 14, 15, 17, 18, 19, 20 (?: damaged) 21, 24, 25, 27 (?: damaged), 28, 30, 31, 32; (B) Inscriptions of magistrates (* indicates, both here and below, inscriptions found without context, outside the site and for the most part before excavations began): Potentia 1, 3*, 4*, 5*, 6* (?: there may be an institutional content) 9, 10, 36* (?: there may be an institutional context), 37* (?: the inscription is long); (C) Inscriptions probably remaining in situ under the Roman Empire (we have supposed that inscriptions quarried from the site before excavations began had remained in situ): Potentia 1, 3*, 4*, 5*, 6*, 9, 10, 23 (a late private inscription), 36* (?: there may be an institutional context), 37* (?: the inscription is long). [Potentia 16*, 22*, may be late private dedications; Potentia 7 is too small to make anything of, but may = Potentia 8*; there is no clear evidence for Potentia 8*, 29, 33, 34, 35; the two bronze fragments, Potentia 2, 26, are excluded from the table.] 228 See Adamesteanu &amp; Dilthey (n. 226) p. 23; there seem to have been traces at some points of an earlier pavement. 229 See Adamesteanu &amp; Lejeune (n. 225) p. 42.</page><page sequence="71">54 IMAGINES ITALICS The evidence of the texts as thus tabulated shows that inscriptions of magistrates correlate with those that remained in situ, and that neither correlates with re-used inscriptions. In our view the monumentalisation of the sanctuary - altar, pavement, and surrounding wall - by an organised community, involved the cancellation of the sanctuary as a locale for private dedications; and this monumentalisation took place c. 200 BC. We are pleased to have this confirmed by Anna Rita Parente, who has kindly informed us that the coins under the altar, fortunately not touched by Adamesteanu and Lejeune, go down to the very end of the third century BC. (If the sword is earlier, it will be a re-deposit.) The only late private re-used dedication seems to be Potentia 14, re-used in a repair, not the construction, of the perimeter wall, after 125-100 BC. * * * We see in general no reason to privilege the theonym outiana more than aravina or kaporroina, or to see the people who worshipped at the sanctuary as the Vtiani; the hypothesis is unnecessary to explain the prominence of Mefitis Vtiana in Roman Potentia (see below, p. 55). But it is this presence that has led to the widely-held view that Macchia di Rossano lay in the territory that was later that of Potentia. This view is not without difficulty, since there is in our view little doubt that Potentia, whose lay-out underlies the streets of the modern town, is a not otherwise attested Latin colony of the early second century BC, whereas the Osean inscriptions at Rossano seem to go down to the Social War. The sanctuary was to all appearances run by a community, that had a senate, which took decisions, and magistrates. But which community? The site lies near the top of the hill above Vaglio di Basilicata and the Basento valley, but actually looks across towards Acheruntia, modern Acerenza. Did it belong to Acheruntia? Given the distance and the deep valley that lies between, this is hard to believe. Serra di Vaglio, much closer, seems to have survived only until the third century BC,230 whereas the period of the overtly political at Rossano is the second century BC., and there is no trace of any kind of political community at Rossano in the period of existence of Serra di Vaglio. And Civita di Tricarico too largely drifted out of existence in the second century BC, in the period in which an institutional structure was taking over at Rossano. We have a hypothesis to offer: the territory of Civita di Tricarico was perhaps in the end part of that of Potentia, and so too perhaps was that of Calle di Tricarico, itself a site from which a tile was exported to Civita (Potentia 44) and to which a tile was exported from Civita (Potentia 42). Is it conceivable that, shortly before Potentia was founded, an Oscan-using community of Lucanians, whose elite had remained loyal to Rome during the Hannibalic War, was brought into being, centred on the sanctuary, for the administration of the territory not intended for the colony, territory that only became part of that of Potentia after the Social War? Such a swathe of independent Lucanian territory might help to explain why the Lucani were still a major fighting force in that war against Rome.231 And with the co-habitatiòn of an independent, Oscan-using Rossano di Vaglio and its territory with Latin Potentia, one might compare the survival of Teanum Sidicinum as an independent, Oscan-using community next door to Cales and Suessa Aurunca. There is then in our view even less sense here in talking of a federal sanctuary than in the case of Pietrabbondante; for the fact that the coinage of the Lucani is not in any real sense federal, see above, p. 48. * * * Our arrangement of the texts, both starting from the archaeological evidence and placing first those of magistrates, places at the begining of the entries a group that in our view belongs between c. 200 BC and the Social War, then texts of individuals, mostly earlier: the chronology of Lejeune, based on orthography and 230 A. de Paolo &amp; A. Sartoris, Bollettino Storico della Basilicata 17 (2001) pp. 15-28, 'Rapporto preliminare delle campagne di scavo a Serra e Rossano di Vaglio (anni 1997-1999)'. 231 The Lucani as a military power are only discussed down to the late third century BC by Isayev (n. 208): see A. Small, JRA 20 (2007) pp. 399-402, reviewing Isayev.</page><page sequence="72">INTRODUCTION 55 letter forms, has already been questioned on other grounds. We have very cautiously supposed the following evolution of orthography: the separate sign for the aspirate was available in South Italy by c. 400 BC.232 Early Later Late Al Z El HI H h OY O Q, CO Lejeune argued consistently for an evolution in the signs used to representuj233 we agree with him that the use of 0, adopted simply because it was not otherwise needed, is early, occurring in Potentia 19, 20, 24, of which the first and last are probably early on other grounds. On the other hand, S is already attested on two helmets of c. 375/350 and 350 BC (Lucania or Brettii or Sicilia 3; Lucania 1); moreover, the orientation is left to right in texts otherwise written from right to left, which one may suppose to have been written by men used to the Osean alphabet; this lends plausibility to Lejeune's original derivation of S from Osean 8, rejected by del Tutto Palma.234 The forms J, S ,) vel sim. seem to be developments of S .235 The shift in the pronunciation of beta in Greek, perhaps c. 150 BC, perhaps lies behind its use for /in Osean; it is attested in Potentia 1, of 125- 100 BC, and 14, which we assign to a similar date.236 For isolated uses of 9, see Buxentum 3; Laos 2. Note also that the use of interpuncts is a late phenomenon, though their absence does not necessarily imply an early date; and that late texts may still choose to use non-lunate and non-cursive forms. We are indeed struck by the enormous variety of hands, and have not attributed much significance to different forms of alpha, though we record them, beta, eta, mu, nu, pi, rho and four-barred sigma (for early broken-barred alpha, see Potentia 13; we are also reluctant to attach much weight to the different forms of J for f * * * Two separate points. First, it has been observed that whereas the magistrates attested at Rossano include a censor - apparently as an eponym - and quaestor, they do not (as yet) include a meddix-, but RV 28 = Potentia 1 looks very much like the inscription of a meddix, though not labelled as such because it was obvious. (Note that Numistro certainly (Numistro 1), Serra di Vaglio probably (Potentia 39), had a meddix.) It may be that a parallel is that of Abella, where most activity seems to have been in the hands of the quaestor. Second, an Osean transcription of apxiTSKTcov perhaps occurs in Potentia 36. * * * The passage from Oscan-using to Latin-using Rossano is problematic in various ways: it is clear that Lejeune wanted to find Mefitis Vtiana and/or Venus Vtiana wherever he could, to add to CIL X, 131, 132, 133, all Mefitis Vtiana, all perhaps from the sanctuary. BRETTII Romans of the second century BC saw the Brettii as bilingual (Ennius, Annates All Skutsch = Festus, Pauli Epit. 31 L; Lucilius 142 Warmington); and, leaving aside the moral connotation of bilingualism as equivalent to unreliability, it has usually been taken that they all spoke both Osean and Greek; the evidence suggests perhaps that some areas used predominantly Osean, some Greek. REFERENCES AND BIBLIOGRAPHY Of those who have worked on these inscriptions after Mommsen, Zvetaieff, von Planta and Conway all travelled widely and saw a large proportion of the inscriptions which they included; and Zvetaieff in particular is the last to have published a comprehensive apparatus of plates; we have included the works of all three in the Bibliography for each entry, with the exception of von Planta and Conway for tile stamps, which clearly interested neither of them. We have also included references to Vetter, Poccetti, and ST, in the References for each entry, as well as SE (i.e., the 'Rivista di epigrafia italica') for inscriptions published after the completion of ST, and whatever is most appropriate for inscriptions omitted from ST; but we have not normally referred to intervening handbooks. We have in general not added references to the most recent volumes of SE, where they add nothing to what we have already discovered. Otherwise, we have been guided by what we regard as essential for the understanding of the context or the content of a text. We use pl. [1] etc., where plates are not actually numbered in a book or article. Perhaps idiosyncratically, we use de, del, di, van, von, etc. throughout in lower case, aware that this does not always correspond to national usages; vive l Europei LOCATION We use the good old-fashioned term of Soprintendenza Archeologica, rather than the Thatcherite absurdity that has replaced it, which might in any case be changed again at any moment on the whim of some Berlusconi minister. AUTOPSY We record both autopsies carried out specifically by us or by colleagues for the Imagines Italicae project, and autopsy by others; we only attribute autopsy to Vetter if there is a date. OBJECT We distinguish volcanic tufa and calcareous tufa: the claim of R. Ling, Burlington Magazine 131 (1989), 153, reviewing L. Richardson, Pompeii (1988), that 'tuff or tufo = volcanic and tufa = calcareous, is not normal English usage, as enshrined in the Oxford English Dictionary. Where a pot is of an identifiable Greek type, we have used the Greek term; in the mare magnum of Italian black-slip wares, particularly given the random use of terminology by many of the authorities we cite, we have judged it more helpful simply to distinguish between plates, cups (with handles) and bowls (without handles). In the case of tile stamps vel sim., it sometimes happens that interpuncts and frames are invisible if the stamp has been weakly impressed; many of the early finds of tile stamps from Pompei were sawn down so as to preserve only the stamp, and in such cases we give only thickness, since the other dimensions are not merely useless, but misleading; where enough of a tile is preserved to reveal the top or bottom of a flange, the former cut away so as to fit under the flange of the tile above, we have sought to mention this. Dimensions are given in metres, converted from Neapolitan palms, etc., as necessary; [0.00] indicates a reconstructed dimension.</page><page sequence="79">62 IMAGINES ITALICS DATE If no authority is given, dating criteria are indicated where possible, e.g., archaeological context, style, class of monument or object, language, letter forms, formulae, nomenclature, etc. EPIGRAPHICAL ACCOUNT We mention alphabet and script (the identification of an alphabet can of course sometimes depend on a decision about the language and the transcription of the text, and we have tried always to draw attention to such uncertainty; for the Ionic script see above, p. 48), direction of script, height of letters in metres, means of production of letters (for inscriptions on stone, we assume cutting of letters, unless we say 'scratched'; for terracotta, we indicate before or after firing), position of the text on the object, type of interpunct. Where something special or unusual stands out, such as clarity of incision, angularity of strokes, elegance, use of drafting lines, etc., we have tried to draw attention to this. We have also commented on letter shape, where more than one shape is used for that phoneme, where the letter shape is unusual, or where the letter shape is in question. In the case of the South Picene alphabet, letter heights exclude the dot used for o. TEXT We have resolved abbreviated names and words where possible. The evidence suggests that the default case both for dedications on the one hand and for tile-stamps and loom-weights on the other hand is the nominative. We hope we have not been over-adventurous. Since the alphabet and/or script is given in the epigraphical account, we limit the use of different type faces for different alphabets: except in the case of coin legends, lower case only is used, in the Latin alphabet for texts in indigenous alphabets or the Latin alphabet, in the Greek alphabet for all Greek scripts; all punctuation given is ancient; word breaks without ancient punctuation are indicated by a space. In the case of coin legends, where they are imbricated in descriptions of the coin types, lower case Italic is used for indigenous alphabets, upper case Greek for all Greek scripts, upper case Latin for the Latin alphabet. Ancient spacing is indicated by vacat, vac, or v. Each line in the original starts on a new line here; a hyphen at the end of the line shows that the final word of the line is completed at the start of the next line. Ligatures are indicated in the Apparatus. CONVENTIONS An underdot indicates a letter that taken in isolation is uncertain; the basis of disputable conjectures is explained in the Apparatus, where possible. An underline indicates a letter or letters seen by (an) earlier editor(s) but no longer visible; we have only used this convention where a substantial block of text has disappeared, not where a single letter has ceased to be visible, though we try to draw attention to such cases in the Apparatus. + = a trace of a letter. We use upper case for numerals. Square brackets indicate a lacuna in the text caused by damage to the object; [-?-] = a lacuna of unknown length; [1], [4-5], etc. = missing letters. [[ ]] enclose wholly or partially erased letters; &lt; &gt; enclose letters omitted in error and supplied by the editor, or engraved in error and corrected by the editor; details appear in the Apparatus; {} enclose letters engraved in error; () enclose the expansion of an abbreviation; (?) indicates that the text is abbreviated but that we cannot expand the text.</page><page sequence="80">INTRODUCTION 63 TRANSLATION We leave praenomina abbreviated in our translations, in order not to mislead; the common praenomina shared with Latin are normally left abbreviated both in text and in translation; the Index of names offers further information.</page><page sequence="81">IMAGINES ITALIC® VOLUME I CORPUS</page><page sequence="82">Italia / ITALIA 1 Coinage References HNltaly (2001) nos. 406-28 Object Gold and silver coinage. Date 90-89 BC Epigraphical account The coinage of the Italians in the Social War, of which the fullest corpus is by A. Campana, La monetazione degli insorti italici durante la guerra sociale (91-87 a.C.) (Soliera 1987), consisted of silver coins of the weight and fabric of the Roman denarius, supplemented by a single gold issue of Attic weight (the one known example weighs 8.47 gm.). This is substantially lighter than the gold issues of Sulla of a decade or so later, which were produced at 30 to the Roman pound, 10.80 gm. While Sulla clearly integrated his gold issues into the Roman monetary system, the Italians used a weight standard, which anyone familiar with the Greek east would have known (see below); for the authenticity of the piece, see M.H. Crawford, in Hans-Markus von Kaenel et al. (edd.), Geldgeschichte vs. Numismatik. Theodor Mommsen und die antike Münze (Berlin 2004) pp. 131-8, 'From Lanzi to Mommsen: the coinages of the peoples of Italy'. In describing the types, our running numbers are those of N. K. Rutter (ed.), Historia Numorum. Italy (London 2001), despite the regrettable errors in the transcription of the Osean legends; we have also given references to Campana, first his series number, then his die-combination numbers. We transcribe Latin legends in upper case Roman, Osean legends in italic. The diacritical on the í is sometimes rather sketchily engraved. There is no evidence that E.A. Sydenham, The Coinage of the Roman Republic (London 1952) no. 626, with Head of Italia, víteliú / Oath- taking scene, has ever existed. Although a mint for the coinage of the Italians in the Social War was presumably initially at Corfinium, it is misleading to assign any of it to that city or to the Paeligni, since it was created as a coinage of the Italians as a whole and perpetuated at a variety of different, if unknown, mints, as the organisation of the Italians and that of their coinage fell to pieces. ITALIA and víteliú thus identify the state of the Italians, rather than a mint. The coinage belongs at least to the period 90-89 BC, the two years of the Social War; it may have continued later, but there is no certain evidence. The arrangement here - we know it to be an uneasy compromise between a numismatic classification and an attempt at a chronology - supersedes that of M.H. Crawford, JVC, ser. 7, 4 (1964) pp. 141-58, 'The coinage of the age of Sulla', unwisely followed by Campana and by F. Tataranni, Ath 93 (2005) pp. 291-304, 'II toro, la lupa et il guerriero'. The arrangement here is essentially that of T.V. Buttrey, which was expounded to MHC in the late 1970s, when the latter first became involved in the production of HNltaly, what Buttrey did not mention was his article in Bollettino della Deputazione Abruzzese di Storia Patria 63 (1973) pp. 503-12, 'Le monete della Guerra Sociale', which is both fundamental and ignored by all subsequent scholars. (We are sceptical as to whether the 73 plaster casts of coins of the Social War in the Museo di Corfinio represent coins that were once in that museum, found locally.) The coinage has three distinctive features, against which the legends used must be viewed: at the beginning, it was initially produced at two central mints, with synchronised issues for both the Oscan-speaking and the Latin-speaking areas; the silver coins were not only compatible with, but also circulated with, Roman denarii; and the types were in part deliberately chosen, in part borrowed from those of Roman denarii, the latter process being one in which no clear system is discernible. (1) Nos. 407 and 408, identical in style and with similar systems of control-letters, are clearly in effect a bilingual issue from a single mint, presumably Corfinium; together they form the largest single block of the Social War coinage, and each generated a number of IMAGINES ITALICHE - VOLUME I - 67</page><page sequence="83">derivative groups (very small variants are ignored). No. 407, bis, is superficially similar to, but not part of, no. 407: no. 407, bis, (a) and (b), may both have been created by copying a specimen of no. 407; the style of no. 407, bis, (a), leads on to that of nos. 409-10, that of no. 407, bis, (b), to that of no. 415, (a) and (b). Although nos. 407, bis, 409 (of C. Papius C.f. Mutilus), and 410 are classified here, they almost certainly form part of the tail-end of the coinage of the Italians. Similarly with no. 413, (a), (b)-(c), and (d), forming three stylistic groups, although the control-letters used suggest that they were produced as a unit; the first group is perhaps distantly derived from the style of nos. 409 and 410, and the last leads on to that of no. 414. In the case of no. 415, (a) and (b) are of similar style and perhaps form a continuous series; (c) is of a different style, although the control-numbers used suggest that it was produced with the rest. There seems to have been an attempt at a bilingual issue. In contrast to the issues just discussed, no. 41 1, the issue of Q. Silo, certainly, and no. 412, perhaps, may be regarded as forming part of the coinage of the mint at Corfinium. A separate group is composed of nos. 416 to 421, which can be plausibly placed together on grounds of fabric and style; the style of nos. 416, 418, 419, and the bulk of no. 417 is identical; a secondary style in no. 417 then leads on to nos. 420 and 421. The group is as a whole clearly bilingual, and distinct from nos. 407 and 408 above all because of its small compact flans; we suppose it to be a centrally organised - because bilingual - issue, produced elsewhere than Corfinium at an early stage of the war. Nos. 422 and 423, one Osean and one Latin, are both anonymous and are unrelated stylistically to each other or to any other issue. There remain the five further issues of C. Papius C.f. Mutilus: nos. 424 and 425, stylistically unrelated to each other or to any other issue; a bilingual issue, no. 426; and nos. 427 and 428, which describe him as imperator. We provisionally assign here the new denarius with ITALIA, which shares with elements of the coinage of Mutilus a similar approach to choice of type and disposition of legend. (2) The different groups of the coinage appear together and with Roman denarii in hoards, and isolated specimens were hoarded occasionally long after the Social War was over (see Campana, p. 37). Note also some isolated finds: of the new denarius near Heraclea; of HNItaly, no. 409, presumably from Campi Salentina (M. Pinder, Die antiken Münzen des königlichen Museums (Berlin 1851) p. XXXI: 'Aus Campii bei Lecce stammt zum Beispiel die höchst seltene Münze mit der Aufschrift Safinim, No. 14 der nachstehenden Beschreibung' (not mentioned in Dressel, Beschreibung, p. 60); of no. 415, c, from Gravina di Puglia (Museo Nazionale di Taranto) and of no. 425 from Labico (F. Ficoroni, Memorie di Labico (Rome 1745) p. 84). Campana, nos. 72, o (no. 408); 107, k (no. 412); 143, k (no. 407), in the Museo Nazionale dell'Aquila, are likely to have local provenances; no. 83, i (no. 425), comes from Sannio, no. 159, e (no. 417), from Marche, nos. 124, n (no. 407: Sannio. Pentri e Frentani dal VI al I secolo a.C. (Rome 1980) p. 225, no. 34); 136, f (no. 407: S. Capini, Campochiaro. Potenzialità di intervento sui beni culturali (Campobasso 1982) p. 54, no. 91), from Campochiaro. A Social War denarius with oath-taking scene was found in Saggio 6, Santa Felicita, Valle d'Ansanto (N. Gambino, La Mefite nella Valle d' Ansanto di Vincenzo Maria Santoli. Rilettura dopo duecento anni 1783-1983 (Rocca San Felice 1991) p. XV, cf. p. XVIII (Indice delle illustrazioni); if it is the piece illustrated in A. Mele (ed.), Il culto della dea Mefite e la Valle d'Ansanto (Avellino 2008) p. 280, fig. 6, it is no. 415, (b); given that Gambino notoriously bought material from all over the place, there is no reason to suppose the other piece in his collection to be of local provenance, any more than the piece in the Zigarelli collection). (3) Of the borrowed types, some were presumably invested with new significance: thus 68 - IMAGINES ITALIŒ - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="84">Italia on nos. 407-8, 410-15 and 423, owes a great deal to Apollo on the issues of, e.g., L. Pomponius Molo and A. Albinus, etc. ( RRC , nos. 334-5), on no. 421 to the issue of M. Cato ( RRC , no. 343), on no. 422 to early Dioscuri denarii, on no. 424 to the issue of M. Acilius M.f. (RRC, no. 255), on no. 426, (a)-(c) to the issue of C. Serveilius M.f. (RRC, no. 239), on no. 426, (d) to the issue of L. Memmius (RRC, no. 304, 1); the seated Italia on nos. 412 and 414 copies the seated Roma of A. Albinus, etc. (RRC, no. 335), the Italia in a biga on no. 417 copies the Victory in a biga of D. Silanus L.f. (RRC, no. 337); but all presumably reflect the significance to the Italians of the concept Italia and the federal capital called Italia at Corfinium, described by Diodorus Siculus (XXXVII, 2). (The reverse types, Dioscuri riding right, Dioscuri riding apart, Dioscuri standing facing (nos. 422, 426, (a)-(c), and 426, (d)) perhaps have no Italian resonance.) Associated with the idea of Italia is the bull as the symbol of Italia (see E. Dench, From Barbarians to New Men (Oxford 1995) pp. 213-16; M. Pobjoy, in E. Herring &amp; K. Lomas, The Emergence of State Identities in Italy (London 2000), pp. 187-21 1, 'The first Italia'), either reclining beside an Italian warrior or goring the Roman wolf. The use by enemies of Rome of the image of the wolf to symbolise their hatred of the predatory power of Rome is dramatically portrayed in Velleius Paterculus II, 27, and Justin XXXVIII, 6, 7. Minerva on nos. 416, 418 and 419, copied from denarii of P. Servilius Rullus (RRC, no. 328), and Mars, on nos. 409 and 428, copied from denarii of Q.Thermus M.f. (RRC, no. 319), on no. 425, copied from denarii of Ti. Veturius (RRC, no. 234), have a universal significance as warrior deities. Victory understandably appears frequently, whether as a substantive type on no. 421, copied from denarii of M. Cato (RRC, no. 343), or crowning Minerva (nos. 416 and 418), or symbolized by a trophy. As types to be considered, there remain Bacchus (no. 427) or emblems of Bacchus as symbols of libertas, oath-taking scenes as symbols of loyalty to the cause of the allies (nos. 408, 411, 413, 415, 423, 425, 428; see also L. Cappelletti, ZPE 127 (1999) pp. 85-92, 'Il giuramento degli Italici sulle monete del 90 a.C.', very speculative), the scene showing a man being greeted as he descends from a ship, perhaps an emissary from Mithridates VI (no. 416: compare no. 406 below), and finally an isolated and puzzling bust of one of the Dioscuri (no. 417). See also A. Burnett, in id., U. Wartenberg, R. Witschonke (edd.), Coins of Macedonia and Rome. Essays in Honour of Charles Hersh (London 1998) pp. 165-72, "The coinage of the Social War'. Of the individuals named on the coins, Q. Silo (no. 41 1; see also on no. 413) and C. Papius C. f. Mutilus (nos. 409 and 424-8) are well-known, while Mi. Iegius Mi.f. (no. 406) and N. Lucius M.f. (no. 410) are otherwise unknown. Date 50-0 BC IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I - 89</page><page sequence="104">Last known location / Autopsy Paris, Bibliothèque Nationale, Cabinet des Médailles, Collection Froehner X 403 / 08/1 1/02 (MHC) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object The indication of provenance in Froehner's manuscript catalogue, Trouvé en Hongrie. Vente Miller von Aichholz, cat. n. 229', could be an inference from the fact that the object came from the collection of Eugène de Miller Aichholz, but is more likely to have been known to Froehner because a note was attached to the object when he bought it: Froehner is scrupulous in distinguishing between certain and supposed provenances. The Miller von und zu Aichholz family was one of the great aristocratic families of the Austro-Hungarian empire; the object with which we are concerned is the only ancient object in the sale catalogue; and there is no reason to follow Lejeune's suggested emendation of the provenance to 'trouvé en Ombrie' (Lejeune also mistranscribes 'Hiller von Aichholz, cat. no. 229'): similar objects of similar date are common in Transdanubia (M. Feugère &amp; R. de Marinis, in M. Feugère &amp; Cl. Rolley (edd.), La vaisselle tardo-républicaine en bronze (Dijon 1991) pp. 97-1 12, 'Les poêlons', esp. nos. 84-6 (not listing this piece, but better than K. Szabò, in J. Swaddling, S. Walker, P. Roberts (edd.), Italy in Europe: Economic Relations (BM Occasional Paper 97, 1995) pp. 273-89, 'Italian bronze vessels in Transdanubia before the Roman conquest')); compare also Lucania or Brettia or Sicilia 3 from Alesia. Froehner's collection passed after his death in 1925 to the Cabinet des Médailles. Epigraphical account Latin alphabet, left to right, Umbrian inscription (A), stippled letters 0.05-0.06, along the rim looking outwards, Latin inscription (B), stippled letters 0.06-0.09, on the handle, a = A, e = II, I = k (see also on the text); the s on the handle is a mess; the letter forms are unsurprising at this date in this script and medium. The final t appears to be shorter than the other letters, but its lower half may have followed to the left a flaw in the stone Translation [-?-] the Tadinates [-?-] have decreed [-?-] Bonomi Ponzi and Sisani read eitukest[-?-] in line 2, the former reconstructing the text as [tutas]/ tarina[tes ]/ ei tuke st[ahu ] ('della tota tadinate qui (?) sto publice"), the latter as [tuter]/ tarina[tiu ]/ ei tuke st[ahu] ( finis dei Tadinates qui (?) sto publice"). Their interpretations are problematic: (1) interpretation as 'Tadinate' ( vel sim.) is difficult, because we would expect the third letter to be / (&lt; intervocalic d, cf. Tab.Ig., Ib, lines 16, 17 tařinate), not r; (2) the interpretation of ei as 'qui (?)' = 'here' is uncertain; (3) the sequence of letters taken as tuke is tupe] and eitupes invites comparison with perfect third person plural eitipes (Tab.Ig, Va, lines 2, 14) 'they have decreed'. Rix (by letter) provides an explanation of eitupes as *eit-hab-e(n)s with the root *hab- 'have' (cf. Umbrian prehubia beside prehabia), whereas eitipes is formed with the root alternant (a different form of the root) *hep- 'have'. Rix's account has to assume that the alphabet at Tadinum employed p for b. If we accept that the Tadinates were independent of the Iguvines in some points of language ( *hap , not *hep) and alphabet (p, not b), we might then also argue that the Tadinate alphabet was different in not having ř Bibliography S. Object Bronze tablet, 0.55 high x 0.145 wide x 0.0025 thick, attached to the upper face of a length of a curved terracotta well-head, originally plastered (0.16-0.19 high x 0.09 width of rim x 0.04-0.045 height of rim x 0.07-0.075 thickness of wall), original external diameter c. 0.725. Of the two holes visible on the left-hand side of the tablet, the upper is open and the lower is filled with lead. Similar holes presumably exist on the right-hand side, but these are covered by a lump of lead. A further lump of lead 0. 1 6 m to the right of the tablet is probably the remains of a clamp joining our length of well-head to the piece to thé right. The tablet is also set in lead. The bottom of the length of well-head is intact, with a series of rows of triple knobs that would have helped to mortar it to a base. Neither this fact, not noted in Antiquarium di Fossato di Vico (2007), nor the shape of the piece is consistent with a dolium, contra Rocca. V[illa] G[iulia] 42886 (193) / 14/04/04 (MHC, UR, MW) 98 - IMAGINES ITALICA - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="113">Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Found in 1868, 'assieme ad altri pezzi dello stesso vaso nel fondo di una buca tonda del diametro di m. 2.40, ed altrettanto profonda, scavata regolarmente nel vivo di uno scoglio sulla cima di un monticello che si erge presso Fossato nella imboccatura della foce che mena a Fabriano tra chilometri 209-210 da Roma1 (Fabretti). The 'buca' was re-excavated in 1940, and turned out to be a cistern (hardly a 'favissa', contra Stefani, p. 178, n. 2). It is perfectly possible for the well-head to have been the opening, since the ground level may have been higher in antiquity and the cistern may have narrowed substantially towards its original top. The architectural elements dumped in the cistern suggest the presence of a sanctuary in the vicinity. IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I - 147</page><page sequence="162">Umbria /AMERIAl Amelia References ST Um 21-2 ; Ve 229; Rocca 1, Am. 1 Object Bronze tablet, 0.038 high x 0.053 wide x 0.001 thick, inscribed on both sides, intact at top and bottom, broken at each end. Side A, which is inscribed with five lines of text, shows two nail-holes, 0.026 apart, near its bottom edge, which corresponds with the top edge of Side B. Roncalli argues that the tablet was suspended from hooks or rings, in such a way that it could be turned and read on the back. But the holes are very small for this; rather, although the tablet is thicker, the impression given is similar to that given by Plestia 1-3, namely that the tablet with Side A showing was fixed to the bottom of a wooden offering; by contrast, the nail- holes on Side B are unpleasingly placed in relation to the text, suggesting that this text had been written earlier, but was being discarded and replaced; this reconstruction is compatible with the fact that the nail-holes were driven through from Side B to Side A, presumably once Side B had been discarded; and with the fact that the engraver of Side A was evidently pre- occupied by the amount of text he had to inscribe. The principal difference between the two texts is indeed that the list of names, presumably dedicators, is slightly different. Date c. 300 BC (on grounds of letter forms) Last known location / Autopsy Naples, Museo Archeologico Nazionale, inv. 2501 / 12/04/06 (MHC, UR) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Di Costanzo: 'Non debbo qui tralasciare l'incontro ch'ebbi in Amelia [which he visited every year: MS Vatlat. 9673, f. 15 recto = 9674, p. 147] di acquistare un frammento di lamina di bronzo con caratteri umbri, o etruschi in ambedue le superficie ... Questo cimelio dopo esser stato qualche tempo in mie mani, lo cedetti al Cardinal Borgia ... L'acquistai in Amelia [in fact in 1788 as a present from Giuseppe Venturelli, a local antiquarian: 9673, f. 15 verso = 9674, p. 147; f. 18 recto = 9674, p. 153], e fu trovata in un antico sepolcro poco distante da quella città ...' It is not clear whence Fiorelli derived his belief that the object was found at Lagoscello; E. Rosa (cited by Ciotti) perhaps drew on local traditions in attributing the find to Sambucetole or perhaps simply chose a better known place near Lagoscello; both are about 2 148 - IMAGINES ITALIC® - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="163">km north-east of Amelia. The text may have ended up in a tomb, even if, as Ciotti plausibly suggests, it came originally from the sanctuary of S. Maria in Canale. Epigraphical account Umbrian alphabet, right to left, letters 0.005, d represented by Umbrian r, theta = O (compare Tabulae Iguvinae, lb, line 1; IV, line 20). Our text supposes outspaced first lines on each side, and a short nomen in A, line 2; B, lines 2, 3. We wonder whether the final hasta of A, line 5, is that of « as a monetary notation. Text and apparatus pa{q}qis blaisils (see Translation) The third letter most resembles an unsuccessful attempt at a q, given that the inscriber was incapable of proper curves; the letter does not to our eyes much resemble either an / or Marinetti's upside down u. IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME 1-159</page><page sequence="174">Despite the unhappiness with curves, all the strokes of the q (not a second p: so rightly Marinetti) are clearly deliberate; the stroke that follows is not in line with the top hasta of the s, and is probably better regarded as an i than as a first attempt at an s (Colonna); the hand of the inscriber then slipped on the top hasta of the b; and the stroke that crosses the second s is clearly accidental. The order of inscribing the hastae of the penultimate letter was as follows, clearly visible on autopsy: (1) the vertical stroke at the right; (2) the horizontal stroke at the top; (3) the sloping, then horizontal stroke at the left and the bottom; (4) the transverse stroke at the right. (3) begins where (2) stops, (4) begins where (3) stops. We regard (4), the transverse stroke at the right, as a deliberate diagonal (the alleged diagonal in CIE 8418 is however a botched shot) and the letter as an incomplete samek = í (Rocca (1995) (2001)) Translation We suspect paqqis in error (see above); we are unhappy with Marinetti's suggestion that the inscriber simply reversed -qu- and adopt a variant suggestion, in the light of the unparalleled nature of -qi-, namely that the inscriber forgot the u after having to engrave the q twice (compare, e.g., the Dueños vase and CIL Ý (1986) 2658). IMAGINES ITALIC® - VOLUME I - 209</page><page sequence="224">Vestini, Marrucini, Paeligni, Marsi (?) / VESTINI, MARRVCINI, PAELIGNI, MARSI (?) 2 [Poggio Buco] References ET AV 0.7-0.12 Date c. 300 BC (on grounds of letter forms) Epigraphical Account Latin alphabet, left to right, letters 0.004-0.005, rounded a, angular s, sloping top of t, double interpunct, on lead sling bullets; the balance of probability suggests that they were lost in an attack on Poggio Buco by a contingent of Roman socii. The site seems to have been largely unoccupied in the fifth and fourth centuries BC, and may have been no more than a military outpost when it was attacked. It comes back to life in the second century BC (G. Bartolom, in E A A, Secondo Supplemento IV (Rome 1996) pp. 390-2. The site is in any case not Statonia: E. Bormann had already observed of the specimen discovered by Pellegrini and republished by Milani ( CIL XI, 2, 2 (1926) p. 1419), '... in qua Statonensium mentionem reperisse sibi visi sunt and E.A. Stanco has now shown that Statonia is to be located at Bomarzo: MEFRA 106, 1 (1994) pp. 247-58, 'La localizzazione di Statonia: nuove considerazioni in base alle antiche fonti', endorsed by M. Munzi, Ostraha 4, 2 (1995) pp. 285-99, 'La nuova Statonia '. An inscription probably in the Latin alphabet has presumably been taken to be Etruscan by supposing that it appeared in mirror image on the bullets in error. The second letter of the second word, resembling a retrograde 3, is clearly differentiated from the six occurrences of s elsewhere, and we follow the lead of La Regina in IMAGINES ITALIC® - VOLUME 1-219</page><page sequence="234">representing it as ps. Note that the Latin alphabetic tradition is aware of the possibility of using different forms of s to represent non-normal usage: certainly 2 to represent the semuncia where S represents the semis (RRC 14/7; 21/7); and perhaps the same letter as is under discussion here to represent the half-obol (RRC 27/4: the coin is rather a half-obol than a litra). We suppose that there was a vacat in the rest of line 1 ; and that the rest of line 4 contained a praenomen and the beginning of a nomen ending in -ares: the implausibility of cognomina at this period (let alone a complete set of cognomina) leads us to see three pairs of aediles, without filiation; for -ares, compare the nomen Caesiarus (R. Cordelia &amp; N. Criniti, Iscrizioni latine di Norcia e dintorni (Spoleto 1982) pp. 68-9 = AE 1983, 294). Date 250-225 BC (?) Last known location / Autopsy Moscow, Pushkin Museum of Fine Arts (acc. A. La Regina) / Th. Mommsen Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Two different sites form part of the discussion: a hill-fort called Coste delle Prete della Civita, sometimes known as Città Danzica (vel sim.), south-southwest of Rapino, and a cave called Grotta del Colle (see Radmilli (1977) fig. 55), west-southwest of Rapino. Archaeological investigations of the cave have made it clear that a ruined church lies at its mouth, built on earlier foundations of uncertain date and function (d'Ercole et al. (1997) p. 92). A number of burials have been excavated below the floor of the church, along with a pit for casting a church bell. There are also recent discoveries of burials without grave goods within the cave (d'Ercole et al. (1997)); reports of such discoveries in the past begin with Romanelli (1809): 'Qui d'appresso [near Colle di Rocca] apresi lunga, e spaventosa grotta, dentro della quale si rinvengono ossa di sterminata grandezza'. It is important to note that there seems to have been in the cave build-up and removal of earth and stones at different periods (de Nino: the cave 'oggi è riempita più della metà [of its original height] dal brecciame di un torrente che per egoismo barbarico vi ha deviato il contadino Angelo Marra, proprietario di un terrenuccio sottostante'); and that explorations of the cave at different times have discovered different categories of objects, presumably because they have probed different areas. The principal problem is the presence of both sepulchral and votive material. Among the results of recent excavations is the establishment of the fact that one area of the cave contained a votive deposit, including pottery, terracottas and probably coins of the Hellenistic period (Galli (1939); d'Ercole et al. (1997) nos. 13 (the 'Dea di Rapino', acquired from Sig. Ferrante, along with a terracotta cockerel), 19-30, without any indication of the archaeological context of the different objects; pottery and terracottas had already been seized IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I - 229</page><page sequence="244">by the Carabinieri in 1932). The uncial as mentioned by Galli (1939) p. 241, n. 14, and the two Prow bronzes mentioned in Agostini et al. (2001) p. 77, n. 6, may have been displaced from their original context, but it is likely that the coins mentioned by Guarini (1841) formed part of a votive deposit: 'Rapini, Aprutii citerioris oppiduli, ingentes inter ruinas, quae Civitatausa, vel tansa vulgo audiunt, tabella aenea literata nuper detecta ... Ruinae, de quibus memoratum, vetus prae se ferunt amplumque sepulcretum, in quo oscorum numismatum cum graecis duobus neapolitanis seges demessa locupletissima a V. C. Carolo Riccio. Aes nostrum in sepulcro quodam latitabat, quod Popedii cuiusdam fuisse videtur'. The first report by Mommsen (1846) of the discovery of the Rapino bronze reads as follows: 'II sepolcreto di essa città [Coste delle Prete della Civita] fu nella così detta Grotta del Colle a 1 miglio da Rapino dalla parte di mezzogiorno [see above], dove sotto un mucchio di pietre che forse costituivano la cassa, si ritrovò uno scheletro e presso a lui un anello forse di avorio e la lastra di bronzo, di cui si parla' (Guidobaldi, p. 13, misrepresents this account). Mommsen goes on to record, on the basis of information from Riccio, the discovery of large numbers of coins 'in diverse tombe della grotta [see below]'. Mommsen, who went to Rapino in 1845, clearly adds something to Guarini; but his visit was some five years after the discovery of the bronze, and his account remains heavily conditioned by that of Guarini. There are no parallels known to us for burials in caves in Italy in the Hellenistic period, while there are numerous parallels for votive deposits in caves, and it seems easiest to suppose that the burial to which Mommsen refers and the tombs reported by Riccio are medieval, which cut into, and became mingled with, the layers of a votive deposit (Mommsen's ' mucchio di pietre' may well have been natural). Mommsen knew of the hill-fort known as Città Danzica, and unwisely jumped (following Guarini) to the conclusion that the Grotta del Colle formed its cemetery. This led him to the inaccurate statement that the Rapino Bronze was found among the ruins of Città Danzica, and means that it is not possible to be certain in relation to his list of finds whether they come from the cave or the hill-fort (pp. 83-4): 'Quivi si sono trovate ancora non poche monete [Footnote: Dice il Guarini che da quel paese il eh. sig. Riccio ebbe due medaglie napoletane ed altre molte "osche"; quando io fui a Rapino tutte le medaglie trovatevi erano già disperse. Però ci farebbe cosa gratíssima il sig. Riccio, se volesse particolarmente notificarci, quali medaglie osche abbia tratte da cotesto paese.] quasi tutte di conio romano, una testina di bronzo, colonne spezzate di pietra ed altre anticaglie; e quivi si vede un pozzo circolare fabbricato con mattoni a coltello, nel quale secondo che un'antica tradizione racconta, furono sepolte le ricchezze della città, quando gli abitanti vinti dai Romani vollero sottrar quelle alla cupidigia de' vincitori'. The coins are separately described later as coming from the cave (pp. 1 18-19): 'Sulle premurose nostre instanze il Sig. Riccio ci ha gentilmente dato il ragguaglio sulle monete trovate in diverse tombe della grotta, dove fu scavato il bronzo di Rapino. Se ne trovarano quasi un centinajo, tutte però di bronzo fuori una sola napoletana di argento. Oltre alcune logore ve ne furono diverse romane incerte [in UD, pp. 336-8, Mommsen guesses that these were Romano-Campanian] - quattro grossi spezzati di asse gettati, e precisamente il quadrante con ruota e cane ed il sestante con ruota e testuggine - dieci bellissime di Isemia con AISERNINO - AESERNINO - AESERNIVM - AESERNINOM (" curiose queste due ultime, aggiunge il Sig. Riccio, e da mè non incontrate pria ") - una bellissima frentana - e finalmente 230 - IMAGINES ITALICA - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="245">due di Teano col gallo e la leggenda romano TIANO.1 The cave might also be the provenance of the 'testina di bronzo' and the 'colonne spezzate di pietra ed altre anticaglie'; on the other hand, our guess would be that the well to which Mommsen refers was in the hill-fort; this is located by Mommsen (p. 84) as follows: ' ... iscrizione latina di carattere bellissimo trovata a poca distanza dalle suddette rovine al convento di S. Andrea e murata adesso in una casa rurale di Rapino stessa, perchè mostra che quel paese detto Civita d'Anzica fù abitato anche a' tempi romani e fu ascritto alla tribù arniese: N. SEPTVMIVS. N. F. ARN. CAPITO. PATER ' ( CIL IX, 2992: 'alla piana di S. Andrea verso Penna di Monte; extat in casa quadam rustica versus Guardiagrele'). The coins referred to above form a tight chronological sequence: a silver piece of Neapolis, 275-250 BC (?) ( HNItaly , nos. 586-7; the bronze of Neapolis cannot be dated), bronzes of Aesernia, 263-240 BC ( HNItaly , nos. 429-31), bronzes of Teanum, 265-240 BC ( HNItaly , nos. 453-6), a bronze of the Frentani, circa 250 BC {HNItaly, no. 621), and four pieces of the Roman series of aes grave with Wheel, circa 230 BC {HNItaly, nos. 234-30). The absence of pieces of the Roman series of aes grave with Prow makes it reasonably certain that the group closes before the Second Punic War. If we suppose that both the coins and the Rapino Bronze formed part of the votive deposit, we should conclude that the inscription is to be dated in the third quarter of the third century BC. (We have been unable to discover in the archives of the Soprintendenza Archeologica any information concerning the archaeological context of the coins published by Catalli, which are in any case only a selection from the coins registered in the archives; nor is it possible to relate the coins discovered in the 1995 excavations (D'Ercole et al. (1997)) to earlier discoveries.) The Rapino Bronze was initially acquired by Don Ignazio di Cicco of Rapino and then in 1846 by the Antikensammlung in Berlin (Misc. 2888, later Fr. 251 1); placed for safekeeping during the Second World War in an area later under Russian control, it is now said to be housed in the Pushkin Museum. References ST Sp CH 2 Object Spiral bronze bracelet, incomplete at each end, 0.013 wide x c. 0.075 diameter. Date 500-400 BC Last known location / Autopsy Chieti, Museo Nazionale, inv. 27048 / 17/10/92 (JSS) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Found in 1979 in 'una zona non precisata dell'Abruzzo centrale' (La Regina), in the Pescara valley, perhaps at the sanctuary above Pescosansonesco (Papi). Epigraphical account South Picene alphabet, letters 0.006-0.009, apart from o, triple interpuncts; e x 2 in tefeh has four horizontal hastae. The text proceeds from right to left round the bracelet, for the most part in a single line; the end is boustrophedon, with the text turning back above itself; it is clear that the engraver realised that (s)he was running out of space, and dropped the level of the text in order to fit two lines in; we suspect that each end of the bracelet was decorated, e.g., with an animal head, which have been broken off; where the text turns, we suspect that no more than one letter has been lost along with the decoration; more will have been lost at the beginning. We accept the view of La Regina and Stuart-Smith that the text was inscribed when the bracelet was in two pieces, that some text was lost when a larger than intended overlap was required to join the two pieces, and that the missing text was then re-inscribed, re-inserting final -n, omitting interpunct, correcting kto a, r to k, inserting r; we print Stuart-Smith's text, which must be read with her illustrative apparatus, both at the join and in general. A [Canosa] References ST Sp B A 1 Object Bronze helmet, Coarelli, Type C. Date 325-275 BC (Coarelli: but see the Introduction, pp. 12-13) Last known location / Autopsy Florence, Museo Archeologico, inv. 1237 / 30/05/05 (MHC) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Buonarroti: 'incisione di bronzo antico trovato a canne, appresso il Sig. A. Andreini, trovato l'anno 1673 o 1674'. Epigraphical account South Picene alphabet, right to left, letters 0.0075-0.015, incised on the underside of the neck- guard of the helmet, beginning to the left of the attachment for the cheek-piece, seen from inside the helmet. Text and apparatus úlúgerna For the fourth sign see the Introduction, p. 10 IMAGINES ITALIC® - VOLUME I - 257</page><page sequence="272">Translation The text is presumably a name, and Janda and Weiss argue that it is the ablative of a place *Uluerna, implied by the reading of Paris, BN, MS lat. 6797 at Pliny, AW III, 65, Uluernates (G. Radke, RE IXA, 1 (1961) p. 582), indicating the origin of the booty represented by the helmet; the text of ST Sp BO 1 = Interpromium B would then indicate the deity to whom that helmet was dedicated and the origin of the booty, Spoletium. But the dedication of a helmet at Olympia by Hieron (Jeffrey, LSAG, p. 275, no. 7) is a wholly false parallel: that helmet was dedicated at a Hellenic sanctuary, whereas at least one of our two helmets was found in a tomb; and the inscription of Hieron was blazoned on the outside of the bowl of the helmet (compare Lucania or Brettii or Sicilia 2 and Lucania 1), not tucked away under the neck- guard, quite apart from the pattern of booty being taken from communities of men, not places. One would expect both this text and that of Interpromium B to be personal names. This text might be a nominative singular feminine adjective, agreeing with 'helmet', indicating an owner or a maker; Interpromium B might be feminine ablative singular adjectives, agreeing with 'helmet', indicating a maker 'from Spoletium'. Date 150-50 BC (?) Last known location / Autopsy None / Informant of G. Mascitti Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object The compilers of epigraphic manuscripts, as emerges from even minimal familiarity, frequently merge texts that should be separate and divide texts that should be unitary; if Vetter were right against Conway that Corfinium 29 + 30 were a single text and offered a pair of names of magistrates, it would be very surprising if no trace whatsoever of the text describing what they had done had survived on the stone. On purely internal grounds, we have two separate funerary inscriptions, presumably on the standard limestone block; and in fact Mascitti records: (1) that he had heard of a 'sepulchrum hisce verbis animatum: V. VIBEDIS. N T. VALESIES. L' (2) 'che nelle campagne [between Pentima and S. Ippolito, towards Pratola Peligna, 'comprendendo il paese di Coccia, da Pratola al Castel Prezzano'] si scavano urne ed acquedotti di piombo, e infino che un uomo trovò un sepolcro in cui era scritto: V. Vibedis IV [ a mistake for N] T. Valesies L.' The first description in particular would also allow the possibility that there were in fact two blocks, within a funerary enclosure. IMAGINES ITALICA - VOLUME I - 339</page><page sequence="354">[Latium] / [VELITRAE 1] [Velletri] References ST VM 2; Ve 222 Object Bronze tablet, 0.039 high x 0.235 long x 0.03 thick. There are no nail-holes, but there is one hook and one stump on the back; Antonini suggests that these connected with 'un complicato meccanismo di apertura / chiusura che mettevano in funzione' to protect the statom of the text, which was in consequence an object: we doubt this, and think that analyses should be undertaken to establish whether the hook and stump are ancient. Date The date suggested by the letter forms is c. 275 BC; the appearance of c in Eg(natus) is not significant for chronology. Last known location / Autopsy Naples, Museo Archeologico Nazionale, inv. 2522 / 14/04/03 (WMB, MW) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object The tablet was discovered at Velletri, but, as the contemporary accounts show (see below, especially Lanzi), no record was preserved of exactly where; and it seems to have been Haeberlin who invented the story that it had been found with two Roman currency bars, also discovered at Velletri (see below). Even if we knew anything of the location or context of the discovery of the tablet, it would still be the case that it is a portable object of high value, like the two currency bars, which were certainly made at Rome (another currency bar made at Rome got to Tifernum Tiberinum via Sestinum (Sestinum 1)). The fact that the inscription was found somewhere in or near Velitrae does not mean that it was made there: the use of Volscian at Velitrae in 300-275 BC would be surprising, given the largely Roman origin of the population after 338 BC; inscriptions from elsewhere in Volscian territory use the Osean alphabet (see Atina 1); and the appearance of two meddices in the inscription are not what one would expect in a Volscian city, since in the second and first centuries BC, Volscian cities are uniquely characterised by the triple aedileship as their supreme, indeed sole, magistracy (see R. Antonini, on Atina 1, n. 23); and it is easiest to hold that this was their original institutional structure: although aedilis is a loan-word from Latin, it is likely to represent a Volscian term, since triple magistracies are alien to Roman institutional structures. Carloni, p. xvi (in the context of a discussion of representations of magistrates): 'Sappiamo da Festo che questo magistrato appresso gli Osci veniva intitolato Meddìx. Fortunatamente quasi nel tempo stesso in cui si rinvennero le figuline che illustriamo, si trovò una lamina di bronzo 340 - IMAGINES ITALICA - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="355">scolpita con vetusti caratteri latini, ma in lingua, che dee dirsi Volsca, per esservi nominata la città di Velletri, ed il suo antichissimo magistrato, detto ancor esso Medix pp. xix-xx: '.. Gio: Paolo Borgia ... informato del ritrovamento di queste figuline avvenuto in uno scavo, che nell'anno 1784 facevasi in Velletri, per dilatare un muro contiguo alla Chiesa di santa Maria della Neve, detta delle Stimate [s/c: the church lies between Via della Neve and Via delle Stimmate, WSW of the Palazzo Comunale, which is at the highest point of the city, see T. Ceccarini et al., Museo Civico di Velletri (Rome 1989) p. 58, n. 2, 'Dedicata alle Stimmate dall'anno 1602, con la concessione alla omonima confraternità'; for the excavations of 1910, see ibid., pp. 57-67; F.R. Fortunati, Prospettiva 47 (1986) pp. 3-11, 'Ipotesi ricostruttive della decorazione del tempio di Velletri'; M. Angle &amp; A. Germano, Museo e territorio (Velletri 2003) pp. 96-7], e che dalle traccie, che vi si osservarono di una vasca, e di rotti muri con intonaco rosso, diede qualche barlume di vestigia di un'antica officina, non permise che perissero, ma con assidua attenzione raccoltine i moltissimi pezzi, e riuniti i combinabili, ne formò quindici quadri, nei quali alcune delle rappresentanze sono ripetute, poiché è noto che si moltiplicavano i bassirilievi col mezzo delle forme, o matrici, e gli collocò quindi nell'istruttivo della nobil sua casa in Velletri ...' L.A. Lanzi, Biblioteca Apostolica Vaticana, MS Borg.lat. 794, ff. 65-81 'Aloysii Lanzii de Lingua Volscorum deque aerea Lamina volsce scripta quae in Museo Borgiano Velitrio est Commentarius sive Curae Secundae' (1790), at f. 77, recto: there is no record 'de loco, num intra vel extra veliternam urbem lamina effossa fuerit ...' Grossi: 'Questo monumento ... si rinvenne nello scavo fatto de' fondamenti di un oratorio nella chiesa di S. Maria della Neve di Velletri', followed by Mommsen and Morandi; but this claim a generation later cannot outweigh that of Lanzi. E.J. Haeberlin, A es Grave (Frankfurt 1910) pp. 64, 80, 143, seems to be the first to claim that the inscription and two Roman currency bars, Eagle/Pegasus ROMANOM (RRC, no. 4) and Sword/Scabbard (RRC, no. 8) (both 280-242 BC), were found together; this claim is accepted by M.H. Crawford, Ath 69 (1981) pp. 542-3, and by Rix (1992) p. 37, but the original find reports do not support this claim. Epigraphical account Latin alphabet, left to right, c reversed in façia, probably representing a palatalised (or 'soft') c, letters 0.005-0.009, double interpuncts; the first triple interpunct after statom marks the end of a sentence, but there is no rationale for the second after toticu, despite the attempt at explanation by Vine. We can see no trace of the single interpuncts claimed by Conway at the ends of lines 2-4. Antonini's excellent enlarged photographs show that a draft of the text was scratched on the surface before it was inscribed: lestr is visible in the vacat at the end of line 1, and the fact that the draft took up too much space no doubt explains the compression of the final version; and medis was drafted in line 4. Text and apparatus deue: declune: statomi sepis: atahus: pis: uelestrom vacal façia: esaristrom: se: bim: asif: uesclis: uinu: arpatitu sepis: toticu- couehriu: sepu: ferom: pihom: estu vac ec: se: cosuties: ma: ca: tafanies: medix: sistiatiens v IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME 1-341</page><page sequence="356">Translation Decided for the goddess Decluna. If anyone shall have taken who may make a ???, it should be a (case of) piaculum; he is to provide an ox, (i.e.) roasted portions, with vessels and wine. If anyone (shall have taken) with the approval of the public assembly, the removal (?) is to be not irreligious. Date Before 400 BC (on grounds of absence of syncope) (Text A) Last known location / Autopsy Naples, Museo Archeologico Nazionale, inv. 266166 / 14/04/03, 06/12/06 (MHC), 29/05/08 (JPTC, MHC, UR) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object From the sanctuary of Dea Marica, via a private collection; contra Cristofani, there is no certainty that the alphabets are the same, or that either inscription is local. For the sanctuary, see M. Andreani, in L. Quilici &amp; S. Quilici Gigli (edd.), Santuari e luoghi di culto nell'Italia antica (Rome 2003) pp. 177 -207, 'Sul santuario di Marica alla foce del Garigliano'. Epigraphical account Text A, Greek alphabet, Euboic/Ionic script (?), left to right, letters 0.009-0.013, upside-down upsilon, four-barred sigma; Text B, Latin alphabet, s normally = four-barred sigma (but see also below). The letters of Text A, apart from the last, are crisply and confidently engraved, of Text B shallow and messy, also smaller. It is in many cases very difficult to decide whether a scratch is deliberate or not, and the beginning and the end are extremely uncertain. We cannot here provide comprehensive photographic documentation and trust readers to use simultaneously the extensive published material. pari med esom kom : it is possible to see why Cristofani read pari, though the p is vestigial. The letter after pari may be read as m, though the last hasta has disappeared in a flaw in the surface; it requires the eye of faith then to read ed rather than h. Of esom, the e is vestigial; the IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I - 359</page><page sequence="374">next letter, rather than the squiggles of either Cristofani or Maras, seems to us to be an imperfect, but Latin, s. duo[-?-]nei: nei ought to be the end of Text B, placed above the main sequence because of lack of space. There are traces of letters at the edges of the break, which is presumably therefore in its present form later than the inscription (de Simone offers no reason for supposing that the break was deliberate); a possible reading might be duo+[-t-]m nei, with a tiny and perhaps illusory trace visible between m and nei. We are inclined, however, to follow the widespread intuition that the text begins with nei pari med ..., since logic requires that esom ... form an explanation of this injunction. We remain astonished that only Morandi has ever offered an epigraphic justification; our epigraphic justification, independently formulated and we hope more comprehensive than that of Morandi, would be as follows: the author started to the right of a flaw that later became the break now present; (s)he realised that (s)he had started too high up, so continued the text, beginning immediately below the flaw, then running into it at the end. (We cannot think of any epigraphic justification for nei pari med as the last element of the text.) Vine is right to object to Cristofani and Mancini that trifoia = Latin Triuia is implausible; as for the letter that follows, its identification is a matter of judgment (see below). Vine's proposal, however, to read the sixth letter as a 'narrow S' is implausible, despite its endorsement by de Simone (p. 73, reading trifosad - 'from Trifossa'), Clackson &amp; Horrocks (reading trifos = tribus, then audeom duo[m], of the two Audii), and Morandi (2009) (reading trufosad : we do not believe the u), given the fact that the author of the text was capable of three normal four-barred sigmas. We follow JPTC's latest suggestion, that the sign at issue is not an s, but an interpunct; we reject audeom for the reasons we give below (note also that there is no reason to link the inscriptions inside and outside the bowl) and because we see, with others, duo+[']m. (We are puzzled that Maras should read certain signs, and then reject the readings because they do not make sense; the duoum of Morandi (2009) is too short.) Text and apparatus Text A ahuidies Text B nei pari med, esom kom meois sokiois trifo(s) | ad deom duoņ[o]m A The last letter seems to us to be a four-barred sigma, with one or two accidental strokes (contra Maras) B The last letter of sokiois seems to be a four-barred sigma, with an accidental stroke at the bottom; all three hastae of the d of ad are identical in depth, and there are no epigraphic grounds for reading i or u rather than d (see above: we doubt both the view of Mancini (1999) that the traces are accidental and the suggestion of Maras that the letter is a Chalcidian lambda); of the last letter before the lacuna we see, on balance, an upright hasta rather than part of an s; for the rest of the text, see above 360 - IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="375">Translation A Of Afidius B Do not take me, I am with my three companions; at (the shrine) of the good gods Bibliography M. IMAGINES ITALICHE - VOLUME I - 379</page><page sequence="393">Campania / CAMPANIA Coinage 1 Campania References M.H. Crawford, in M. Taliercio (ed.), Presenza e circolazione della moneta in area vesuviana (Naples 2008) pp. 391-4, 'Coinage with akruiiurrí Object Struck bronze coinage Date 265-240 BC Epigraphical account Osean alphabet, right to left; the average weight is 5.7125 gr. The issue is in any case Campanian, struck either for an Oscan-using group at Neapolis or at the community of the *akruiiús (the absence of the diacritical on -urn is presumably a die-engraver's error), using a second-hand obverse die acquired from Neapolis; the coins show that this die must already have been very worn. The context will be the large-scale bronze issues of Neapolis of the First Punic War period, struck in the interest of Rome. The identity of the *Acrui remains uncertain. Date 265-240 BC Epigraphical account Etruscan alphabet, left to right, a reversed. The existence of two varieties supports the view that this is a substantive issue, not a group of pieces with blundered legends; but the objections to attributing it to Venafrum, stated by Lindsay to MacDonald, are insuperable: the e of Venafrum is short; and the same letter cannot represent v and f. The types occur also at Cales, Suessa, Teanum Sidicinum and Aquinum; and the fabric of this issue is similar to that of those issues; the legend is straight at Cales, Suessa and Teanum Sidicinum, curved at Aquinum. There is an isolated occurrence of Etruscan v on Teanum Sidicinum 11. Text and apparatus (1) Head of Athena 1. / Cock r.; before, downwards, veinav (a) Glasgow, Hunterian Museum, C. Maria Capua Vetere, probably fondo Patturelli References ST Cp 21 Object Top half of terracotta stele, 0.547 high x 0.191 wide at top, 0.164 wide at bottom x 0.034 thick, damaged at the top on the back, the clay very friable in breaks; a facing head of Medusa above a boar running left, both in relief, at the top. Date Stutzinger &amp; Rix argue that the 'Stilisierung' of the head of Medusa and the boar was copied from Roman coins, namely a currency bar of 275-242 BC (RRC, no. 9) and the Minerva facing/Bull as (RRC, no. 37). The currency bar, however, has a sow, not a boar; and the Minerva facing/Bull as is of about 225 BC and much too late to have served as a model. The boar in fact more closely resembles that on the quadrans of the Apollo/ Apollo series of aes graue (RRC, no. 18/4) of 275-250 BC. But the use of coins as models is neither demonstrated nor plausible, and it is more likely that both coins and stelai drew on monumental models Last known location / Autopsy Frankfurt am Main, Archäologisches Museum, inv. 93, 5 / 05/1 1/03 (MHC) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Found in February 1889 (Buecheler (1889)), then in the A. Bourguignon verbürgten Aussage nicht im Fondo Patturelli bei dem grossen Heiligtum, sondern im Fondo Tirone in der Richtung nach dem Tifate zu; denn nur dort graben die Leute von denen Hr. B. diese Stele und andere Sachen, z.B. eine Heraklesfigur aus Terracotta, gekauft hat.' The piece does not appear in Collection d'Antiquités [of A. Bourguignon] ... Date See the Introduction, p. 27 Last known location / Autopsy Paris, Bibliothèque Nationale, Cabinet des Médailles, Collection Froehner X 390 / 08/1 1/02 (MHC) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Sosso: TSTel dicembre dell'anno scorso 1882, un contadino mi mostrò una terracotta molto ben conservata, assicurandomi d'averla rinvenuta a poca distanza dal luogo detto Tirone ... ed in una specie di stanzetta sotterranea, che le continue pioggie avevano scoperta ...'; the stele was briefly in the A. Bourguignon collection at Naples, then in the collection of W. Froehner (whose catalogue attributes the piece to le Curti), passing after his death in 1925 to the Cabinet des Médailles. A drawing of the inscription was passed by Bourguignon to von Dühn, who 408 - IMAGINES ITALICHE - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="422">passed it to Bücheler; the information that came with the drawing was that the piece probably came from the temple in the fondo Patturelli. Epigraphical account Osean alphabet, right to left, letters 0.02-0.03 (line 1), 0.013-0.02 (lines 2-5), impressed before firing. There are two epigraphic fields, separated by a line also made before firing. The upper field bears a text of five lines; in the lower field, traces of only one letter in each of four lines survive and none of these letters reaches the maximum height of those in lines 2-5 of the upper field. It cannot be excluded that there were two lines above the top surviving line; but this is perhaps unlikely since the four surviving lines are carefully aligned with lines 2-5 of the upper field. It is plausible to suppose that the second text runs on from the first; but it is mysterious why the whole text was divided in this way. Von Planta guessed that this inscription came like the four with 'Viriium Vesuliais deivinais' from the Fondo Tirone, because of its being brought to Naples with one of the four and being like them of the Vir(r)ii; but if this inscription had come from the Fondo Tirone, it is likely that Bourguignon would 434 - IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="448">have been informed, whereas all he could discover was that the seller was believed to live somewhere near S. This perhaps makes Rix's supplement mame(rekkieis) less likely than the traditional mame(rtei ): even if the inscription was never intended to continue, we know too little to say that a divine name could not have been abbreviated. IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I - 447</page><page sequence="461">Text and apparatus vac vinuxs veneliis peracis estam tetet venilei viniciiu[l] vac The crack in the rim may conceal the loss of one letter at the end Translation Venox, son of Venel, Peracis (Percius) gave this to Venil (Venel), son of Venox -iis is an adjectival suffix, indicating filiation, in the nominative, -iiu (or -iiui) is the same suffix, in the dative; it is generally supposed that the gift is from father to son, whose nomen is therefore not given, and who also has the praenomen of the grandfather. This form of a filiation occurs in Umbrian and Faliscan, and may have been more widespread originally, for instance in Latin; this placing of the filiation occurs in Umbrian and the Tabula Veliterna (ST VM 8 is a forgery), with no other certain example in the south, estam occurs in Umbrian and South Picene; but tetet, venilei and viniciiu(i ) are not Umbrian. The language is therefore not Umbrian; it may be a language from the north, but it does not fit in with any other language we know in detail, although it is closer to South Picene than anything else (JPTC). Given all this, it is significant that the stamnos comes from Vulci (compare the amphora of Vulci with Etruscan inscription, of c. 600 BC made at Vulci, found in the necropolis of Calatia: M. Cristofani, in L'incidenza dell'antico I (Naples 1995) pp. 343-54, 'Per la storia etno- linguistica della Campania arcaica: i ceti litterati', at pp. 345-6); that the name racus on ET Cm 2.76, or a variant thereof, is attested at Caere and Vulci; and that Percius recalls the Etruscan gentilicium Perkna. Although the burial is a normal elite burial for Capua of the period, it would be extremely unwise to suppose that the language of this inscription is the language of Capua, rather than that of an individual immigrant who spoke an Italic language and had Etruscan connections. These considerations were not available to G. Meiser, Giotta 65 (1987) pp. 104-25, 'Pälignisch, Latein und Südpikenisch', at pp. 108, n. 29, 1 14, n. 55 Bibliography G. Text and apparatus vibi[s:] smintiis: vibis: smintiis: sum The text is reported in 7 fragments in Atti (though not in the order resulting from restoration and given here): vi, bi, smintiis, v, ibis, smintiis-, s, and n; the last two presumably form part of sum; the fragments are distinguishable in SIO, pl. VII, 3. The restoration cannot be wholly certain; but the claim of Mancini that half the text stood on the wall of the tomb opposite the door is in flat contradiction with the report in Atti vibi[..] simintiis, Helbig; but the redundant i does not appear in SIO, pl. VII, 3, nor is it printed by Conway or von Planta, and it should be regarded as a misprint (Helbig also prints ST Cp 5 = Capua 37 upside down); the lacuna of one letter reported by von Planta between the second and third words presumably corresponds to an interpunct seen by Helbig, an interpunct whose lower point is just visible in SIO, pl. VII 3; the lacuna indicated by Helbig in the second occurrence of the name, sminti[i]s, was neither reported in Atti nor recorded by Zvetaieff; the lacuna may be an error or the letter may have been made good in the restoration of the piece; there is no reason with von Planta to hypothesise anything missing at the end, that had disappeared by the time the drawing in SIO, pl. VII 3 was made Translation Vibius Smintius; I am Vibius Smintius Bibliography Atti della Commissione Conservatrice ... 1872, p. 9; W.
