Maria Capua Vetere, le Curti, south of the fondo Patturelli References ST Cp 41; Ye 102 Object A terracotta model, perhaps of a part of the human body, hollow at the back, 0.09 long X 0.032 (at the narrower end) - 0.056 (at the broader end) wide x 0.05 thick; the sides slope outwards, so that the back of the object is wider than the inscribed top face. Almost complete: a flake of material broken off at the narrower end, not affecting the inscription, is modern damage. Both the nature and the purpose of the object are the subject of debate, neither clarified by a difficult to interpret inscription and lack of concrete evidence as to find spot. Three grooves on the sides perhaps allowed the object to be fixed between three nails to a flat surface, with the result that the object forms half of whatever it is. It has been taken to be a phallus or a finger, either of which would be consistent with an ex voto context, although it does not much resemble any example of either known to us, even if one assumes that only the inscribed top face and the two sides were intended to be seen. The grooves were made before firing; on autopsy, Lucy Morgan wondered whether they might be intended to represent lines, segmenting the finger into its three limbs, or marks on the phallus: the groove at the narrower end might mark off the head of the phallus or the fingernail: for presumably votive fingers, compare W. Geli, Pompeiana II (London 1832) p. 196: (the Capitolium) 'contained ... (m)any fingers of bronze1. The description of the object as a plate by A.L. Prosdocimi, in La Campania fra il VI e il III secolo (Galatina 1992) pp. 1 19-48, 'Note su 'italico' e 'sannita', at p. 133, is surprising. Frei-Stolba's ((1990) p. 47) view of the object as an 'Erkennungsmarke ( tessera ) im weiteren Sinn ... Der Träger wies sich mit dieser tessera als Mitglied der gens der Perdi aus' rests on 464 - IMAGINES ITALICHE - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="478">Eichner's translation (see below) and assumes the object to be a finger. We confess to some bafflement as to how the object could actually have been used as a 'token for identification'. Date c. 275 BC (Eichner &amp; Frei-Stolba, pp. 77-8) Last known location / Autopsy Chur, Rätisches Landesmuseum, inv. K 331* / 06/05/02 (Lucy Morgan) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Found by workers of Orazio Pascale before 1892, the year of his death, in Capua, le Curti, whether in a sanctuary or in a tomb is uncertain (Frei-Stolba (1990) pp. 47-50); although Riccio is not a model of reliability, he perhaps suggests a sanctuary, discovered before 1855: 'Nè questa è la sola fabbrica grandiosa che offrì Capua antica in terre cotte. Il sig. D. Orazio di Pasquale, mio amico, nel tempo della mia permanenza in S. Maria, ne trovò altre due, una di bovi campani in terra cotta poco lungi da S. Erasmo [in the centre of the city], ed un'altra di Veneri in basso rilievo a stampa, piccole, e di niun conto, ma pure in numero esorbitante, nei proprii terreni alle Curti, contigui immediatamente al tempio cennato de' Pattorelli' (G. Riccio, Notizie degli scavamenti del suolo dell'antica Capua e dei suoi monumenti (Naples 1855) p. 12 = Polior ama Pittoresco 15 (1853-4) pp. 330-1, 346-8, 368, 371-3, 385-6, 393-4, 403-5; 16 (1855-6) pp. 15-16, 19-20, 36-7, 43-4, 117-18, 127-8, at 15, p. 372). The object passed into the Robert von Planta collection and thence to the Rätisches Landesmuseum, Chur. Epigraphical account Osean alphabet, right to left, letters 0.005-0.008, roughly incised before firing on the outer, rounded face of the object. The first four letters of the first line are damaged, but it is uncertain whether the first line has been damaged since the object was seen by von Planta or whether those letters were not already difficult to read before any modern damage. The damage appears to be a surface fracture which begins before, and is almost as long as, the first line of text and which impinges on all but the last two letters. Within the area in question, which is a deeper orange in colour, a darker patch above but not touching the first four letters, looks like a burn mark. There are at least three finger prints fired onto the object, one at the narrower end, one below the groove at the broader end; and most but not all of the black mark just described coincides with a finger or thumb print. On balance, we would argue that the text of the first line was probably imperfect before firing, in that the text was squashed up a little by the pressure of a finger or thumb on the wet clay. Text and apparatus pęrkium- púiieh súm Von Planta did not indicate that any letters were uncertain and it may be (see above) that the first four letters of line 1 were undamaged, when he saw them; we print the text as it is now, underdotting the letters in question Translation (Property) of the Percii, to whom I belong Eichner &amp; Frei-Stolba (1989) pp. 73-6, following Lejeune, argue against a translation that is an inverted question and answer: 'Of the Percii. Il foro', at pp. 45-8) to discover any steps to the west is no argument against this view, since any trace of such steps - whether outside or within the original western end of the podium - may have been removed either when the temple was shortened in the first century AD or - without ever being noticed - in early archaeological exploration / E.T. Sgobbo claimed that a statue base should have a crown moulding and argued that the holes were the result of accidental damage in the course of re-use; but both plain block bases and stepped bases continued into the Hellenistic period in the Greek world (information from Jim Coulton). The proportions here would be very suitable for the top block of a stepped base, and would not be surprising at Cumae. The front of the block has margins of c. 0.035 on either side of the epigraphic field, and is damaged at the top and, in particular, the top right-hand corner; a clamp-hole at the left- hand end of the epigraphic field derives from re-use of the block. A small fragment not numbered in 1893 was identified in 1897 as forming part of lines 33-6 and is indicated in our text; three further small fragments, on two of which the only identifiable letter is s (von Planta) or s and pf( Conway), cannot be assigned. We accept the traditional relationship between the lines of Fragment A and Fragment D, but think it speculative to join the last line of Fragment D (= line 13) and the first line of Fragment B (= line 15) on the assumption that they together bore the same name as in line 35; in any case, as von Planta observed, Fragment A has a moderate margin, Fragment B a large margin, Fragment C no margin, which suggests a significant gap in each case. We can also see no rational basis for associating Fragment B and Fragment E. On the other hand, we have supplemented on the hypothesis that lines 33-8 in part repeat lines 3-9, while emphasing that the hypothesis is just that. Epigraphical account Latin alphabet, left to right, letters 0.006-0.01, a = A,/ = l', / = L Line 1 is indented, lines 2-6 aligned, while the beginnings of lines 7-8 follow the curve of the tablet. Mancini suggests that lines 6-8 are Osean written by a user of Latin. The first group of names has Osean endings (Harines is perhaps Etruscan in origin, as Mancini suggests), the second group Latin, and it is not to be excluded that this identifies the language they spoke. The author has forgotten -m at the end of olu, solu, olu; and -s at the end of fancua, recta, flatu, sicu are almost Latin. Nella sommità della parete di fronte (n. 4) vedesi dipinta, in grandi proporzioni, una di quelle palmette ovvie ne' vasi; ed esternamente (n. 1) tre zone contornano gli stipiti e l'archetto della porta, anch' essa 5 1 8 - IMAGINES ITALICA - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="532">di pietra e di un sol pezzo, diligentemente lavorata nel di dentro, rozza e grossolana nella faccia esteriore. References Panella &amp; Morizio Date c. 100 BC (Panella &amp; Morizio) Epigraphical Account Apart from the Latin stamp on Dressel IB amphorae, the wreck included the stamps -CI3, 8, and TI on Dressel 1 A amphorae; the last of the three had a pronouncedly overhanging horizontal hasta. The first stamp is clearly Greek, the form of the last is in our view more characteristic of the Greek than of the Osean alphabet; a kiln in the territory of Cumae would be capable of the mix of alphabets. IMAGINES ITALICA - VOLUME I - 557</page><page sequence="571">Campania / TEANVM SIDICINVM 24 Teano, loc. Bagno nuovo References ST Po 51; Ve 70 Object Section of wall plaster, reconstructed from numerous fragments, 0.255 high x 1.02 wide; the beginning of a slope back at the top suggests that the piece formed the inscribed fascia of a funerary naiskos: it is less appropriate to the edge of a funerary bed or the wall of a chamber-tomb (the possibilities are discussed by Antonini (2007) p. 82, nn. 313-14). The only other case known to us of square interpuncts and diacriticals is Teruentum 21, a strip of gilded bronze, and we wonder whether the surface in which the letters are incised was also originally painted to resemble metal. We are certain that the single piece of 'marmo quadrato' reported by Broccoli is the smaller of the two in the Inventario of 1796; we also note that the dimension given by Broccoli, 'meno di [2.64 m]', and the widths given by Documenti inediti, 10/12 and 1 1/12 palmi = 0.22 and 0.242 m, are uncannily close to the height of this piece, where 0.255 is a maximum, and we think it more likely that part(s) of this piece were mis-described to Broccoli and in the Inventario as 'marmo', presumably because the plaster still adhered to pieces of limestone, than that there is another Osean inscription from Teano waiting to be discovered in the Museo Archeologico Nazionale. Date c. 200 BC (?) Last known location / Autopsy Naples, Museo Archeologico Nazionale, inv. 2549 / 15/04/03 (WMB, MW) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object The documents cited by Antonini are decisive for the origin of the piece, Broccoli: 'Tutte le conseguenze poi coincidono a farci credere, che detto Bagno abbi esistito appunto sul ramo antico della strada latina, che menava da Teano a Benevento ... In un contorno di stucco [in 1793], residuo di parte maggiore e grandiosa, apparvero caratteri osci ben chiari, che monchi e spezzati niun senso presentavano al lettore. Date 265-240 BC Epigraphical account Osean alphabet, right to left, legend wrong in HNltaly, I is sometimes reversed on the obverse or the reverse, the final m is sometimes omitted on the obverse, where the form of the p is also variable. Garrucci, pl. LXXXVIII, 15, bears the legend kaiattnim on the reverse (reported in his text and by von Planta (1897) 226, 2, as kaiatiním ); this is a mis-reading of the legend kupelternum. But it is the obverse of this coin that is interesting: the specimen illustrated by Garrucci shows an obverse die of Nola, Head of Apollo 1. NQA[AI], used for an issue of Cubulteria. Rutter remarks {HNltaly (2001) p. 58) that the letters '1, characteristic of issues of Neapolis of 270 -255 BC {HNltaly, nos. 586, 589), appear also on issues of Aesernia (Sambon 180 = HNltaly, no. 431), Cales (not documented, but in fact Sambon 951-3 = HNltaly, no. 436), and Suessa Aurunca {HNltaly, no. 450), perhaps suggesting joint production. IZ is not documented at Nola. Text and apparatus (1) Head of Apollo 1., kupelternum / Man- faced bull r. (2) Head of Apollo 1. / Man-faced bull r., kupelternum The alphabets used for die-marks include the Greek letters O and X, the Greek or Osean letters TT or p, the Osean letters k (Sambon 1070; SN G Aarhus 54) and ú (Sambon 1068) Translation Of the Cubulter(i)ni 580 - IMAGINES ITALICHE - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="594">Campania / ALLIFAE 1 Coinage Alife References HNltaly (2001) nos. 459-64 Object Silver didrachms, obols and hemiobols. Date c. 400-395 BC (didrachms), c. 325-275 BC (obols and hemiobols) Epigraphical account Greek alphabet with 8, except for (6), with Osean alphabet, a reversed x 2, and perhaps (4) (see below); we have no idea what the engraver for (2) was trying to achieve; the 'whiskers' on either side of the O of (1) are die-breaks, so that the letter is not O on its side: we think that the engraver was using omicron + aspirate to represent / (see the Introduction, p. 21): the earliest legend is thus not aliflia, contra M. Janda, in H. Rix (ed.), Oskisch-Umbrisch. Texte und Grammatik (Wiesbaden 1993) pp. 147-54, 'Die Inschrift des Helms von Canosa und der Buchstabe ß des Südpikenischen', at p. 153 (with two mis-printed legends), or alfàha, contra J. Stuart-Smith, Phonetics and Philology: Sound-change in Italic (Oxford 2004) pp. 86-7. The legend aXXup hanon does not exist. Most of the variant legends on the obols are attributable to carelessness or incompetence, with that on (3, i) being a particularly unhappy attempt to write all(i)/[a)non, but with various other disasters also; and the engravers shift randomly between A and A. We doubt whether any engraver cut B for / deliberately, rather than simply cutting it as easier than 8 (contra R. Antonini, Quaderni dell'Istituto di Linguistica dell'Università di Urbino 6 (1989) pp. 33-91, 'Materiali per un'indagine sull'alfabeto osco', at pp. 51, 53; Stuart-Smith, pp. 137-9): 8 must have been excruciatingly difficult to cut for such tiny coins; and the fact the engraver sometimes cut B and sometimes cut 8 suggests that in both cases he was trying to cut 8. We also think that 00 is a different failed attempt to cut 8. On the other hand, the legend on (4) is perhaps intended to be Osean, contra Stuart-Smith, p. 86, n. 100. The 'Piccola moneta di argento di Alife con testa di bue barbato e chiomato e dal rovescio una tigre sotto una serpe' (Ruggiero, Scavi , p. 426) is presumably a specimen of Phistelia 1, 7 and a specimen of Phistelia 1, 10, stuck together. Many of the known obols come from authorised, but very inadequately documented, excavations at Piedimonte d' Alife, for which the account in Sambon must be corrected by H. Dressel, in Historische und philologische Aufsätze, Ernst Curtius ... gewidmet (Berlin 1884) pp. 247-58, 'Numismatische Beiträge aus dem Grabfelde bei Piedimonte d'Alife'; Annali dell'Instituto 1884, pp. 219-68, 'La necropoli presso Alife'. Date Last known location / Autopsy Piedimonte Matese, Museo Civico, inv. 500 / 04/09/06 (MHC, UR) Discovery, archaeological context, later history of object Reportedly found on Monte Cila to the north-west of the town; it is not mentioned by A. Epigraphical account Osean alphabet, right to left, roughly scratched, letters 0.09-0.13, 0.04 between lines 1 and 2, 0.02 between lines 2 and 3; despite the alleged provenance, it must be regarded as highly uncertain whether the inscription is in fact ancient. Apart from the oddity both of the text - an attempt at part of the legend of the coinage of Phistelia - and of the manner of its incision, the text we have looks as if it was scratched onto an already broken piece of stone: in particular, 584 - IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="598">the bottom half of the u in the top line displays a right-hand hasta that becomes shallow before the edge of the stone, rather than a break in a hasta of full depth; and the four hastae (not five, since the middle hasta is accidental damage) in the bottom line look like random scratches to fill the space, with one of them indeed being in the broken area. Campana, Panorama Numismatico 13 (1996) pp. 337-52, 'Samnium: Fistelia'; HNItaly (2001) nos. 61 1-19 Object Didrachms, obols, half-obols, quarter-obols Date c. 405-400 BC (didrachms), 325-275 BC (obols, half-obols, quarter-obols) Epigraphical account The ethnic appears sometimes in Osean alphabet and language, sometimes in Greek alphabet and language (see below); (4) is clearly intentionally bilingual. All readings involving diacriticals are in our view mis-readings: note that upsiis is consistently without diacritical; A sometimes appears as A; J and V are often very similar, whence the mis-reading of fistluis as fistulis. We think that fistlus is a nominative plural, like Arpi in Latin, and that fistluis, fisteluis is an ablative plural; we regard fistlui as incomplete, fistlun, 'fls]t&lt;l&gt;uin and fitul as blundered { fistulun in J. Stuart-Smith, Phonetics and Philology: Sound-change in Italic (Oxford 2004) p. 137, is presumably a mis-print). Garrucci, pl. (5) is by far the commonest variety of obol with legend. A specimen of (1) was in the Castellino del Biferno hoard (Garrucci, p. 94). Epigraphical account Etruscan alphabet (contra Mancini), right to left, n and m reversed, space after vipieis and veliieis, but not after culchna, Text A round the body of the kylix, Texts B and C both on the base; it is not stated by Lepsius whether Text A or Texts B and C are inside or outside the vase, but the 'in fundo lancis figlinae' of Guarini makes the latter very likely. Text and apparatus Text A vipieis veliieis culchna sim Text B a Text C vue A The third letter is manifestly p, not f contra Sgobbo and Poccetti: the stroke which goes from the top of this letter through the following letter is manifestly accidental; it is hence also not, contra Mancini, a diacritical; veliteis (von Planta, Conway) would have to be a mistake for IMAGINES ITALIC® - VOLUME I - 593</page><page sequence="607">velit&lt;i&gt;eis in order to equate to 'of Velidius', we cannot read the letter before -eis as /, so reject venleis (Vetter, Poccetti), and read veliieis with ST, taking the stroke through the second / as accidental, as also earlier in the text C vuv, if one horizontal hasta is an accident Translation Text A I am the kylix of Vibius Velius Texts B and C are perhaps equivalents in the Etruscan alphabet of the Greek trade marks discussed by A.W. Epigraphical account Etruscan or Osean alphabet, right to left, letters 0.015-0.025, scratched after firing round the motif at centre of inside, looking inwards. On the fifth letter, Hoenigswald correctly notes: ' ... what seems an oblique stroke crossing the ... letter turns out, on close examination, to be a slip of the writer while executing the topmost hasta of the preceding e .' There are quite a number of similar slips. If the alphabet is the Osean alphabet (note the shape of the e), then the text is mageis sim; if the alphabet is Etruscan, then the text is maceis sim. Ganschow, Untersuchungen zur Baugeschichte in Herculaneum (Antiquitas Reihe 3, 30, Bonn 1989) pp. 271-82) 608 - IMAGINES ITALICS - VOLUME I</page><page sequence="622">Last known location / Autopsy Plastered dado, on the right-hand side of the fauces of the Casa Sannitica, Insula V, 1, c. 1 m from the door, c. 1.65-1.60 m from the floor, noticed by M. della Corte on 24/09/1932 / 19/09/04 (MHC) Epigraphical account Text A, Osean alphabet, right to left, letters c. 0.008, created by pressing a blunt point into the wet plaster and black paint of the surround of the dado, Text B, Osean alphabet, right to left, letters c. 0.008, created by scratching a double line on the white surface of the dado. Antonini has identified five copies, of which the bottom two - we presume - are to be regarded as forming della Corte's nos. 3 19-20 = ST Cm 43-4 = Pocc 125, a-b. Some of the letters of the secondary copies require the eye of faith to read; we do not here reproduce these secondary texts; unlike Antonini, we end both primary texts with úvies, regarding the traces that follow as secondary (the last letter in A, the last-but-one letter in B, of úvies are too faint to appear in our photographs). If we are right that the texts are craftsmen's signatures, they may have initially been made invisible by a final coat of paint. Text and apparatus Text A s(e)p(is)- lavs- l(úvkis)&lt;-&gt; úvies Text B s(e)p(is)&lt;-&gt; lavs- l(úvkis)&lt;-&gt; úvies See Translation A MHC could not see any trace of a diagonal hasta in the second letter, only a faint crack, which indeed continues beyond the bottom right-hand corner of the letter; contra della Corte, the first I is normal, there is no central horizontal hasta in the first v, there is no diacritical to the left of the /; we are not convinced by Antonini that av are in ligature, though nothing turns on it B The left-hand end of the line begins to slope downwards, with the left-hand hasta of the ú and the top hasta of the v, recovering with the z'; MHC could not see any trace of a diagonal hasta in the second letter; we are not convinced by Antonini that av are in ligature, though nothing turns on the question; contra della Corte, there is no diacritical to the left of the i Translation S(e)p(pius), Lauius (compare Schulze, p. 179), L(ucius), sons of Ouius, (made this) (?) The problems with reading (Sironen, Antonini) 's&lt;a&gt;lavs- l(úvkie) úvie' are: (1) that s al aus elsewhere in our material occurs only in a funerary context; (2) that the second letter in both texts is a p of remarkable legibility (the apparent diagonal hasta in Text A is in our view accidental damage); and (3) that a greeting to the owner of the house in such a position would be surprising IMAGINES ITALICHE - VOLUME I - 609</page><page sequence="623">Bibliography M. della Corte, Rend.Acc.Arch.Napoli, n.s., 33 (1958) pp. 239-308, 'Le iscrizioni di Ercolano [found between 1929 and 1941]', at p. 266, nos. 317-18, pl.
